Immediate Inference
intermediate
8 min
The Rule: Constituent of Higher is Constituent of Lower, and Its Partial Converse
Author explains the rule and its partial converse with examples from the genus-species hierarchy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a constituent of a higher genus is also a constituent of any lower species, and determine whether a given constituent of a lower species also constitutes the higher.

Core Idea
The constituent of a higher genus necessarily constitutes lower species, but not every lower constituent constitutes the higher.

This lesson unpacks a fundamental rule about the hierarchy of genera and species: what makes a higher genus what it is (its differentia) also makes every lower species under it. But the reverse is only sometimes true. Understanding this rule sharpens our ability to analyze definitions and avoid errors in classification, which is crucial in disciplines like Usul al-Fiqh and Kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the higher and lower terms
In the rule, 'higher' (al-'ali) means any genus or species above another in the hierarchy, and 'lower' (al-safil) means any genus or species under another. For example, in the sequence 'substance → body → living body → animal → human', animal is higher than human, and human is lower than animal.
The terms are relative: an intermediate genus is both higher (relative to what is below) and lower (relative to what is above).
2
Understand what 'constituent' means
A 'constituent' (muqawwim) here is a differentia that removes ambiguity from the genus and completes its essence. For instance, 'sensitive' (al-hassas) is a constituent of animal: it removes the ambiguity of 'living body' by specifying that it has sensory perception.
The term 'constituent' is equivalent to the differentia that makes the genus determinate.
3
Apply the rule to a concrete hierarchy
The rule says: if X is a constituent of a higher genus (e.g., 'sensitive' is a constituent of animal), then X is also a constituent of any lower species under that genus (e.g., human). This is because the lower species includes the higher genus as a part of its essence.
Test with the example: 'sensitive' is part of animal, and animal is part of human, so 'sensitive' is part of human.
4
Check the converse: does every constituent of the lower also constitute the higher?
The converse is not universally true. 'Rational' (al-natiq) is a constituent of human (the lower) but does not constitute animal (the higher), because animal also includes non-rational species. However, some constituents of the lower, like 'sensitive', do constitute the higher. So the converse holds only as a particular affirmative: some constituents of the lower are also constituents of the higher.
The author emphasizes that 'not the converse' negates the universal claim, not the particular.

قوله والمقوم للعالي: اللام للاستغراق، أي كل فصل مقوم للعالي فهو فصل مقوم للسافل؛ لأن مقوم العالي جزء معه مقوماً: تقويم الفصل للجنس عبارة عن رفع الإيهام الواقع فيه، وهو المراد من كون الفصل علة للجنس، والمراد من عدم تحصيل الجنس كونه مبهماً؛ فإن الصورة الجنسية إذا حصلت عند العقل تردد في أنها أي شيء هل هو إنسان أو فرس أو بقر إلى غير ذلك، ثم إذا انضم إليها صورة الفصل يحصل صورة مطابقة لتمام الماهية، ووجهه تردد العقل في الصورة التي يدركها بنفسه لا بالآلات الحسية والخيالية يقف إلى حد وهو الماهية النوعية، فإذا حصلت فيه الصورة المطابقة لها انتهت الصور، فالصورة الجنسية ليست تامة محصلة بل ناقصة غير محصلة تكملها صورة الفصل وليس معنى العلة إلا هذا التكميل ورفع إزالة الإبهام ويختلف مراتب التكميل ورفع الإبهام بحسب اختلاف مراتب الأجناس؛ فإن الجنس العالي فيه إبهام عظيم وإذا انضم فصل يقل إبهامه ثم يتناقص الإبهام ويزداد الكمال بضم فصل فصل إلى النوع الحقيقي مثاله: إذا تصور من جسم موجود أنه لا في موضوع فقد حصل في العقل صورة الجوهر ويقع التردد في أنها هل تطابق المادة والصورة أو العقل أو النفس إلى غير ذلك، فإذا انضم إليها ذو أبعاد ثلاثة حصل صورة الجسم وارتفع ذلك الإبهام العظيم ويبقى التردد في أنها هل تطابق النباتات أو الجمادات أو الحيوانات، ثم إذا اقترن بها فصل النامي ارتفع ذلك الإبهام، وهكذا إلى النوع أيضاً، فقد تبين من هذا البيان أن ماهية الجنس غير محصلة وماهية النوع محصلة، وينبغي أن يعلم أن المراد بأن ماهية النوع محصلة في العقل وماهية الجنس غير محصلة فيه؛ لأن ماهية النوع لا تحتاج في ارتفاع إبهامها إلى انضمام كلي آخر بخلاف ماهية الجنس؛ فإن النوع لا يبقى له تحصل منتظر إلا بالإشارة فقط بخلاف الجنس؛ فإنه لابد له من تحصل زائد حتى يقبل التحصل بالإشارة كما في الحواشي القديمة لجلال العلماء على شرح التجريد. (عبد) بانضمامه كالناطق؛ فإنه يقسم الحيوان إلى قسمين؛ لأنه يحصل بانضمامه إلى الجنس وجودا قسما هو الحيوان الناطق وبانضمامه إليه عدما قسما آخر هو الحيوان الغير الناطق، ولا يخفى أن ارتكاب مثل هذا التكلف غير سديد. فالأولى أن يقال مقسما؛ لأنه محصل قسم للجنس؛ لما فيه من عدم التكلف والاختصار. فافهم. (إسماعيل) ثم قال: ولا عكس. للعالي والعالي جزء للسافل وجزء الجزء جزء، فمقوم العالي جزء للسافل. ثم إنه يميز السافل عن كل ما يميز العالي عنه فيكون جزءاً مميزا له، وهو المعنى بالمقوم. وليعلم أن المراد بالعالي ههنا كل جنس أو نوع يكون فوق آخر سواء كان فوقه آخر أو لم يكن، وكذا المراد بالسافل كل جنس أو نوع يكون تحت آخر سواء كان تحته آخر أو لم يكن، حتى أن الجنس المتوسط عال بالنسبة إلى ما تحته وسافل بالنسبة إلى ما فوقه قوله ولا عكس أي كليا بمعنى أنه ليس كل مقوم للسافل مقوما للعالي؛ فإن الناطق مقوم للسافل الذي هو الإنسان، جزء للسافل كالحساس؛ فإنه مقوم للعالي أي الحيوان ومميز له عن جميع ما عداه فهو مقوم للسافل أيضاً وهو الإنسان؛ لأن الحيوان داخل في حقيقة الإنسان فما يكون داخلا في الحيوان يكون داخلا فيه أيضاً؛ إذ الجزء لشيء يكون جزءاً لذلك الشيء فالحساس داخل في حقيقة الإنسان ومميز له عما يميز الحيوان عنه، وهذا هو المراد بالمقوم. (إسماعيل) وليعلم: يعني ليس المراد بالعالي ههنا ما مر أي ما لا يكون فوقه آخر؛ لأن هذا التخصيص باطل كما يدل عليه الدليل بل المراد ههنا كل جنس أو نوع يكون فوقه آخر سواء كان تحت شيء أو لا، وكذا بالسافل أيضاً ليس ما لا يكون تحته آخر بل المراد ما يكون تحت آخر سواء كان تحته آخر أو لا، فالجنس المتوسط كالجسم النامي مثلا جنس عال باعتبار كونه فوق حيوان وسافل باعتبار كونه تحت الجسم المطلق وهو نوع عال أيضاً باعتبار أنه فوق نوع هو الحيوان وسافل أيضاً باعتبار نوع آخر وهو الجسم المطلق.(إسماعيل) كليا دفع دخل هو: أنه كل مقوم للعالي مقوم للسافل قضية موجبة كلية وعكسها. بعض مقوم للسافل مقوم للعالي موجبة جزئية صادق بالضرورة، فلا معنى لقوله : "لا" عكس فتوضيح الدفع أن قول الشارح كليا إيماء إلى أن قوله لا" "عكس رفع الإيجاب الكلي لا السلب الكلي ورفع الإيجاب الكلي لا ينافيه الإيجاب الجزئي. (إسماعيل وعبد كل مقوم فصلا قريبا أو بعيدا فلا يرد أنه إن أريد بالمقوم الفصل القريب فلا شيء من المقوم القريب للسافل مقوم للعالي، وإن أريد الفصل البعيد فكل مقوم بعيد للسافل مقوم للعالي. (عبد)
The statement 'and the constituent of the higher is a constituent of the lower': the lam is for generalization, i.e., every differentia that is a constituent of the higher is a differentia that is a constituent of the lower, because the constituent of the higher is a part with it as a constituent: the evaluation (taqwim) of the differentia for the genus is the removal of the ambiguity (ibham) occurring in it, and this is what is meant by the differentia being a cause ('illa) for the genus, and what is meant by the genus not being actualized (tahsil) is its being ambiguous; for when the generic form is acquired by the intellect, it deliberates as to what it is—whether it is human, horse, cow, etc.—then when the form of the differentia is joined to it, a form matching the complete essence is obtained, and the intellect ceases deliberation. So the generic form is not complete and actualized but incomplete and unactualized, completed by the form of the differentia. The meaning of 'cause' is nothing but this completion and removal of ambiguity. And the levels of completion and removal of ambiguity differ according to the levels of genera. For the highest genus has great ambiguity; when a differentia is joined, its ambiguity decreases, then the ambiguity decreases further and perfection increases by conjoining differentia after differentia up to the true species. Example: If one conceives of an existent body that is not in a subject, the form of substance (jawhar) is acquired in the intellect, and deliberation occurs as to whether it corresponds to matter and form, or intellect, or soul, etc. Then when 'possessing three dimensions' is joined to it, the form of body (jism) is obtained and that great ambiguity is removed, but deliberation remains as to whether it corresponds to plants, inanimates, or animals. Then when the differentia of growth (nami) is coupled, that ambiguity is removed, and so on up to the species. So from this exposition it is clear that the essence of the genus is not actualized, while the essence of the species is actualized. And one should know that what is meant by 'the essence of the species is actualized in the intellect and the essence of the genus is not actualized in it' is that the species, in removing its ambiguity, does not need the addition of another universal, unlike the genus. For the species has no remaining anticipated actualization except by indication (ishara) only, unlike the genus, for it must have an additional actualization until it accepts actualization by indication, as in the old glosses of Jalal al-'Ulama' on Sharh al-Tajrid. (Abd) By its conjunction like 'rational', for it divides animal into two divisions, because by its conjunction with the genus existence-wise it produces a division which is rational animal, and by its conjunction with it non-existence-wise it produces another division which is non-rational animal. And it is not hidden that undertaking such affectation is not sound. So it is better to call it a divider because it is an actualizer of a division for the genus, for there is no affectation and it is concise. So understand. (Ismail) Then he said: 'And not the converse.' For the higher is a part of the lower, and a part of a part is a part, so the constituent of the higher is a part for the lower. Then it distinguishes the lower from everything that the higher is distinguished from, so it becomes a distinguishing part of it, and that is what is meant by constituent. And know that what is meant by 'higher' here is every genus or species that is above another, whether there is another above it or not, and similarly what is meant by 'lower' is every genus or species that is under another, whether there is another under it or not, so that the intermediate genus is higher relative to what is under it and lower relative to what is above it. His statement 'and not the converse' means universally, i.e., it is not the case that every constituent of the lower is a constituent of the higher. For 'rational' is a constituent of the lower which is human. A part of the lower like 'sensitive' (hassas) is a constituent of the higher, i.e., animal, and distinguishes it from all else, so it is also a constituent of the lower, i.e., human, because animal is included in the reality of human, so whatever is included in animal is included in it; for the part of a thing is a part of that thing. So 'sensitive' is included in the reality of human and distinguishes it from what animal distinguishes it from, and this is what is meant by constituent. (Ismail) And know: meaning the higher here is not what was previously mentioned, i.e., what has nothing above it, because this specification is invalid as the proof indicates. Rather, what is meant here is every genus or species that has another above it, whether it is under something or not, and similarly for the lower, not what has nothing under it, but rather what is under another, whether there is something under it or not. So the intermediate genus like 'growing body' (al-jism al-nami) is a higher genus insofar as it is above animal, and a lower genus insofar as it is under absolute body; and it is a higher species insofar as it is above a species which is animal, and a lower species insofar as another species which is absolute body. (Ismail) Universally, to repel an objection: It is that 'every constituent of the higher is a constituent of the lower' is a universal affirmative proposition, and its converse 'some constituent of the lower is a constituent of the higher' is a particular affirmative that is necessarily true, so there is no meaning to his statement 'not the converse'. The clarification of the repulsion is that the commentator's statement 'universally' indicates that his statement 'not the converse' negates the universal affirmative, not the universal negative, and negating the universal affirmative does not contradict the particular affirmative. (Ismail and Abd) Every constituent is a differentia, proximate or remote, so it is not objected that if what is meant by constituent is the proximate differentia, then none of the proximate constituent of the lower is a constituent of the higher; and if what is meant is the remote differentia, then every remote constituent of the lower is a constituent of the higher. (Abd)
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage explains the logical rule that the constituent of a higher genus is also a constituent of any lower species under that genus. The author begins by explaining the lam in 'al-muqawwim li al-'ali' as for generalization, so the rule applies to every differentia that constitutes a higher genus. The reason given is that the constituent of the higher is a part of the higher, and the higher is a part of the lower (since the lower genus/species includes the higher), so by transitivity the constituent of the higher is a part of the lower and thus constitutes it. The author then gives a detailed illustration using the example of successive genera: from substance (jawhar) to body (jism) to growing body (jism nami) to animal (hayawan) to human (insan). Each higher genus has ambiguity that is removed by adding a differentia. The differentia that constitutes the higher also constitutes the lower because it is part of the essence of the lower through the hierarchy. The author then discusses the converse: 'not the converse', meaning that not every constituent of the lower is a constituent of the higher. For example, 'rational' (natiq) constitutes human (the lower) but does not constitute animal (the higher) because animal includes non-rational animals. However, some constituents of the lower like 'sensitive' (hassas) do constitute the higher (animal) as well. The author clarifies that the terms 'higher' and 'lower' here are relative, not absolute. The passage ends with a response to a potential objection: the statement 'not the converse' negates the universal claim that every constituent of the lower is a constituent of the higher, but it does not negate the particular claim that some are. This is consistent with the fact that some lower constituents (like 'sensitive') are also constituents of the higher.

Checkpoint
Which of the following best explains why the converse of the rule ('every constituent of the lower is a constituent of the higher') is false?
A) The higher genus does not contain all the constituents of the lower species.
B) Differentiae like 'rational' constitute lower species but not the higher genus.
C) The rule is not about differentiae but about genera.
D) The converse holds only for intermediate genera.

Key Takeaway
The constituent of a higher genus always constitutes any lower species, but the converse is only a particular truth: some constituents of the lower also constitute the higher.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Consider the genus 'living being' (al-hayy) which includes animal and plant. The differentia 'sensitive' (al-hassas) constitutes the higher genus? Is it also a constituent of every species under it? Justify using the hierarchical example from the lesson.
?
Task 2: In Kalam, the genus 'accident' (al-'arad) includes whiteness, blackness, etc. The differentia 'temporally originated' (huduth) is a constituent of accident. Does 'temporally originated' also constitute every species of accident? Explain why or why not based on the rule and its converse.

Classification of Universals: Necessary and Non-Necessary
Classifies universals into necessary and non-necessary, and subdivides necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a universal is necessary or non-necessary relative to its subject, and classify necessary universals as necessary by essence or necessary by existence.

Core Idea
Necessary universals cling to their subject; necessary by essence clings in conception, necessary by existence clings in reality.

This passage builds on the distinction between accidental universals (property and general accident) by dividing them into necessary (lāzim) and non-necessary (mufāriq). It then subdivides necessary into two types based on whether the attachment is due to the essence itself or due to its particular existence. Understanding this classification is crucial for defining things properly in Islamic logic, as it helps distinguish between essential and accidental properties in definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal property or general accident
The passage refers to 'كل واحد من الخاصة والعرض العام' (each one of the peculiar property and the general accident). These are the two types of accidental universals that are being classified.
2
Determine if the universal is inseparable
If the universal cannot be separated from its subject (i.e., it is impossible for the subject to exist without it), then it is necessary (lāzim). If it can be separated, it is non-necessary (mufāriq).
This is the primary division: 'إما لازم أو مفارق'.
3
Check the perspective of necessity: essence or existence
If necessary, determine whether it is necessary by essence (بالنظر إلى الماهية) or by existence (بالنظر إلى الوجود). Necessary by essence means the universal is inseparable whenever the subject is conceived, regardless of actual existence. Necessary by existence means the universal is inseparable only when the subject exists externally.
Example: 'potential writer' for human essence is necessary by essence; 'actual writing' is necessary by existence for a specific human.
4
Apply to a new example
Test yourself: is 'walking' necessary for animal essence? According to the passage, 'māshī' (walker) is a necessary universal for animal essence because animals are defined as walking beings, so it is necessary by essence. But for humans, it is non-necessary because humans may or may not walk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسامهما وعلى غيرها، وكل منهما إن امتنع انفكاكه عن الشيء فلازم بالنظر إلى الماهية أو الوجود، قوله وعلى غيرها كالماشي يقال على حقيقة الإنسان وعلى غيرها من الحقائق الحيوانية. قوله: وكل منهما: أي كل واحد من الخاصة والعرض العام، وبالجملة الكلي الذي هو عرض لأفراده إما لازم أو مفارق؛ إذ لا يخلو إما أن يستحيل انفكاكه عن معروضه أو لا، فالأول هو الأول والثاني هو الثاني، ثم اللازم ينقسم بتقسيمين: أحدهما: أن لازم الشيء إما لازم له بالنظر إلى نفس الماهية مع قطع النظر عن خصوص وجودها في الخارج أو في الذهن؛ وذلك بأن يكون هذا الشيء بحيث كلما تحقق في الذهن أو في الخارج كان هذا اللازم ثابتا له، وإما لازم له بالنظر إلى وجوده أي إلى خصوص وجوده الخارجي
Their divisions and upon others, and each of them if its separation from the thing is impossible, it is necessary with respect to the essence or existence. His statement: 'and upon others' like 'walker' is said of the human essence and of other animal essences. His statement: 'and each of them' i.e., each one of the peculiar property and the general accident. In summary, the universal that is an accident of its individuals is either necessary or non-necessary, for it is either impossible to be separated from its subject or not. The first is the first, the second is the second. Then the necessary is divided into two divisions: one of them is that the necessary of a thing is either necessary for it with respect to the essence itself disregarding the particularity of its existence in the external or in the mind, such that this thing is such that whenever it is realized in the mind or in the external, this necessary is established for it; or it is necessary for it with respect to its existence, i.e., with respect to its particular external existence.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage introduces a further classification of the universal property and general accident (collectively called 'universals that are accidents of their individuals') into two kinds: necessary (lāzim) and non-necessary (mufāriq / 'accidental' in the sense of separable). A necessary universal is one that cannot be separated from its subject (the individual); a non-necessary one can be separated. The author then subdivides the necessary universal into two types: (1) necessary with respect to the essence itself (bi-l-nazar ilā nafs al-māhiyya), independent of whether the essence exists in the mind or externally, meaning that whenever the essence is conceived, the universal must be present (e.g., the property of being a potential writer for human essence). (2) necessary with respect to existence (bi-l-nazar ilā wujūdihi), i.e., with respect to its particular external existence, meaning that the universal is necessary only when the thing exists externally (e.g., accidental properties like being a certain color). The example given is 'walker', which is a necessary universal for animal essence (since animals are defined as walking beings) but may be non-necessary for humans (since humans can walk or not). The passage lays the groundwork for understanding the different modalities of predication.

Key Terms
لازم
مفارق
بالنظر إلى الماهية
بالنظر إلى الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a necessary universal by essence?
A) 'Student' for a specific person.
B) 'Potential writer' for human essence.
C) 'Actual writer' for a human who writes.
D) 'Color' for a dyed object.

Key Takeaway
The key to this classification is that 'necessary' in logic means inseparable, not always existing; and the two perspectives (essence vs existence) determine how strongly the universal attaches.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the universal 'rational' (nāṭiq) for human, determine if it is necessary or non-necessary. If necessary, of which type? Explain your reasoning using the passage's criteria.
?
Provide a Fiqh-related example of a universal that is necessary by existence (e.g., the property of being in a specific location for a prayer) and justify why it is not necessary by essence.

Absolute Temporal Proposition (وقتية مطلقة)
Definition and example of absolute temporal proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an absolute temporal proposition and distinguish it from other modal propositions using the condition of time-specific necessity.

Core Idea
Time-specific necessity: the predicate is necessary at a designated moment.

Modal propositions in Islamic logic are divided by time restriction. This lesson focuses on the absolute temporal (وقتية مطلقة), where necessity applies only at a specific time. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing arguments in Islamic sciences that depend on timed conditions, such as lunar eclipse rulings in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term 'وقتية مطلقة' in the Arabic passage. What is being defined?
It is a type of simple modal proposition.
2
Extract the condition of time specificity
The definition states that necessity is restricted to a specific time (تقييد الضرورة بالوقت). Note the contrast with non-specific time.
3
Examine the example
The moon eclipse example: 'كل قمر منخسف بالضرورة وقت حيلولة الأرض' – the necessity holds only at the time of opposition, not at quadrature.
The commentary explains why the moon must be eclipsed at that time due to earth's interposition.
4
Compare with other types
Contrast with absolute permanent (دائمة مطلقة) and absolute distributed (منتشرة مطلقة). What makes the temporal type distinct?
The key is that the time is specified and the necessity does not last indefinitely.

أو غير معين فمنتشرة مطلقة أو بدوامها ما دام الذات فدائمة مطلقة، أو ما دام الوصف :نحو كل قمر منخسف بالضرورة وقت حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس ولا شيء من القمر بمنخسف بالضرورة وقت التربيع، فتسمى حينئذٍ وقتية مطلقة لتقييد الضرورة بالوقت وعدم تقييد القضية باللادوام. الرابع: إنها ضرورية في وقت من الأوقات، كقولنا: كل إنسان متنفس بالضرورة وقتا ما أو لا شيء منه بمتنفس بالضرورة وقتا ما فتسمى حينئذ منتشرة مطلقة؛ لكون وقت الضرورة فيها منتشرا أي غير معين وعدم تقييد القضية باللادوام. منخسف : فإنه قد تقرر في غير هذا الفن أن نور القمر مستفاد من ضياء الشمس، فظاهر أن حيلولة الأرض بينه وبين الشمس مانعة من تلك الإضاءة، فلابد من كونه مظلما في هذا الوقت المعين. ( إسماعيل) وقتية مطلقة ومحمولها يكون عرضا لازما لذات الموضوع في وقت معين، فيتحقق في مادة الضرورية بالضرورة ولا عكس منتشرة مطلقة ويكون محمولها أيضاً عرضا لازما لذات الموضوع لا في وقت غير معين وفي التحقق، كالوقتية المطلقة.
Or it is non-specific, so it is an absolute distributed proposition. Or by its permanence as long as the essence exists, so it is an absolute permanent proposition. Or as long as the description: such as 'Every moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of the earth's interposition between it and the sun' and 'No moon is eclipsed necessarily at the time of the quadrature'. Then it is called an absolute temporal proposition because necessity is restricted to a time and the proposition is not restricted to non-permanence. Fourth: It is necessary at some time, like our saying: 'Every human is breathing necessarily at some time' or 'No human is breathing necessarily at some time'. Then it is called an absolute distributed proposition because the time of necessity is distributed, i.e., non-specific, and the proposition is not restricted to non-permanence. (Commentary on 'eclipsed': It has been established in another discipline that the light of the moon is derived from the sun's rays, so it is apparent that the earth's interposition between it and the sun prevents that illumination, so it must be dark at that specific time. (Isma'il) An absolute temporal proposition: its predicate is an accidental attribute necessarily attached to the subject's essence at a specific time, so it is realized in the matter of necessity necessarily, and not conversely. An absolute distributed proposition: its predicate is also an accidental attribute necessarily attached to the subject's essence, but at a non-specific time, and in actuality it is like the absolute temporal proposition.)
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage defines two types of modal propositions: absolute temporal (وقتية مطلقة) and absolute distributed (منتشرة مطلقة). The author begins by listing the divisions of simple modal propositions: non-specific, permanent with essence, and with description. Then he gives examples for time-specific and time-non-specific necessity. The example for absolute temporal is the lunar eclipse: it is necessary that the moon is eclipsed specifically when the earth is between it and the sun, and not at quadrature. The example for absolute distributed is human breathing: every human breathes necessarily at some unspecified time. The commentary by Isma'il explains that the predicate in an absolute temporal proposition is a necessary accident at a specific time, so it falls under the matter of necessity. The absolute distributed proposition is similar but the time is non-specific. This classification helps in understanding the various kinds of modal propositions used in logical reasoning.

B) Every moon is eclipsed necessarily during opposition.
C) Every human is created necessarily at the moment of conception.
D) Every animal moves necessarily at some time.

Key Takeaway
An absolute temporal proposition restricts necessity to a specific time, as illustrated by the moon's eclipse at opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Answer: It is absolute temporal because the time (Fajr) is specific. Compare: 'Every Muslim prays necessarily at some time during the day' would be absolute distributed.

Absolute Distributed Proposition (منتشرة مطلقة)
Definition and example of absolute distributed proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an absolute distributed proposition and understand how it differs from absolute temporal propositions.

Core Idea
Non-specific time: necessity applies at some time, but not designated.

While absolute temporal propositions tie necessity to a specific moment, absolute distributed (منتشرة مطلقة) involve necessity at an unspecified time. This lesson unpacks that distinction using the example of human breathing, which is necessary at some time but not at any particular moment. Knowing this helps in evaluating modal claims in Islamic sciences like Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term 'منتشرة مطلقة' in the Arabic passage. Note that it is also called 'absolute distributed'.
It is the fourth type listed.
2
Extract the condition of non-specific time
The definition says 'ضرورية في وقت من الأوقات' – necessary at some time, but the time is not determined ('غير معين').
The passage explicitly contrasts this with the temporal type.
3
Examine the example
The example: 'كل إنسان متنفس بالضرورة وقتا ما' – every human breathes necessarily at some time. This is true even though no specific time is mentioned.
The predicate (breathing) is a necessary attribute for humans at some point, but not necessarily all the time.
4
Compare with absolute temporal
Contrast the moon eclipse (specific time) with human breathing (non-specific). What changes? Both are necessary, but the temporal scope differs.
In absolute distributed, the necessity is 'spread out' over possible times.

Necessity vs. Permanence in Modal Propositions
Author defines absolute permanent proposition and explains the difference between necessity and permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ضرورة (necessity) and دوام (permanence) in modal propositions and identify the defining features of the absolute permanent proposition (دائمة مطلقة).

Core Idea
Permanence is broader than necessity: everything necessary is permanent, but not everything permanent is necessary.

In this passage, the author defines a key modal proposition type: the absolute permanent proposition (دائمة مطلقة). He clarifies a critical distinction between necessity (ضرورة) and permanence (دوام) that is often misunderstood. Understanding this distinction is essential for evaluating arguments in kalām about the eternity of the world and divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms being compared
Find where the author contrasts 'ضرورة' and 'دوام' in the Arabic. Note that he begins by stating the difference between them.
2
Extract the definitions
Read the definitions: ضرورة is 'استحالة انفكاك شيء عن شيء' (impossibility of separation) and دوام is 'عدم انفكاكه عنه وإن لم يكن مستحيلا' (non-separation even if not impossible).
The example of celestial sphere motion illustrates permanence without necessity.
3
Analyze the relationship using the example
The motion of the celestial sphere is permanent (it never ceases) but not necessary (it could cease). This shows that دوام is more general: it includes both necessary and non-necessary permanence.
The author explicitly says 'الدوام أعم من الضرورة' (permanence is more general than necessity).
4
Identify why the proposition is called 'دائمة مطلقة'
The proposition is called 'دائمة' because it asserts permanence, and 'مطلقة' because the permanence is not restricted by an attributive description (وصف عنواني).
The commentary explains that the name reflects its logical properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: فدائمة مطلقة: الفرق بين الضرورة والدوام أن الضرورة هي استحالة انفكاك شيء عن شيء، والدوام عدم انفكاكه عنه وإن لم يكن مستحيلا كدوام الحركة للفلك. ثم الدوام أعني عدم انفكاك النسبة الإيجابية أو السلبية عن الموضوع إما ذاتي أو وصفي، فإن كان الحكم في الموجهة بالدوام الذاتي أي بعدم انفكاك النسبة عن الموضوع ما دام ذات الموضوع موجودة. والدوام عدم انفكاكه عنه إلخ فالدوام أعم من الضرورة؛ فإن الشيء كلما استحال انفكاكه عن الشيء الآخر يكون ثبوته له دائما البتة، وإلا فيكون منفكا عنه في بعض الأوقات فيلزم وقوع المحال، بخلاف إذا كان الشيء غير منفك عن الآخر أزلا وأبداً؛ فإنه لا يستلزم أن يكون ثبوته ضرورياً؛ الجواز أن يكون الانفكاك ممكنا غير واقع؛ فإن الممكن لا يجب وقوعه بالفعل كدوام الحركة للفلك. وفي التمثيل بمادة افتراق الدوام عن الضرورة إشارة ضمنية إلى أن الدوام أعم من الضرورة؛ فإن تحقق الدوام كلما تحققت الضرورة ظاهر. وإسماعيل) وإن لم يكن مستحيلا فالدوام قد يكون مع الضرورة وقد لا يكون فهو أعم مطلقا من الضرورة صدقا وبينهما عموم وخصوص مطلقا . (عبد) سميت القضية دائمة؛ لاشتمالها على الدوام، ومطلقة؛ لعدم تقييد الدوام بالوصف العنواني
His saying: 'So it is an absolute permanent proposition': The difference between necessity and permanence is that necessity is the impossibility of a thing being separated from another thing, and permanence is the non-separation of it from it even if it is not impossible, like the permanence of motion for the celestial sphere. Then permanence—I mean the non-separation of the affirmative or negative relation from the subject—is either essential or descriptive. If the judgment in the modal proposition is for essential permanence, i.e., the non-separation of the relation from the subject as long as the subject's essence exists. And permanence is non-separation from it, etc. So permanence is more general than necessity; for whenever a thing's separation from another is impossible, its existence for it is perpetual, otherwise it would be separated at some times, leading to the occurrence of the impossible. But if a thing is not separated from another eternally, it does not necessitate that its existence is necessary; it is possible that the separation is possible but not actual, for the possible is not necessarily actual, like the permanence of motion for the celestial sphere. In the example, there is an implicit indication of the matter of the distinction of permanence from necessity, showing that permanence is more general than necessity; for the realization of permanence whenever necessity is realized is obvious. (Isma'il) And even if it is not impossible, permanence may be with necessity or without it, so it is absolutely more general than necessity in truth, and between them is absolute generality and specificity. (Abd) The proposition is called 'permanent' because it includes permanence, and 'absolute' because permanence is not restricted by the attributive description.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage defines the absolute permanent proposition (دائمة مطلقة) and distinguishes between necessity (ضرورة) and permanence (دوام). The author begins by citing a line from the matn and then explains the difference: necessity implies impossibility of separation, while permanence means actual non-separation even if separation is possible, like the motion of the celestial sphere which is permanent but not necessary (since it could be otherwise). Then he divides permanence into essential (ذاتي) and descriptive (وصفي). Essential permanence means the predicate holds as long as the subject's essence exists. The commentary emphasizes that permanence is more general than necessity: whenever necessity holds, permanence holds, but not vice versa. The example of the celestial sphere's motion illustrates this: it is permanent but not necessary. The proposition is called 'permanent' (دائمة) because it asserts permanence, and 'absolute' (مطلقة) because permanence is not restricted to a description. This distinction is crucial for understanding modal propositions in Islamic logic, especially when evaluating arguments about the createdness of the world or attributes of God.

دوام

ذاتي

Distinguishing Urfiyya Amma and Mutlaqa Amma
Author defines descriptive permanent and absolute general propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a general conventional proposition (ʿurfiyya ʿāmma) and an absolute general proposition (muṭlaqa ʿāmma) based on their modal conditions.

Core Idea
General conventional propositions depend on an attribute; absolute general propositions assert pure actuality.

This passage from the Tahzeeb al-Mantiq clarifies two key modal propositions that frequently appear in Islamic logical analysis. Understanding the difference is essential for correctly interpreting statements in kalam and fiqh, especially when rulings are conditional on attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proposition types
Locate the definitions of ʿurfiyya ʿāmma and muṭlaqa ʿāmma in the Arabic. The passage states: 'So it is a general conventional, or by its actuality so it is an absolute general.'
The matn is very concise; the commentary expands on the meaning.
2
Extract the modal condition for each
For ʿurfiyya ʿāmma: the judgment holds as long as a descriptive attribute (ʿunwān) remains. The commentary says: 'if the judgment is for descriptive permanence, i.e., the non-separation of the relation from the subject as long as the attributive description is established.' For muṭlaqa ʿāmma: the judgment asserts actuality (bi-l-fiʿl) without any temporal or descriptive restriction.
The condition for ʿurfiyya is often understood by custom (ʿurf).
3
Analyse the example given
The passage gives the example 'Every writer moves his fingers' as a ʿurfiyya ʿāmma. It explains that people of custom understand this to be true only while the person is a writer. This illustrates the attribute condition.
If the same statement were taken as muṭlaqa, it would be false for a writer who is not currently writing.
4
Contrast with absolute general
The muṭlaqa ʿāmma proposition asserts that the relation is actually realized, regardless of any attribute. The predicate is a 'separable accident' (ʿaraḍ mufāriq) that can cease without destroying the subject. For example, 'The sun is rising' is true in the actual moment but not dependent on a description.
The muṭlaqa is the most general of modal propositions; others are special cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعرفية عامة، أو بفعليتها فمطلقة عامة. قوله: أو بفعليتها: أي بتحقق النسبة بالفعل. فالمطلقة العامة هي التي حكم فيها بكون النسبة متحققة. سميت القضية دائمة؛ لاشتمالها على الدوام، ومطلقة؛ لعدم تقييد الدوام بالوصف العنواني أو إن كان الحكم بالدوام الوصفي أي بعدم انفكاك النسبة عن ذات الموضوع ما دام الوصف العنواني ثابتا لتلك الذات سميت عرفية؛ لأن أهل العرف يفهمون هذا المعنى من القضية السالبة بل من الموجبة أيضاً عند الإطلاق، فإذا قيل: كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع فهموا أن هذا الحكم ثابت ما دام كاتبا، وعامة؛ لكونها أعم من العرفية الخاصة التي سيجيء ذكرها. (إسماعيل) أي بتحقق النسبة بالفعل مراد المصنف بالفعل ههنا مقابل القوة أعم من أن يكون بالقوة ثم خرج إلى الفعل في الحال أو الماضي أو الاستقبال أو على الاستمرار والدوام. (شوستري) فالمطلقة العامة ومحمولها يكون عرضا مفارقا ذات الموضوع ويتحقق في مواد القضايا السابقة؛ لأنها أعمها وتحقق الخاص في مادة العام ليس بضروري.
So it is a general conventional (proposition), or by its actuality so it is an absolute general (proposition). His saying: 'Or by its actuality' i.e., by the actual realization of the relation. So the absolute general proposition is that in which it is judged that the relation is actualized. The proposition is called 'permanent' because it includes permanence, and 'absolute' because permanence is not restricted by the attributive description. Or if the judgment is for descriptive permanence, i.e., the non-separation of the relation from the subject as long as the attributive description is established for that essence, it is called 'conventional' because people of custom (ʿurf) understand this meaning from the negative proposition, rather also from the affirmative when unqualified. For when it is said: 'Every writer moves his fingers', they understand that this judgment holds as long as he is a writer. And 'general' because it is more general than the specific conventional that will be mentioned. (Isma'il) 'By its actuality' means the author intends by 'actuality' here the opposite of potentiality, general as to whether it is potential then emerges to actuality in the present, past, future, or continuously and permanently. (Shustari) So the absolute general proposition: its predicate is a separable accident of the subject's essence, and it is realized in the matters of the previous propositions because it is the most general of them, and the realization of the specific in the matter of the general is not necessary.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage defines two more types of modal propositions: general conventional (عرفية عامة) and absolute general (مطلقة عامة). The matn states: 'So it is a general conventional, or by its actuality so it is an absolute general.' The commentary explains that the absolute general proposition judges that the relation is actually realized, not merely potential. The conventional proposition is defined as one where the judgment holds as long as an attributive description remains, e.g., 'Every writer moves his fingers' is understood by custom to be true only as long as the person is writing. It is called 'general' because it includes all such cases, unlike the specific conventional which will be mentioned later. The commentator Shustari clarifies that 'by its actuality' means real existence in time, whether present, past, future, or permanent. The absolute general proposition's predicate is a separable accident, meaning it can cease without destroying the subject. This type is the most general of all modal propositions, and the others are special cases within it. This classification helps in analyzing propositions in arguments, such as when evaluating whether a ruling in fiqh is conditional on a specific attribute.

Key Terms
عرفية عامة
مطلقة عامة
عرض مفارق
بالفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of modal proposition is 'Every writer moves his fingers' an example of?
A) Absolute permanent (دائمة مطلقة)
B) Absolute temporal (وقتية مطلقة)
C) General conventional (عرفية عامة)
D) Absolute general (مطلقة عامة)

Key Takeaway
The general conventional proposition ties its truth to an ongoing attribute, while the absolute general proposition simply asserts actuality without any such condition.

The Three Essential Differences for Logical Contradiction
Commentary on the necessity of three differences for contradiction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a pair of categorical propositions satisfies the three required differences (quality, quantity, modality) for a genuine contradiction.

Core Idea
Contradiction demands more than opposite truth values; it requires specific logical differences.

In this passage, the commentator expands on the conditions for contradiction beyond the basic truth conditions. He explains that for two propositions to be genuine contradictories, they must differ in quality (affirmative/negative), quantity (universal/particular), and modality (necessary/possible). Without these differences, two propositions may both be true or both false, failing the strict definition. This precision is vital for rigorous logical argumentation in Islamic disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quality difference
The most basic condition: one proposition must be affirmative, the other negative. Check immediately.
2
Check quantity if propositions are categorical
If propositions have quantifiers (all, some, none), they must differ: one universal, the other particular. Two universals can both be false, and two particulars can both be true.
3
Check modality if propositions are modalized
If they include 'necessarily', 'possibly', etc., the modality must differ. Two necessary propositions can both be false; two possible can both be true.
4
Apply the test
Given a pair, verify all three conditions. If any condition is not met, they are not contradictories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالعكس، ولا بد من الاختلاف في الكم والكيف والجهة وخرج فإنهما قد يصدقان معا نحو بعض الحيوان إنسان وبعضه ليس بإنسان، فلم يتحقق التناقض بين الجزئيتين قوله وبالعكس أي ويلزم من كذب كلّ من القضيتين صدق الأخرى، بهذا القيد الاختلاف الواقع بين الموجبة والسالبة الكليتين؛ فإنهما قد تكذبان معا، نحو: لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان وكل حيوان إنسان، فلم يتحقق التناقض بين الكليتين أيضاً، فقد علم أن القضيتين لو كانتا محصورتين يجب اختلافهما في الكم، كما سيُصرح به المصنف محله، قوله ولابد من الاختلاف: أي يشترط في التناقض أن يكون إحدى القضيتين موجبة والأخرى سالبة؛ ضرورة أن الموجبتين وكذا السالبتين قد تجتمعان في الصدق والكذب معاً، ثم إن كانت القضيتان محصورتين يجب اختلافهما في الكم أيضاً، كما مر، ثم إن كانتا موجهتين يجب اختلافهما في الجهة؛ فإن الضروريتين قد تكذبان معا، كقولنا كل إنسان كاتب بالضرورة ولا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب بالضرورة والممكنتين قد تصدقان معاً، كقولنا: كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان العام ولا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب بالإمكان العام. يرد: أنه لفظ قد تصدقان واعلم أنهم أخرجوا القضايا الذهنية وغير المتعارفة عن التناقض وعن العكوس أيضاً، فلا يصدق على بعض النوع إنسان ولا يكذب نقيضه وهو لا شيء من النوع بإنسان؛ إذ الجزئية المذكورة ليست متعارفة؛ إذ الإنسان لا يصدق على النوع صدق الكلي على جزئياته فافهم وبالعكس: قيل: لا حاجة إلى قوله: "بالعكس"؛ لأنه مندرج في قوله "من" صدق كل كذب الأخرى"؛ إذ المراد من لفظ "كل" وكذا من "الأخرى" أعم من الأصل والنقيض معاً، أقول: هذا الاندراج يفهم بدلالة الالتزام والالتزام مهجور في التعريفات. أي يشترط إشارة إلى أن لفظ "لابد" قد يستعمل في الركن وتارة في الشرط وههنا مستعمل في الشرط بقرينة ذكره بعد التعريف، وإلى أن الاختلاف في الكيف شرط في الجميع والاختلاف في الكم شرط في نوع منه وهو التناقض بين المحصورتين فلا يرد النقض ؛ لوجود التناقض بين المخصوصتين بدون الاختلاف في الكم. في الصدق نحو كل إنسان حيوان وبعض الإنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الإنسان بفرس وبعض الإنسان ليس بفرس. والكذب: نحو كل إنسان فرس وبعض الإنسان فرس ولا شيء من الإنسان بناطق وبعض الإنسان ليس بناطق. والاتحاد فيما عداها. قوله والاتحاد فيما عداها أي يشترط في التناقض اتحاد القضيتين فيما عدا الأمور الثلاثة المذكورة أعني الكيف والكم والجهة، وقد ضبطوا هذا الاتحاد في ضمن الاتحاد في أمور ثمانية. الآلة والاتحاد في ما عداها والله درّ المصنف حيث لم يحصر ما عداها في الثمانية، فلا یرد أنه لا تناقض باختلاف والعلة والمفعول به والتمييز مثلا نحو زيد رام بالسهم الأحمد نگرى وزيد ليس برام بالسهم الاورنگ آبادي، والنحار عامل أي بأمر السلطان والنجار ليس بعامل أي لأمر غيره، وزيد ضارب أي لعمرو وزيد ليس بضارب أي لبكر، وعندي عشرون أي درهما وليس عندي عشرون أي دينارا، فلابد من الاتحاد في ماعدا الثمانية، ولذا قال بعض المحققين أنه لابد في تحقق التناقض من وحدة النسبة الحكمية؛ فإن هذه الوحدة مستلزمة الموحدات الثمانية وغيرها ولا يخفى عليك أن إدراج ماعدا الثمانية مما ذكر آنفا في وحدة المحمول ممكن، كما أدرج الفارابي بعضا منها في بعض، كما بين. فإن قيل: الشيء كما يحمل على نفسه كذلك يحمل على نقيضه أعني اللاشيء، فاجتمع النقيضان أعني الشيء واللاشيء. قلنا: اتحاد نحو الحمل أيضاً شرط في تحقق التناقض، وحمل الشيء على نفسه حمل أولي وعلى اللاشيء حمل متعارف فلم يوجد شرط التناقض والحمل الأولي حمل الشيء على نفسه كحمل الإنسان على نفسه؛ لأنه أول الحمل والحمل المتعارف حمل الشيء على أفراده كحمل الإنسان على زيد وعمرو وبكر وغير ذلك وحمل الحيوان على الإنسان. في أمور ثمانية فلا تناقض عند اختلاف الموضوع، نحو: زيد قائم وعمرو ليس بقائم، والمحمول، نحو: زيد قام، وزيد ليس بقاعد والمكان نحو زيد جالس أي في السوق وزيد ليس بجالس أي في الدار، والشرط، نحو: الجسم مفرق للبصر أي بشرط كونه أبيض والجسم ليس مفرق للبصر أي بشرط كونه أسود، والإضافة، نحو: زيد أب أي لعمرو وزيد ليس بأب أي لبكر ، والجزء والكل نحو الزنجي أسود أي بعضه والزنجي ليس بأسود أي كله؛ فإن عظمه أبيض والقوة والفعل نحو: الخمر مسكر في الدن أي بالقوة والخمر ليس بمسكر في الدن أي بالفعل، والزمان، نحو، زيد نائم أي في الليل وزيد ليس بنائم أي في النهار. فهذه ثمان وحدات ذكرها المتقدمون لتحقق التناقض. وأما المتأخرون فبعضهم أدرجوا وحدة الشرط والكل والجزء تحت وحدة الموضوع؛ لاختلافه بعدم الوحدات، ووحدة المكان والإضافة والقوة والفعل تحت وحدة المحمول؛ لاختلافه بعدم هذه الوحدات. فبقي ثلاث وحدات وحدة الموضوع ووحدة المحمول ووحدة الزمان، وبعضهم أدرجوا وحدة الزمان تحت وحدة المحمول؛ لاختلافه باختلاف الزمان فاكتفوا بوحدتين. وردها الفارابي إلى وحدة النسبة الحكمية؛ فإن اختلاف شيء من الموضوع والمحمول وما يتعلق بهما يستلزم اختلاف النسبة.
And conversely, and it is necessary to have difference in quantity, quality, and modality, and it excluded that both may be true together, like 'some animal is human' and 'some animal is not human', so contradiction is not realized between two particulars. His statement 'and conversely' means that from the falsity of each of the two propositions the truth of the other necessarily follows. By this restriction, the difference between the universal affirmative and negative is that both may be false together, like 'no animal is human' and 'every animal is human', so contradiction is not realized between two universals either. Thus it is known that if the two propositions are quantified, they must differ in quantity, as the author will clarify in its place. His statement 'and there must be difference' means that it is required for contradiction that one of the two propositions be affirmative and the other negative; necessarily because two affirmatives and likewise two negatives may agree in truth and falsity together. Then if the two propositions are quantified, they must also differ in quantity, as mentioned. Then if they are modalized, they must differ in modality; for two necessary propositions may both be false, like 'every human is a writer necessarily' and 'no human is a writer necessarily', and two possible propositions may both be true, like 'every human is a writer with general possibility' and 'no human is a writer with general possibility'. It is objected that the wording 'both may be true' — and know that they excluded mental propositions and uncommon ones from contradiction and also from converses. So it is not true that 'some of the species is human' nor is its contradictory 'no species is human' false; because the mentioned particular is not common, since 'human' is not predicated of 'species' as a universal is predicated of its particulars. So understand. 'And conversely': It is said that there is no need for his statement 'conversely' because it is included in his statement 'from the truth of each the falsity of the other'; since the intended meaning of 'each' and also of 'the other' is more general than both the original and the contradictory. I say: this inclusion is understood by implication, and implication is abandoned in definitions. That is, it indicates that the word 'there must be' is sometimes used for pillar and sometimes for condition, and here it is used for condition by the context of its mention after the definition, and that difference in quality is a condition in all, and difference in quantity is a condition in a type of it, namely contradiction between quantified propositions, so the objection does not apply; because contradiction exists between unquantified propositions without difference in quantity. In truth, like 'every human is animal' and 'some human is animal', and 'no human is a horse' and 'some human is not a horse'. And in falsity, like 'every human is a horse' and 'some human is a horse', and 'no human is rational' and 'some human is not rational'. And unity in what is other than them. His statement 'and unity in what is other than them' means that it is required for contradiction that the two propositions be united in what is other than the three mentioned matters, namely quality, quantity, and modality. And they have regulated this unity under unity in eight matters. The instrument and unity in what is other than them — and how excellent is the author in not restricting what is other than them to the eight, so it is not objected that there is no contradiction with difference in instrument, cause, object, distinction, etc., for example: 'Zayd is a shooter with an Ahmad Nagri arrow' and 'Zayd is not a shooter with an Aurangabadi arrow', and 'the carpenter is an agent by the sultan's order' and 'the carpenter is not an agent by someone else's order', and 'Zayd is a beater of Amr' and 'Zayd is not a beater of Bakr', and 'I have twenty dirhams' and 'I do not have twenty dinars'. Therefore unity in what is other than the eight is necessary. Hence some investigators said that for the realization of contradiction, unity of the relational judgment is necessary; for this unity necessitates the eight unities and others. And it is not hidden from you that including what is other than the eight mentioned above under unity of predicate is possible, just as al-Farabi included some of them under others, as explained. For if it is said: 'The thing is predicated of itself just as it is predicated of its opposite, namely non-thing, so the two opposites, namely thing and non-thing, come together.' We say: Unity of the mode of predication is also a condition for the realization of contradiction. Predication of a thing of itself is primary predication, and of non-thing is conventional predication, so the condition of contradiction is not present. Primary predication is predicating a thing of itself, like predicating 'human' of itself, because it is the first predication, and conventional predication is predicating a thing of its individuals, like predicating 'human' of Zayd, Amr, Bakr, etc., and predicating 'animal' of human. In eight matters: so there is no contradiction when there is difference in subject, like 'Zayd is standing' and 'Amr is not standing'; in predicate, like 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is not sitting'; in place, like 'Zayd is sitting in the market' and 'Zayd is not sitting in the house'; in condition, like 'the body is pupil-dispersing on condition of being white' and 'the body is not pupil-dispersing on condition of being black'; in relation, like 'Zayd is a father of Amr' and 'Zayd is not a father of Bakr'; in part and whole, like 'the black person is black with respect to a part' and 'the black person is not black with respect to the whole, for his bones are white'; in potency and act, like 'wine is intoxicating in the jar potentially' and 'wine is not intoxicating in the jar actually'; in time, like 'Zayd is sleeping at night' and 'Zayd is not sleeping during the day'. These are eight unities mentioned by the earlier scholars for the realization of contradiction. As for the later scholars, some of them brought unity of condition, part and whole under unity of subject because of their difference through these unities, and unity of place, relation, potency and act under unity of predicate because of their difference through these unities. So three unities remained: unity of subject, unity of predicate, and unity of time. And some of them brought unity of time under unity of predicate because of its difference through difference in time, so they sufficed with two unities. And al-Farabi reduced them to unity of the relational judgment; for difference in anything of the subject, predicate, and what pertains to them necessitates difference in the relation.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the conditions for logical contradiction (tanāquḍ). The commentator first addresses a matn statement about the necessity of difference in quantity, quality, and modality. He explains that contradiction cannot occur between two particulars (juz'iyyatayn) because both can be true simultaneously, nor between two universals (kulliyyatayn) because both can be false. Thus, quantifiers must differ when propositions are quantified. The three required differences are detailed: quality (affirmative vs. negative), quantity (universal vs. particular), and modality (necessary vs. possible vs. actual). Examples are given for each, e.g., necessary propositions like 'Every human is a writer necessarily' and 'No human is a writer necessarily' can both be false, so modality must differ. The commentary then notes that mental and uncommon propositions are excluded from contradiction. Next, the condition of unity in all other respects is introduced, traditionally formulated as eight unities: subject, predicate, place, condition, relation, part/whole, potency/act, and time. Each is illustrated with examples from everyday language and fiqh-like contexts, e.g., difference in instrument ('shooter with arrow type') or condition ('pupil-dispersing if white vs. black'). The commentary also discusses later reductions of these eight unities: some scholars reduce to three (subject, predicate, time) or two (subject and predicate), and al-Farabi ultimately reduces all to unity of the relational judgment (waḥdat al-nisba al-ḥukmiyya), arguing that any difference in subject, predicate, or their attachments entails a difference in the relation expressed by the judgment. This passage thus provides a comprehensive treatment of the logical conditions governing contradiction, essential for properly evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can two universal propositions never be contradictory?
A) They can never be of opposite quality.
B) They can both be false at the same time.
C) They are always true.
D) They lack modality.

Key Takeaway
For two propositions to be genuine contradictories, they must differ in quality, and if quantified or modalized, also in quantity or modality respectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the pair: 'Every human is mortal' and 'No human is mortal'. Do they satisfy the three conditions? Check quality, quantity, modality.
?
Find a pair of contradictories from the opening of a kalam textbook (e.g., al-Sanusiyya) and verify the conditions.

The Eight Unities for Contradiction and Their Reductions
Commentary enumerating the eight unities and their reductions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight unities required for contradiction and explain how later logicians, including al-Farabi, reduced them.

Core Idea
The eight unities ensure that only the minimum logical differences count; all else must be identical for true contradiction.

Beyond the three differences, the logicians established that for two propositions to be contradictory, they must agree in eight respects: subject, predicate, place, condition, relation, part/whole, potency/act, and time. This lesson explores these unities, their illustrative examples, and the historical reductions culminating in al-Farabi's single unity of relational judgment.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the eight unities
Subject, predicate, place, condition, relation, part/whole, potency/act, time.
2
Understand each with examples
E.g., difference in place: 'Zayd is sitting in the market' vs 'Zayd is not sitting in the house'.
3
Trace the reductions
Later scholars merged place, relation, potency/act under predicate; condition, part/whole under subject; time under predicate or kept separate. al-Farabi reduced all to unity of relational judgment.
4
Apply to a real pair
Given two statements about a fiqh ruling, check if any of the eight unities are violated.

الشيء واللاشيء. قلنا: اتحاد نحو الحمل أيضاً شرط في تحقق التناقض، وحمل الشيء على نفسه حمل أولي وعلى اللاشيء حمل متعارف فلم يوجد شرط التناقض والحمل الأولي حمل الشيء على نفسه كحمل الإنسان على نفسه؛ لأنه أول الحمل والحمل المتعارف حمل الشيء على أفراده كحمل الإنسان على زيد وعمرو وبكر وغير ذلك وحمل الحيوان على الإنسان. في أمور ثمانية فلا تناقض عند اختلاف الموضوع، نحو: زيد قائم وعمرو ليس بقائم، والمحمول، نحو: زيد قام، وزيد ليس بقاعد والمكان نحو زيد جالس أي في السوق وزيد ليس بجالس أي في الدار، والشرط، نحو: الجسم مفرق للبصر أي بشرط كونه أبيض والجسم ليس مفرق للبصر أي بشرط كونه أسود، والإضافة، نحو: زيد أب أي لعمرو وزيد ليس بأب أي لبكر ، والجزء والكل نحو الزنجي أسود أي بعضه والزنجي ليس بأسود أي كله؛ فإن عظمه أبيض والقوة والفعل نحو: الخمر مسكر في الدن أي بالقوة والخمر ليس بمسكر في الدن أي بالفعل، والزمان، نحو، زيد نائم أي في الليل وزيد ليس بنائم أي في النهار. فهذه ثمان وحدات ذكرها المتقدمون لتحقق التناقض. وأما المتأخرون فبعضهم أدرجوا وحدة الشرط والكل والجزء تحت وحدة الموضوع؛ لاختلافه بعدم الوحدات، ووحدة المكان والإضافة والقوة والفعل تحت وحدة المحمول؛ لاختلافه بعدم هذه الوحدات. فبقي ثلاث وحدات وحدة الموضوع ووحدة المحمول ووحدة الزمان، وبعضهم أدرجوا وحدة الزمان تحت وحدة المحمول؛ لاختلافه باختلاف الزمان فاكتفوا بوحدتين. وردها الفارابي إلى وحدة النسبة الحكمية؛ فإن اختلاف شيء من الموضوع والمحمول وما يتعلق بهما يستلزم اختلاف النسبة.
And conversely, and it is necessary to have difference in quantity, quality, and modality, and it excluded that both may be true together, like 'some animal is human' and 'some animal is not human', so contradiction is not realized between two particulars. His statement 'and conversely' means that from the falsity of each of the two propositions the truth of the other necessarily follows. By this restriction, the difference between the universal affirmative and negative is that both may be false together, like 'no animal is human' and 'every animal is human', so contradiction is not realized between two universals either. Thus it is known that if the two propositions are quantified, they must differ in quantity, as the author will clarify in its place. His statement 'and there must be difference' means that it is required for contradiction that one of the two propositions be affirmative and the other negative; necessarily because two affirmatives and likewise two negatives may agree in truth and falsity together. Then if the two propositions are quantified, they must also differ in quantity, as mentioned. Then if they are modalized, they must differ in modality; for two necessary propositions may both be false, like 'every human is a writer necessarily' and 'no human is a writer necessarily', and two possible propositions may both be true, like 'every human is a writer with general possibility' and 'no human is a writer with general possibility'. It is objected that the wording 'both may be true' — and know that they excluded mental propositions and uncommon ones from contradiction and also from converses. So it is not true that 'some of the species is human' nor is its contradictory 'no species is human' false; because the mentioned particular is not common, since 'human' is not predicated of 'species' as a universal is predicated of its particulars. So understand. 'And conversely': It is said that there is no need for his statement 'conversely' because it is included in his statement 'from the truth of each the falsity of the other'; since the intended meaning of 'each' and also of 'the other' is more general than both the original and the contradictory. I say: this inclusion is understood by implication, and implication is abandoned in definitions. That is, it indicates that the word 'there must be' is sometimes used for pillar and sometimes for condition, and here it is used for condition by the context of its mention after the definition, and that difference in quality is a condition in all, and difference in quantity is a condition in a type of it, namely contradiction between quantified propositions, so the objection does not apply; because contradiction exists between unquantified propositions without difference in quantity. In truth, like 'every human is animal' and 'some human is animal', and 'no human is a horse' and 'some human is not a horse'. And in falsity, like 'every human is a horse' and 'some human is a horse', and 'no human is rational' and 'some human is not rational'. And unity in what is other than them. His statement 'and unity in what is other than them' means that it is required for contradiction that the two propositions be united in what is other than the three mentioned matters, namely quality, quantity, and modality. And they have regulated this unity under unity in eight matters. The instrument and unity in what is other than them — and how excellent is the author in not restricting what is other than them to the eight, so it is not objected that there is no contradiction with difference in instrument, cause, object, distinction, etc., for example: 'Zayd is a shooter with an Ahmad Nagri arrow' and 'Zayd is not a shooter with an Aurangabadi arrow', and 'the carpenter is an agent by the sultan's order' and 'the carpenter is not an agent by someone else's order', and 'Zayd is a beater of Amr' and 'Zayd is not a beater of Bakr', and 'I have twenty dirhams' and 'I do not have twenty dinars'. Therefore unity in what is other than the eight is necessary. Hence some investigators said that for the realization of contradiction, unity of the relational judgment is necessary; for this unity necessitates the eight unities and others. And it is not hidden from you that including what is other than the eight mentioned above under unity of predicate is possible, just as al-Farabi included some of them under others, as explained. For if it is said: 'The thing is predicated of itself just as it is predicated of its opposite, namely non-thing, so the two opposites, namely thing and non-thing, come together.' We say: Unity of the mode of predication is also a condition for the realization of contradiction. Predication of a thing of itself is primary predication, and of non-thing is conventional predication, so the condition of contradiction is not present. Primary predication is predicating a thing of itself, like predicating 'human' of itself, because it is the first predication, and conventional predication is predicating a thing of its individuals, like predicating 'human' of Zayd, Amr, Bakr, etc., and predicating 'animal' of human. In eight matters: so there is no contradiction when there is difference in subject, like 'Zayd is standing' and 'Amr is not standing'; in predicate, like 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is not sitting'; in place, like 'Zayd is sitting in the market' and 'Zayd is not sitting in the house'; in condition, like 'the body is pupil-dispersing on condition of being white' and 'the body is not pupil-dispersing on condition of being black'; in relation, like 'Zayd is a father of Amr' and 'Zayd is not a father of Bakr'; in part and whole, like 'the black person is black with respect to a part' and 'the black person is not black with respect to the whole, for his bones are white'; in potency and act, like 'wine is intoxicating in the jar potentially' and 'wine is not intoxicating in the jar actually'; in time, like 'Zayd is sleeping at night' and 'Zayd is not sleeping during the day'. These are eight unities mentioned by the earlier scholars for the realization of contradiction. As for the later scholars, some of them brought unity of condition, part and whole under unity of subject because of their difference through these unities, and unity of place, relation, potency and act under unity of predicate because of their difference through these unities. So three unities remained: unity of subject, unity of predicate, and unity of time. And some of them brought unity of time under unity of predicate because of its difference through difference in time, so they sufficed with two unities. And al-Farabi reduced them to unity of the relational judgment; for difference in anything of the subject, predicate, and what pertains to them necessitates difference in the relation.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the conditions for logical contradiction (tanāquḍ). The commentator first addresses a matn statement about the necessity of difference in quantity, quality, and modality. He explains that contradiction cannot occur between two particulars (juz'iyyatayn) because both can be true simultaneously, nor between two universals (kulliyyatayn) because both can be false. Thus, quantifiers must differ when propositions are quantified. The three required differences are detailed: quality (affirmative vs. negative), quantity (universal vs. particular), and modality (necessary vs. possible vs. actual). Examples are given for each, e.g., necessary propositions like 'Every human is a writer necessarily' and 'No human is a writer necessarily' can both be false, so modality must differ. The commentary then notes that mental and uncommon propositions are excluded from contradiction. Next, the condition of unity in all other respects is introduced, traditionally formulated as eight unities: subject, predicate, place, condition, relation, part/whole, potency/act, and time. Each is illustrated with examples from everyday language and fiqh-like contexts, e.g., difference in instrument ('shooter with arrow type') or condition ('pupil-dispersing if white vs. black'). The commentary also discusses later reductions of these eight unities: some scholars reduce to three (subject, predicate, time) or two (subject and predicate), and al-Farabi ultimately reduces all to unity of the relational judgment (waḥdat al-nisba al-ḥukmiyya), arguing that any difference in subject, predicate, or their attachments entails a difference in the relation expressed by the judgment. This passage thus provides a comprehensive treatment of the logical conditions governing contradiction, essential for properly evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Key Terms
تناقض
وحدة الموضوع
وحدة المحمول
وحدة الزمان
وحدة النسبة الحكمية

B) Two unities: subject and predicate
C) One unity: the relational judgment

Key Takeaway
The eight unities progressively reduce to the condition that the relational content of the judgment must be identical for a true contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Eight Unities Required for Logical Contradiction
Author explains the eight unities and their reductions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the eight unities is violated in a given pair of propositions to determine if they are genuine contradictories.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires more than opposite truth values—eight unities must be satisfied.

In logic, two propositions cannot be contradictory unless they agree in subject, predicate, place, condition, relation, part/whole, potentiality/actuality, and time. This lesson unpacks each unity with examples from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the base conditions
Recall that contradiction already requires difference in quality (affirmation/negation), quantity (universal/particular), and modality (necessity/possibility). The eight unities are beyond these.
The eight unities ensure the propositions are about the exact same situation.
2
Review each of the eight unities
Go through the list: subject, predicate, place, condition, relation, part/whole, potentiality/actuality, time. For each, the passage provides a pair of propositions that differ in that aspect and thus are not contradictory.
Example: difference in place: 'Zayd is sitting in the market' vs. 'Zayd is not sitting in the house' are not contradictory.
3
Examine the reductions by later scholars
Later scholars subsumed some unities under subject or predicate, leaving three or two. This shows the hierarchy of conditions.
Al-Farabi reduced all to the unity of the judgmental relation.
4
Apply the conditions to a new pair
Take a pair of propositions from Islamic jurisprudence, e.g., 'This transaction is valid with condition A' and 'This transaction is not valid with condition B'. Determine which unity is violated.

ثمانية. الآلة والاتحاد في ما عداها والله درّ المصنف حيث لم يحصر ما عداها في الثمانية، فلا یرد أنه لا تناقض باختلاف والعلة والمفعول به والتمييز مثلا نحو زيد رام بالسهم الأحمد نگرى وزيد ليس برام بالسهم Contradiction and its conditions, and unity beyond them. He said: 'and unity beyond them' means that for contradiction it is required that the two propositions be united beyond the three mentioned things, namely quality, quantity, and modality. They have restricted this unity within unity in eight matters. The instrument and unity beyond them—and well has the author done in not restricting what is beyond them to the eight, so that it is not objected that there is no contradiction with differences in cause, object, specification, for example: 'Zayd is a shooter of the arrow to Ahmad Nagri' and 'Zayd is not a shooter of the arrow to Aurangabadi', and 'The carpenter is a worker by the Sultan's command' and 'The carpenter is not a worker by another's command', and 'Zayd is a beater of Amr' and 'Zayd is not a beater of Bakr', and 'I have twenty dirhams' and 'I do not have twenty dinars'. So unity beyond the eight is necessary. Therefore some investigators said that for the realization of contradiction, unity of the judgmental relation is necessary; for this unity entails the eight unities and others. It is not hidden from you that including what is beyond the eight mentioned above in the unity of the predicate is possible, as al-Farabi included some of them in others, as explained. If it is said: A thing is predicated of itself just as it is predicated of its contradictory, namely non-thing, so two contradictories, namely thing and non-thing, are combined. We say: Unity of the mode of predication is also a condition for the realization of contradiction. Predication of a thing of itself is primary predication, and of non-thing is conventional predication, so the condition of contradiction is not found. Primary predication is predication of a thing of itself, like predication of man of itself, because it is primary predication. Conventional predication is predication of a thing of its individuals, like predication of man of Zayd, Amr, Bakr, etc., and predication of animal of man. There are eight matters: So there is no contradiction with difference of subject, e.g., 'Zayd is standing' and 'Amr is not standing'; predicate, e.g., 'Zayd stood' and 'Zayd is not sitting'; place, e.g., 'Zayd is sitting in the market' and 'Zayd is not sitting in the house'; condition, e.g., 'The body is seen by the eye with the condition of being white' and 'The body is not seen by the eye with the condition of being black'; relation, e.g., 'Zayd is a father of Amr' and 'Zayd is not a father of Bakr'; part and whole, e.g., 'The black person is black in part' and 'The black person is not black in whole, for his bone is white'; potentiality and actuality, e.g., 'Wine is intoxicating in the jar potentially' and 'Wine is not intoxicating in the jar actually'; time, e.g., 'Zayd is sleeping at night' and 'Zayd is not sleeping during the day'. These are eight unities mentioned by the earlier scholars for the realization of contradiction. As for the later scholars, some of them subsumed the unity of condition, whole, and part under the unity of subject, due to their difference without the unities; and the unity of place, relation, potentiality, and actuality under the unity of predicate, due to their difference without these unities. So three unities remained: unity of subject, unity of predicate, and unity of time. And some subsumed the unity of time under the unity of predicate, due to its difference with difference of time, so they sufficed with two unities. Al-Farabi reduced them to the unity of the judgmental relation, for difference in something of the subject, predicate, and what pertains to them entails difference of the relation. (Mukhtasar) This.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for logical contradiction (tanāquḍ) in propositions. It begins by explaining that the author's phrase 'and unity beyond them' means that for two propositions to be genuine contradictories, they must be identical in everything except the three previously mentioned aspects: quality (affirmation/negation), quantity (universal/particular), and modality (necessity/possibility etc.). The commentator praises the author for not limiting the required unities to exactly eight, because other differences can also prevent contradiction, such as difference in cause, object, or specification. Several examples illustrate: e.g., 'Zayd is a shooter of the arrow to Ahmad Nagri' vs. 'Zayd is not a shooter of the arrow to Aurangabadi' differ in the object (the person aimed at), so they are not contradictory even if the subject and predicate appear similar. Similarly, 'The carpenter is a worker by the Sultan's command' vs. 'not a worker by another's command' differ in the condition. The commentator then states that some scholars require 'unity of the judgmental relation' (waḥdat al-nisba al-ḥukmiyya) to encompass all necessary unities. Al-Farabi is said to have included some within others. Next, an objection is raised: A thing can be predicated of itself and also of its contradictory (non-thing), e.g., 'thing' is true of both 'thing' and 'non-thing', which would combine contradictories. The answer is that the mode of predication differs: predication of a thing of itself is 'primary predication' (ḥaml awwalī), while predication of a thing of its contradictory is 'conventional predication' (ḥaml muta'ārif). The condition of unity in the mode of predication is not met, so no contradiction arises. The passage then lists the eight classical unities necessary for contradiction: subject, predicate, place, condition, relation, part/whole, potentiality/actuality, and time. Each is exemplified with a pair of propositions that differ in that respect and hence are not contradictory. Later scholars reduced these by subsuming some under others: condition, part/whole under subject; place, relation, potentiality/actuality under predicate; time under predicate or kept separate. Al-Farabi ultimately reduced all to the unity of the judgmental relation.

النسبة الحكمية
