The Mode of Predication and the Unity of Judgmental Relation
Author answers an objection about predication and discusses reductions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a thing predicated of itself does not contradict a thing predicated of its opposite, and how al-Farabi reduced all unities to the judgmental relation.

Core Idea
The mode of predication is an additional condition for contradiction—primary vs. conventional predication differ.

An objection challenges that 'thing' can be predicated of itself and of 'non-thing', combining contradictories. The response reveals a hidden condition: the mode of predication must be the same. This lesson explores that and the ultimate reduction to the judgmental relation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection: 'Thing' is predicated of itself (e.g., 'Man is a thing') and also of its contradictory 'non-thing' (e.g., 'Nothing is a thing'). This seems to combine both a thing and its contradictory in one subject? Actually, the example in the passage is: 'A thing is predicated of itself and also of its contradictory (non-thing).' So if 'thing' is true of both, then 'thing' and 'non-thing' would both be true of something, which is impossible if 'thing' and 'non-thing' are contradictories. But the objector claims they are combined.
The objection points to a potential flaw in the definition of contradiction.
2
Analyze the response: primary vs. conventional predication
The answer is that predication of a thing of itself (e.g., 'Man is a man') is primary predication (haml awwali), while predication of a thing of its individuals (e.g., 'Man is Zayd') or of non-thing is conventional predication (haml muta'ārif). These are different modes, so the condition of unity in the mode of predication fails, and no contradiction arises.
Primary predication: the predicate is the same essence as the subject. Conventional: predicate is broader or different.
3
Study al-Farabi's reduction to the judgmental relation
Al-Farabi reduced all unities to the unity of the judgmental relation (nisba hukmiyya). This is the relation between subject and predicate. Any difference in subject, predicate, or their accessories changes the relation, so if the relation is the same, all unities are satisfied.
This is the most comprehensive condition.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the propositions 'Allah is knowing' and 'Allah is not knowing'. These appear contradictory, but only if the mode of predication is the same. In Kalam, the attribute of knowledge is not identical to the essence (primary) but is an attribute (conventional). This nuance prevents false contradictions.

Source Text

التناقض وشرائطه والاتحاد فيما عداها. قوله والاتحاد فيما عداها أي يشترط في التناقض اتحاد القضيتين فيما عدا الأمور الثلاثة المذكورة أعني الكيف والكم والجهة، وقد ضبطوا هذا الاتحاد في ضمن الاتحاد في أمور ثمانية. الآلة والاتحاد في ما عداها والله درّ المصنف حيث لم يحصر ما عداها في الثمانية، فلا یرد أنه لا تناقض باختلاف والعلة والمفعول به والتمييز مثلا نحو زيد رام بالسهم الأحمد نگرى وزيد ليس برام بالسهم الاورنگ آبادي، والنحار عامل أي بأمر السلطان والنجار ليس بعامل أي لأمر غيره، وزيد ضارب أي لعمرو وزيد ليس بضارب أي لبكر، وعندي عشرون أي درهما وليس عندي عشرون أي دينارا، فلابد من الاتحاد في ماعدا الثمانية، ولذا قال بعض المحققين أنه لابد في تحقق التناقض من وحدة النسبة الحكمية؛ فإن هذه الوحدة مستلزمة الموحدات الثمانية وغيرها ولا يخفى عليك أن إدراج ماعدا الثمانية مما ذكر آنفا في وحدة المحمول ممكن، كما أدرج الفارابي بعضا منها في بعض، كما بين. فإن قيل: الشيء كما يحمل على نفسه كذلك يحمل على نقيضه أعني اللاشيء، فاجتمع النقيضان أعني الشيء واللاشيء. قلنا: اتحاد نحو الحمل أيضاً شرط في تحقق التناقض، وحمل الشيء على نفسه حمل أولي وعلى اللاشيء حمل متعارف فلم يوجد شرط التناقض والحمل الأولي حمل الشيء على نفسه كحمل الإنسان على نفسه؛ لأنه أول الحمل والحمل المتعارف حمل الشيء على أفراده كحمل الإنسان على زيد وعمرو وبكر وغير ذلك وحمل الحيوان على الإنسان. في أمور ثمانية فلا تناقض عند اختلاف الموضوع، نحو: زيد قائم وعمرو ليس بقائم، والمحمول، نحو: زيد قام، وزيد ليس بقاعد والمكان نحو زيد جالس أي في السوق وزيد ليس بجالس أي في الدار، والشرط، نحو: الجسم مفرق للبصر أي بشرط كونه أبيض والجسم ليس مفرق للبصر أي بشرط كونه أسود، والإضافة، نحو: زيد أب أي لعمرو وزيد ليس بأب أي لبكر ، والجزء والكل نحو الزنجي أسود أي بعضه والزنجي ليس بأسود أي كله؛ فإن عظمه أبيض والقوة والفعل نحو: الخمر مسكر في الدن أي بالقوة والخمر ليس بمسكر في الدن أي بالفعل، والزمان، نحو، زيد نائم أي في الليل وزيد ليس بنائم أي في النهار. فهذه ثمان وحدات ذكرها المتقدمون لتحقق التناقض. وأما المتأخرون فبعضهم أدرجوا وحدة الشرط والكل والجزء تحت وحدة الموضوع؛ لاختلافه بعدم الوحدات، ووحدة المكان والإضافة والقوة والفعل تحت وحدة المحمول؛ لاختلافه بعدم هذه الوحدات. فبقي ثلاث وحدات وحدة الموضوع ووحدة المحمول ووحدة الزمان، وبعضهم أدرجوا وحدة الزمان تحت وحدة المحمول؛ لاختلافه باختلاف الزمان فاكتفوا بوحدتين. وردها الفارابي إلى وحدة النسبة الحكمية؛ فإن اختلاف شيء من الموضوع والمحمول وما يتعلق بهما يستلزم اختلاف النسبة. (محصل) هذه
Contradiction and its conditions, and unity beyond them. He said: 'and unity beyond them' means that for contradiction it is required that the two propositions be united beyond the three mentioned things, namely quality, quantity, and modality. They have restricted this unity within unity in eight matters. The instrument and unity beyond them—and well has the author done in not restricting what is beyond them to the eight, so that it is not objected that there is no contradiction with differences in cause, object, specification, for example: 'Zayd is a shooter of the arrow to Ahmad Nagri' and 'Zayd is not a shooter of the arrow to Aurangabadi', and 'The carpenter is a worker by the Sultan's command' and 'The carpenter is not a worker by another's command', and 'Zayd is a beater of Amr' and 'Zayd is not a beater of Bakr', and 'I have twenty dirhams' and 'I do not have twenty dinars'. So unity beyond the eight is necessary. Therefore some investigators said that for the realization of contradiction, unity of the judgmental relation is necessary; for this unity entails the eight unities and others. It is not hidden from you that including what is beyond the eight mentioned above in the unity of the predicate is possible, as al-Farabi included some of them in others, as explained. If it is said: A thing is predicated of itself just as it is predicated of its contradictory, namely non-thing, so two contradictories, namely thing and non-thing, are combined. We say: Unity of the mode of predication is also a condition for the realization of contradiction. Predication of a thing of itself is primary predication, and of non-thing is conventional predication, so the condition of contradiction is not found. Primary predication is predication of a thing of itself, like predication of man of itself, because it is primary predication. Conventional predication is predication of a thing of its individuals, like predication of man of Zayd, Amr, Bakr, etc., and predication of animal of man. There are eight matters: So there is no contradiction with difference of subject, e.g., 'Zayd is standing' and 'Amr is not standing'; predicate, e.g., 'Zayd stood' and 'Zayd is not sitting'; place, e.g., 'Zayd is sitting in the market' and 'Zayd is not sitting in the house'; condition, e.g., 'The body is seen by the eye with the condition of being white' and 'The body is not seen by the eye with the condition of being black'; relation, e.g., 'Zayd is a father of Amr' and 'Zayd is not a father of Bakr'; part and whole, e.g., 'The black person is black in part' and 'The black person is not black in whole, for his bone is white'; potentiality and actuality, e.g., 'Wine is intoxicating in the jar potentially' and 'Wine is not intoxicating in the jar actually'; time, e.g., 'Zayd is sleeping at night' and 'Zayd is not sleeping during the day'. These are eight unities mentioned by the earlier scholars for the realization of contradiction. As for the later scholars, some of them subsumed the unity of condition, whole, and part under the unity of subject, due to their difference without the unities; and the unity of place, relation, potentiality, and actuality under the unity of predicate, due to their difference without these unities. So three unities remained: unity of subject, unity of predicate, and unity of time. And some subsumed the unity of time under the unity of predicate, due to its difference with difference of time, so they sufficed with two unities. Al-Farabi reduced them to the unity of the judgmental relation, for difference in something of the subject, predicate, and what pertains to them entails difference of the relation. (Mukhtasar) This.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for logical contradiction (tanāquḍ) in propositions. It begins by explaining that the author's phrase 'and unity beyond them' means that for two propositions to be genuine contradictories, they must be identical in everything except the three previously mentioned aspects: quality (affirmation/negation), quantity (universal/particular), and modality (necessity/possibility etc.). The commentator praises the author for not limiting the required unities to exactly eight, because other differences can also prevent contradiction, such as difference in cause, object, or specification. Several examples illustrate: e.g., 'Zayd is a shooter of the arrow to Ahmad Nagri' vs. 'Zayd is not a shooter of the arrow to Aurangabadi' differ in the object (the person aimed at), so they are not contradictory even if the subject and predicate appear similar. Similarly, 'The carpenter is a worker by the Sultan's command' vs. 'not a worker by another's command' differ in the condition. The commentator then states that some scholars require 'unity of the judgmental relation' (waḥdat al-nisba al-ḥukmiyya) to encompass all necessary unities. Al-Farabi is said to have included some within others. Next, an objection is raised: A thing can be predicated of itself and also of its contradictory (non-thing), e.g., 'thing' is true of both 'thing' and 'non-thing', which would combine contradictories. The answer is that the mode of predication differs: predication of a thing of itself is 'primary predication' (ḥaml awwalī), while predication of a thing of its contradictory is 'conventional predication' (ḥaml muta'ārif). The condition of unity in the mode of predication is not met, so no contradiction arises. The passage then lists the eight classical unities necessary for contradiction: subject, predicate, place, condition, relation, part/whole, potentiality/actuality, and time. Each is exemplified with a pair of propositions that differ in that respect and hence are not contradictory. Later scholars reduced these by subsuming some under others: condition, part/whole under subject; place, relation, potentiality/actuality under predicate; time under predicate or kept separate. Al-Farabi ultimately reduced all to the unity of the judgmental relation.

Key Terms
تناقض
حمل أولي
حمل متعارف
النسبة الحكمية

Key Takeaway
The mode of predication is a crucial condition for contradiction, and all unities can be reduced to the unity of the judgmental relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Contradictories of Simple Modal Propositions
Author defines contradictories of modal propositions and defends the removal rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the contradictory of any simple modal proposition (necessary, perpetual, conditional) using the rule of removal and resolve objections to the definition.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a modal proposition is the removal of its modality, not the negation of its content.

This lesson unpacks the definition of contradictories for modal propositions. The authors establish that the contradictory of a necessary proposition is the general possible, and of a perpetual is the general absolute. They also defend the principle that a contradictory is a 'removal' by addressing an objection about existence and non-existence. By the end, you will be able to apply this rule and understand its foundation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modality
Look at the original proposition: is it necessary (ḍarūriyya), perpetual (dā'ima), or conditional (mashrūṭa)? The contradictory depends on the modality.
2
Apply the removal rule
Remove the modality: for necessity, replace with possibility of the opposite; for perpetuity, replace with its negation, which entails actuality of the opposite.
Removal (raf') is the key concept.
3
Verify the eight unities
Ensure the contradictory proposition shares the same subject, predicate, etc., as required for a proper contradiction.
Refer to the poem summarizing the eight conditions.
4
Understand the objection
If 'removal' defines contradictory, then existence (removal of non-existence) is not explicitly a removal, yet it is a contradictory. This challenges the definition.
The objection is raised and answered.
5
Grasp the resolution
Removal includes both explicit (like negation of modality) and implicit (like existence being the removal of non-existence). Thus the definition holds.
This broadens the understanding of 'removal'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقائض الموجهات البسيطة فالنقيض للضرورية الممكنة العامة وللدائمة المطلقة العامة، وللمشروطة العامة الحينية الممكنة قال قائلهم شعر: در تناقض هشت وحدت شرط دان وحدت موضوع و محمول و مکان وحدت شرط و اضافت جزء و کل قوت و فعل ست در آخر زمان قوله: فالنقيض للضرورية: اعلم أن نقيض كل شيء ،رفعه، فنقيض القضية التي حكم فيها بضرورة الإيجاب والسلب هو قضية حكم فيها بسلب تلك الضرورة، وسلب كل ضرورة هو عين إمكان الطرف المقابل، فنقيضُ ضرورة الإيجاب هو إمكان السلب ونقيض ضرورة السلب هو إمكان الإيجاب ونقيض الدوام هو سلب الدوام، وقد عرفت أنه يلزمه فعلية الطرف المقابل، فرفع دوام الإيجاب يلزمه فعلية السلب وسلب دوام السلب يلزمه فعلية الإيجاب، فالممكنة العامة نقيض صريح للضرورية المطلقة، والمطلقة العامة لازمة لنقيض الدائمة المطلقة، نقيض كل شيء رفعه واعترض عليه بأن العدم نقيض الوجود، وقد تقرر عندهم أن التناقض من الطرفين، فثبت أن الوجود نقيض العدم مع أنه ليس ،رفعه، فكيف يصح أن نقيض كل شيء رفعه؟ بل لزم منهما شيء آخر وهو أن رفع العدم رفع العدم أيضاً نقيض له، فللعدم نقيضان: الوجود وسلب العدم، وقد تقرر عندهم أن النقيض لكل شيء واحد والجواب أن المراد من الرفع أعم من الصريحي والضمني والوجود وإن لم يكن رفعا للعدم صريحا لكنه رفعه ضمنا وسلب السلب ليس نقيضا مغايرا للوجود بل هما شيء واحد في الحقيقة ولا فرق بينهما بحسب المصداق. فتدبر . ( إسماعيل) يلزمه فعلية الطرف المقابل واعلم أن بين دوام النسبة وبين فعلية الطرف المقابل لها ملازمة؛ لأنها لازم عام، فلا يرد أن النقيض الصريح للدوام هو سلب الدوام وفعلية الطرف المقابل لازم لسلب الدوام على ما قلتم ووجود اللازم لا يستلزم وجود الملزوم، ففعلية الطرف المقابل لا يستلزم سلب الدوام فكيف يكون فعلية الطرف المقابل نقيضا للدوام. نقيض صريح نحو كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة ونقيضه بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان بالإمكان العام ونحو: لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة ونقيضه بعض الإنسان حجر بالإمكان العام.
The contradictories of simple modals. So the contradictory of the necessary is the general possible, and of the perpetual is the general absolute, and of the general conditional temporal is the possible. Their poet said in verse: 'In contradiction, know eight unities: unity of subject, predicate, and place; unity of condition, relation, part and whole; potentiality and act; lastly time.' He said: 'So the contradictory of the necessary': Know that the contradictory of everything is its removal. So the contradictory of a proposition in which necessity of affirmation or negation is judged is a proposition in which the negation of that necessity is judged. The negation of every necessity is exactly the possibility of the opposite side. So the contradictory of necessity of affirmation is possibility of negation, and the contradictory of necessity of negation is possibility of affirmation. The contradictory of perpetuity is the negation of perpetuity, and you have known that it entails the actuality of the opposite side. So the removal of perpetuity of affirmation entails actuality of negation, and the negation of perpetuity of negation entails actuality of affirmation. So the general possible is an explicit contradictory of the absolute necessary, and the general absolute is a necessary consequent to the contradictory of the absolute perpetual. An objection was made against 'the contradictory of every thing is its removal': Non-existence is the contradictory of existence, and it is established among them that contradiction is between two sides. So it is established that existence is a contradictory of non-existence, while it is not its removal. So how is it correct that the contradictory of every thing is its removal? Rather, something else follows from them: that the removal of non-existence is also a contradictory of non-existence. So non-existence has two contradictories: existence and the removal of non-existence. But it is established among them that every thing has only one contradictory. The answer: What is meant by 'removal' is more general than explicit and implicit. Existence, even if not an explicit removal of non-existence, is its implicit removal. And the removal of negation is not a contradictory different from existence; rather, they are one thing in reality, with no difference between them in terms of reference. So ponder. (Isma'il) 'It entails the actuality of the opposite side.' And know that between the perpetuity of the relation and the actuality of its opposite side there is a necessary concomitance, because it is a general necessary consequence. So it is not objected that the explicit contradictory of perpetuity is the negation of perpetuity, and the actuality of the opposite side is a necessary consequence of the negation of perpetuity according to your statement, and the existence of the necessary consequence does not entail the existence of the antecedent. So the actuality of the opposite side does not entail the negation of perpetuity. So how can the actuality of the opposite side be a contradictory of perpetuity? An explicit contradictory: e.g., 'Every human is an animal necessarily' and its contradictory 'Some human is not an animal with general possibility'; and 'No human is a stone necessarily' and its contradictory 'Some human is a stone with general possibility.'
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage defines the contradictories of simple modal propositions. It begins by stating that the contradictory of a necessary proposition is a general possible proposition, the contradictory of a perpetual proposition is a general absolute, and the contradictory of a general conditional temporal is a possible proposition. A Persian verse is quoted summarizing the eight unities of contradiction from the previous discussion. Then the commentator explains the principle: the contradictory of any proposition is its 'removal' (raf'). For a necessary proposition (e.g., 'Every human is an animal necessarily'), the contradictory is not the negation of the proposition itself (e.g., 'Some human is not an animal necessarily'), but rather the negation of the modality: 'Some human is not an animal possibly'. Because the necessity is removed, and the removal of necessity equals the possibility of the opposite. Thus the contradictory of 'necessarily A' is 'possibly not A'. Similarly, the contradictory of 'perpetually A' is 'non-perpetually A', which entails the actuality of the opposite. An objection is then raised: The definition 'the contradictory of every thing is its removal' seems problematic because existence is the contradictory of non-existence, yet existence is not the 'removal' of non-existence in an explicit sense. The answer clarifies that 'removal' is meant broadly, including implicit removal. Existence is an implicit removal of non-existence, and the 'removal of negation' is identical to existence in reality. Another objection is handled: If the contradictory of perpetuity is the negation of perpetuity, and the actuality of the opposite is a necessary consequence of that negation, then the actuality of the opposite cannot itself be the contradictory because the necessary consequence does not entail the antecedent. The response explains that the relation is one of necessary concomitance (mulāzama), so the actuality of the opposite is indeed the contradictory of the perpetuity because they are mutually exclusive and exhaustive under the same conditions. Finally, two examples of explicit contradictories are given: 'Every human is necessarily an animal' has its contradictory 'Some human is not an animal with general possibility'; 'No human is necessarily a stone' has its contradictory 'Some human is a stone with general possibility'.

Key Terms
نقيض
ضرورية
ممكنة عامة
دائمة

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a modal proposition is the removal of its modality, not the negation of its content; this is captured by the principle 'the contradictory of every thing is its removal'.

The Contradictory of the General Conditional Modal
Defines the contradictory of 'urfiyya 'amma as 'ayniyya mutlaqa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the contradictory of any 'urfiyya 'amma proposition correctly.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a general conditional is a particular negative existential modal.

The general conditional ('urfiyya 'amma) asserts that a predicate holds permanently as long as the subject has a certain attribute. Finding its contradictory requires negating that permanence and specifying the time condition. This lesson walks through that logical operation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of the 'urfiyya 'amma
Locate the subject, predicate, descriptive attribute, and the permanence judgment in the proposition.
2
Negate the permanence
The contradictory must deny that the predicate holds for all times the attribute is present. This yields a particular negative.
3
Specify the time condition
The negation becomes 'at some moment when the attribute is present, the predicate does not hold'. This is the 'ayniyya mutlaqa in negative quality.
4
Apply to a sample proposition
Use the example: 'Every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer' becomes 'Some writer does not move his fingers while he is actually a writer'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك لأن الحكم في العرفية العامة بدوام النسبة ما دام ذات الموضوع متصفةً بالوصف العنواني، فنقيضها الصريح هو سلب ذلك الدوام، ويلزمه وقوع الطرف المقابل في بعض أوقات الوصف العنواني، وهذا معنى الحينية المطلقة المخالفة للعرفية العامة في الكيف، فنقيض قولنا: "بالدوام كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا" قولنا: ليس بعض الكاتب متحرك الأصابع حين هو كاتب بالفعل، والمصنف لم يتعرض لبيان نقيض الوقتية والمنتشرة المطلقتين من البسائط؛ إذ لا يتعلق بذلك غرض فيما سيأتي من مباحث العكوس والأقيسة بخلاف باقي البسائط. فتأمل.
That is because the judgment in the general conditional is of the permanence of the relation as long as the subject is qualified by the descriptive attribute, so its explicit contradictory is the negation of that permanence, and it entails the occurrence of the opposite side in some moments of the descriptive attribute, and this is the meaning of the absolute existential which differs from the general conditional in quality. So the contradictory of our statement: 'Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, by permanence' is our statement: 'Some writer is not moving his fingers while he is actually a writer.' And the author did not address the contradictory of the absolute temporal and spread [modals] among the simples, because no purpose attaches to that in what follows concerning the topics of converses and syllogisms, unlike the rest of the simples. So reflect.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage defines the contradictory (naqid) of the general conditional ('urfiyya 'amma) modal proposition, which is a type of simple modal. The author begins by explaining the nature of the judgment in an 'urfiyya 'amma: it asserts that the relation (here, moving fingers) holds for the subject (writer) with permanence (dawam) as long as the subject is actually qualified by the descriptive attribute (being a writer). The explicit contradictory of this is the negation of that permanence. Specifically, it entails that the opposite side (not moving fingers) occurs at some time during the subject's possession of the attribute. This is precisely the meaning of the absolute existential ('ayniyya mutlaqa), which differs from the 'urfiyya 'amma in quality (the 'urfiyya is affirmative universal, the 'ayniyya is negative particular). For example, the contradictory of 'Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, by permanence' is 'Some writer is not moving his fingers while he is actually a writer'. The passage then notes that the author (al-musannif) did not address the contradictories of two other simple modals: the absolute temporal (waqtiyya) and the absolute spread (muntashira). The reason given is that no purpose is served by them in the later discussions of converses (aks) and syllogisms (aqyisa), unlike the other simples. The student is advised to reflect (tammal) on this omission. The explanation is consistent with the principle that the contradictory of any proposition is its negation; for modals with temporal restrictions, the negation produces a corresponding possibility modal.

Key Terms
العرفية العامة (al-'urfiyya al-'amma): A simple modal proposition that asserts the predicate holds for the subject with permanence as long as the subject is qualified by the descriptive attribute. [Note: Stable term in Islamic logic; appears as a standard modal type.]
الحينية المطلقة (al-'ayniyya al-mutlaqa): A proposition that asserts the predicate holds actually at some moment the subject is qualified by the attribute; here it is the contradictory of the general conditional. [Note: Also called 'ayniyya mutlaqa; consistent usage.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the contradictory of the proposition 'Every mu'min is truthful as long as he is a mu'min, by permanence'?
A) No mu'min is truthful as long as he is a mu'min.
B) Some mu'min is not truthful while he is actually a mu'min.
C) Some mu'min is truthful at all times.
D) Every mu'min is not truthful at some time.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a general conditional (urfiyya 'amma) is the absolute existential particular negative (ayniyya mutlaqa saliba juz'iyya).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Identify the quality and quantity changes needed to produce the contradictory of an 'urfiyya 'amma, and explain why it is not simply a universal negative.

Why the Author Omitted Temporal and Spread Modals
Notes the omission of two simple modals from the discussion of contradictories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author did not treat the contradictories of waqtiyya and muntashira, based on their irrelevance to later topics.

Core Idea
Not all logical possibilities are pursued; only those needed for later discussions are addressed.

After defining the contradictory of the general conditional, the text notes that the author intentionally omitted the contradictories of two other simple modals: the temporal (waqtiyya) and the spread (muntashira). The reason is pragmatic: they are not needed for the upcoming topics of converses and syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two omitted modals
Recognise al-waqtiyya (temporal) and al-muntashira (spread). Understand that they restrict judgment to a specific or indefinite time.
2
Understand the author's rationale
The author states that no purpose (gharad) attaches to them in the discussions of converses (aks) and syllogisms (aqyisa).
3
Compare with the included simples
The 'urfiyya 'amma and other simples are needed because they appear in syllogistic moods and conversion rules, unlike waqtiyya and muntashira.
4
Reflect on the principle of selection
This illustrates that logical textbooks are not exhaustive but purposive; topics are chosen for utility in further argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك لأن الحكم في العرفية العامة بدوام النسبة ما دام ذات الموضوع متصفةً بالوصف العنواني، فنقيضها الصريح هو سلب ذلك الدوام، ويلزمه وقوع الطرف المقابل في بعض أوقات الوصف العنواني، وهذا معنى الحينية المطلقة المخالفة للعرفية العامة في الكيف، فنقيض قولنا: "بالدوام كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا" قولنا: ليس بعض الكاتب متحرك الأصابع حين هو كاتب بالفعل، والمصنف لم يتعرض لبيان نقيض الوقتية والمنتشرة المطلقتين من البسائط؛ إذ لا يتعلق بذلك غرض فيما سيأتي من مباحث العكوس والأقيسة بخلاف باقي البسائط. فتأمل.
That is because the judgment in the general conditional is of the permanence of the relation as long as the subject is qualified by the descriptive attribute, so its explicit contradictory is the negation of that permanence, and it entails the occurrence of the opposite side in some moments of the descriptive attribute, and this is the meaning of the absolute existential which differs from the general conditional in quality. So the contradictory of our statement: 'Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, by permanence' is our statement: 'Some writer is not moving his fingers while he is actually a writer.' And the author did not address the contradictory of the absolute temporal and spread [modals] among the simples, because no purpose attaches to that in what follows concerning the topics of converses and syllogisms, unlike the rest of the simples. So reflect.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage defines the contradictory (naqid) of the general conditional ('urfiyya 'amma) modal proposition, which is a type of simple modal. The author begins by explaining the nature of the judgment in an 'urfiyya 'amma: it asserts that the relation (here, moving fingers) holds for the subject (writer) with permanence (dawam) as long as the subject is actually qualified by the descriptive attribute (being a writer). The explicit contradictory of this is the negation of that permanence. Specifically, it entails that the opposite side (not moving fingers) occurs at some time during the subject's possession of the attribute. This is precisely the meaning of the absolute existential ('ayniyya mutlaqa), which differs from the 'urfiyya 'amma in quality (the 'urfiyya is affirmative universal, the 'ayniyya is negative particular). For example, the contradictory of 'Every writer is moving his fingers as long as he is a writer, by permanence' is 'Some writer is not moving his fingers while he is actually a writer'. The passage then notes that the author (al-musannif) did not address the contradictories of two other simple modals: the absolute temporal (waqtiyya) and the absolute spread (muntashira). The reason given is that no purpose is served by them in the later discussions of converses (aks) and syllogisms (aqyisa), unlike the other simples. The student is advised to reflect (tammal) on this omission. The explanation is consistent with the principle that the contradictory of any proposition is its negation; for modals with temporal restrictions, the negation produces a corresponding possibility modal.

Key Terms
الوقتية (al-waqtiyya): A modal that restricts judgment to a specific time; not treated further. [Note: Standard term; no shift.]
المنتشرة (al-muntashira): A modal that spreads over an indefinite time; not treated further. [Note: Standard term; no shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) They had already been covered earlier.

Key Takeaway
The author omitted the contradictories of temporal and spread modals because they are not needed for later topics, showing the practical orientation of the text.

The Contradictory of Compound Modal Propositions
Defines the contradictory of compound modals as a disjunction between contradictories of parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contradictory of any compound modal proposition using the disjunctive form with man' al-khulu.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a compound modal is a disjunction, not a simple negation.

This passage defines how to find the contradictory (naqid) of compound modal propositions—propositions that combine two simple modals. Unlike simple propositions, the contradictory is not a single negation but a disjunction that prevents both parts from being false. This is crucial for understanding logical opposition in modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound proposition
Locate the two simple modals combined in the compound, e.g., 'necessarily as long as…' and 'not always'.
The compound is often signaled by a conjunction like 'but' or 'while'.
2
Extract contradictories of each part
For each simple modal, find its contradictory using known rules (e.g., necessary becomes possible not).
The contradictory of an absolute universal affirmative is a particular negative, etc.
3
Form a disjunction with man' al-khulu
Combine the two contradictories with 'either…or…' ensuring at least one must be true (preventing vacancy).
Man' al-khulu means both cannot be false simultaneously.
4
Verify with an example
Apply the method to the provided example or a similar one from fiqh to check correctness.
The example in the passage shows the transformation clearly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمركبة المفهوم المردّد بين نقيضي الجزئيين. قد علمت أن نقيض كل شيء رفعه، فاعلم أن رفع المركب إنما يكون برفع أحد جزئيه لا على التعيين بل على سبيل منع الخلو؛ إذ يجوز أن يكون برفع كلا الجزئين، فنقيض القضية المركبة نقيض أحد جزئيه على سبيل منع الخلو، فنقيض قولنا: كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع بالضرورة ما دام كاتبا لا دائما أي لا شيء من الكاتب بمتحرك الأصابع بالفعل قضية منفصلة مانعة الخلو، هي: قولنا: إما بعض الكاتب ليس بمتحرك الأصابع بالإمكان حين هو كاتب وإما بعض الكاتب متحرك الأصابع دائما. وأنت بعد اطلاعك على حقائق المركبات ونقائض البسائط تظهر لك نقائض المركبات الباقية.
And for the compound [modal], the concept is disjunctive between the contradictories of the two parts. You have known that the contradictory of everything is its negation. Know that the negation of a compound is only by negating one of its two parts, not determinately but by way of preventing vacancy (man' al-khulu), since it is possible that it be by negating both parts. So the contradictory of a compound proposition is the contradictory of one of its two parts by way of preventing vacancy. Thus the contradictory of our statement: 'Every writer is moving his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer, but not always' i.e., 'No writer is moving his fingers actually' is a disjunctive proposition that prevents vacancy, namely our statement: 'Either some writer is not moving his fingers possibly while he is a writer, or some writer is moving his fingers always.' And after you have knowledge of the realities of compounds and the contradictories of simples, the contradictories of the remaining compounds become clear to you.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage defines the contradictory (naqid) of compound modal propositions (al-murakkaba). A compound modal is formed by combining two simple modals, e.g., 'necessarily as long as he is a writer, but not always.' The key principle is that the contradictory of a compound is not a simple negation of the whole, but a disjunction between the contradictories of its two parts, with the condition of preventing vacancy (man' al-khulu). This means that at least one of the two contradictories must be true, but not necessarily both. The author illustrates with an example: the proposition 'Every writer is moving his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer, but not always' (which translates to 'No writer is moving his fingers actually') is a compound of two parts: (1) 'Every writer is moving his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer' (a special conditional) and (2) 'No writer is moving his fingers actually' (a universal negative absolute). The contradictory of the first part is the possible particular negative (some writer is not moving his fingers possibly while he is a writer), and the contradictory of the second part is the universal affirmative absolute (every writer is moving his fingers always). The contradictory of the compound is the disjunction 'Either some writer is not moving his fingers possibly while he is a writer, or some writer is moving his fingers always.' This ensures that at least one of the two original parts is false. The passage concludes that after understanding the nature of compounds and the contradictories of simples, one can derive the contradictories of all other compound modals. An example from Islamic sciences: for the compound 'Every prayer is valid necessarily as long as it is performed properly, but not always' (i.e., some prayers are invalid), the contradictory is the disjunction: 'Either some prayer is not valid possibly while performed properly, or some prayer is valid always.'

Key Terms
المركبة
منع الخلو
المفهوم المردد

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a compound modal proposition is a disjunction between the contradictories of its two parts, with the condition that at least one of the contradictories must be true (man' al-khulu).

The Contradictory of Composite Modal Propositions
Defines the contradictory of a composite modal proposition as a disjunction on man' al-khuluww.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contradictory of any composite modal proposition as a disjunction on man' al-khuluww.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a composite is not a simple negation but a disjunction that prevents vacuity.

You already know that the contradictory of a simple proposition is its direct negation. But what about composite modal propositions, like conditionals or composites with modal qualifiers? The author of this passage explains that the contradictory of such a composite is itself a disjunction — specifically, a disjunction of the contradictories of its parts, with the condition that at least one of those parts is negated (man' al-khuluww). This is a key technique for handling complex modal statements in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of 'contradictory'
The author begins by reminding us that the contradictory of any proposition is its negation (raf'). For a simple proposition, this is straightforward: 'Every S is P' has the contradictory 'Some S is not P'.
This is the foundation for understanding the composite case.
2
Apply negation to a composite proposition
For a composite proposition (e.g., a conditional or a modal composite), the negation cannot simply be the negation of the whole. Instead, it must be a disjunction that negates one of its parts, not determinately, but on the basis of man' al-khuluww (at least one part is negated, possibly both).
This is the core rule: the contradictory of a composite is a disjunctive proposition with man' al-khuluww.
3
Identify the parts of the given composite
In the example, the composite is 'Every writer is a finger-mover necessarily as long as he is a writer, not always.' This is a composite of two propositions: (1) 'Every writer is a finger-mover necessarily as long as he is a writer' and (2) 'Every writer is not a finger-mover always' (or 'No writer is a finger-mover always').
The composite is formed by combining two modal statements.
4
Form the disjunctive contradictory
The contradictory is then a disjunction of the contradictories of each part, with man' al-khuluww. For part (1), its contradictory is 'Some writer is not a finger-mover possibly when he is a writer.' For part (2), its contradictory is 'Some writer is a finger-mover always.' The disjunction 'Either ... or ...' with man' al-khuluww is the contradictory.
The two disjuncts cover the possibilities: either the first part is false (in the modal sense possible) or the second part is false (in the modal sense always), or both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التصديقات ١٢٤ نقائض الموجهات البسيطة وللمركبة المفهوم المردّد بين نقيضي الجزئيين قوله وللمركبة قد علمت أن نقيض كل شيء ،رفعه، فاعلم أن رفع المركب إنما يكون برفع أحد جزئيه لا على التعيين بل على سبيل منع الخلو؛ إذ يجوز أن يكون برفع كلا الجزئين، فنقيض القضية المركبة نقيض أحد جزئيه على سبيل منع الخلو، فنقيض قولنا: كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع بالضرورة ما دام كاتبا لا دائما أي لا شيء من الكاتب بمتحرك الأصابع بالفعل قضية منفصلة مانعة الخلو، هي :قولنا: إما بعض الكاتب ليس بمتحرك الأصابع بالإمكان حين هو كاتب وإما بعض الكاتب متحرك الأصابع، دائما وأنت بعد اطلاعك
The contradictories of simple modal propositions. And for the composite [proposition], the concept is the alternation between the contradictories of the two parts. [The commentator says:] His saying 'and for the composite' – you have known that the contradictory of everything is its negation. Know that the negation of the composite is only by negating one of its parts, not determinately but on the basis of preventing vacuity (man' al-khuluww), since it is permissible that it be by negating both parts. So the contradictory of the composite proposition is the contradictory of one of its parts on the basis of preventing vacuity. For example, the contradictory of our saying: 'Every writer is a finger-mover necessarily as long as he is a writer, not always' i.e. 'No writer is actually a finger-mover' is a disjunctive proposition that prevents vacuity, which is our saying: 'Either some writer is not a finger-mover possibly when he is a writer, or some writer is a finger-mover always.' And after you have knowledge...

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of the contradictory (naqīḍ) for composite modal propositions. The author begins by restating the general principle that the contradictory of any proposition is its negation. For a composite proposition, negation must be of one of its parts, not determinately, but on the basis of man' al-khuluww (prevention of emptiness), meaning that at least one of the parts must be negated, and it could be both. Consequently, the contradictory of a composite proposition is the contradictory of one of its parts on that basis. A concrete example is provided: the proposition 'Every writer is a finger-mover necessarily as long as he is a writer, not always' is a composite. Its contradictory is not a simple categorical but a disjunctive (munfaṣila) that prevents vacuity, namely 'Either some writer is not a finger-mover possibly when he is a writer, or some writer is a finger-mover always.' The commentator then notes that after knowing the natures of composites, one can derive the contradictories of the remaining composites.

Implicit Contradictory of Necessity: From Necessity to Possibility
Author explains that the contradictory of necessary and conditional propositions is implied, even if not explicitly stated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the specific type of possibility that contradicts a given modal necessary proposition (essential, descriptive, or temporal).

Core Idea
The contradictory of necessity is always the corresponding possibility, and it is logically implied even when not stated.

In this short passage, the author draws a comprehensive logical principle: the contradictory of any necessary proposition is not simply 'not necessary' but a specific modal possibility. He breaks down necessity into three types and shows how each directly implies its own contradictory. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to handle modal negations in theological and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Extract the general rule
Identify the statement: 'the contradictory of necessity is possibility.' This is the master key for negating modal necessity propositions.
2
Distinguish the three types of necessity
List the three types: essential (dhātiyya), descriptive (waṣfiyya), and temporal (waqtiyya). For each, note the corresponding possibility: essential possibility, descriptive possibility, and temporal possibility (further divided into definite and diffused).
The correspondence is one-to-one: the type of necessity determines the type of its contradictory possibility.
3
Understand the implicit nature (iltizām)
The author says the contradictory is 'mentioned implicitly' (iltizāman). This means that when a necessary proposition is asserted, its contradictory is logically entailed even if not verbally expressed. Practice mentally deriving the contradictory for a given necessary proposition.
This is an immediate inference of contradiction.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the essential necessary proposition: 'Allah is necessarily existent' (wājib al-wujūd li-dhātihi). Its contradictory is 'Allah is possibly non-existent' (mumkin al-`adam). Though this contradictory is false in reality, the logical structure remains valid as a rule for immediate inference.
The example illustrates that the rule concerns logical form, not factual truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتأمل أمر شامل لنقيض المذكور التزاما؛ لأنه لما بين نقيض الضرورية والمشروطة العامة علم أن نقيض الضرورة الإمكان، فإن كانت الضرورة ذاتية فنقيضها الإمكان الذاتي وإن كانت وصفية فالإمكان الوصفي، وإن كانت وقتية فالإمكان الوقتي معينا كان أو منتشرا، فعلى هذا معنى قوله : " لم يتعرض" أي صراحة، إلا أن النقيض مذكور التزاما. فتأمل بالمذكور الالتزامي.
So consider a comprehensive matter regarding the contradictory of what was mentioned implicitly: because when he explained the contradictory of the necessary and the general conditional, it is known that the contradictory of necessity is possibility. If the necessity is essential, its contradictory is essential possibility; if it is descriptive, then descriptive possibility; if it is temporal, then temporal possibility, whether definite or diffused. Based on this, the meaning of his saying 'he did not mention' is explicitly, except that the contradictory is mentioned implicitly. So reflect on the implicit mention.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage expands on the previous discussion to draw a general principle about the contradictories of necessary and conditional propositions. The author notes that once one understands the contradictory of a necessary proposition (ḍarūriyya) and a general conditional (mashrūṭa 'āmma), it becomes clear that the contradictory of necessity (al-ḍarūra) is possibility (al-imkān). This rule applies to the different types of necessity: if the necessity is essential (dhātiyya), its contradictory is essential possibility; if it is descriptive (waṣfiyya), its contradictory is descriptive possibility; if it is temporal (waqtiyya), its contradictory is temporal possibility, whether the time is definite (mu'ayyan) or diffused (muntashir). The commentator then interprets the matn's phrase 'he did not mention' to mean that the contradictory is not explicitly stated but is implied (iltizāman). Finally, he instructs the reader to reflect on the implicit mention.

Key Terms
ضرورة (ḍarūra): necessity
إمكان (imkān): possibility
ذاتية (dhātiyya): essential
وصفية (waṣfiyya): descriptive
وقتية (waqtiyya): temporal
التزاماً (iltizāman): implicitly, by entailment

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a necessary proposition is always its corresponding possibility, and this relationship is logically implicit even if not verbally stated.

Contradictories of Composite Propositions
Author details the contradictory of the particular conditional and extends to 'urfiyya khassa and wujudiyya la-daruriyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contradictory of any composite proposition by applying the man' al-khuluww (disjunctive) principle.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a composite is a disjunction of the contradictories of its components.

In classical Islamic logic, composite propositions are built from simpler ones. To logically negate them, you cannot simply negate the whole—you must disjoin the negations of their parts. This lesson unpacks the method using the particular conditional, customary particular, and existential non-necessary as examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the composite and its components
Determine which two simpler propositions compose the composite. For example, the particular conditional (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa) is composed of a general conditional and an absolute general.
2
Find the contradictory of each component
Use standard rules: the contradictory of a general conditional (mashrūṭa 'āmma) is the possible temporal (ḥīniyya mumkina); of an absolute general (muṭlaqa 'āmma) is the absolute perpetual (dā'ima muṭlaqa).
These are standard contradictories from earlier sections.
3
Form the disjunction 'man' al-khuluww'
The contradictory of the whole is a disjunction (exclusive 'or') of the two contradictories, meaning at least one must hold. This is called 'man' al-khuluww' (preventing vacuity).
It is not a conjunction; the composite's contradictory is the alternating concept between the two.
4
Express as a single disjunctive proposition
Combine the two contradictories into one statement using 'either...or...' with man' al-khuluww. For example: 'Either some writer is not a finger-mover when he is a writer, or some writer is a finger-mover always.'
This disjunction is the exact contradictory of the original composite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كل كاتب إلخ فهذه مشروطة خاصة مركبة من المشروطة العامة والمطلقة العامة، فنقيضها هو نقيض إحدى هاتين القضيتين على سبيل منع الخلو، فنقيض المشروطة الحينية الممكنة ونقيض المطلقة العامة الدائمة المطلقة، فنقيض هذه المشروطة الخاصة هو المفهوم المردد بين أحد هذين النقيضين على سبيل منع الخلو. وأنت بعد اطلاعك إلخ: يعني أنه يظهر نقائض المركبات الباقية بأدنى تأمل بعد الاطلاع على حقائق المركبات ونقائض البسائط؛ فإنا إذا علمنا أن العرفية الخاصة الموجبة الكلية مركبة من عرفية عامة موجبة كلية ومطلقة عامة سالبة كلية ونقيض الأول السالبة الجزئية الحينية المطلقة، ونقيض الثاني الدائمة الموجبة الجزئية ظهر علينا أن نقيض العرفية الخاصة هو المفهوم المردد بين هذين النقيضين على سبيل منع الخلو، فنقيض قولنا بالدوام كل كاتب متحرك الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما أي لا شيء من الكاتب بمتحرك الأصابع بالفعل قضية مانعة الخلو هي قولنا: إما ليس بعض الكاتب يمتحرك الأصابع حين هو كاتب بالفعل وإما بعض الكاتب متحرك الأصابع دائما، وكذا نقيض الوجودية اللاضرورية كقولنا كل إنسان كاتب بالفعل لا بالضرورة أي لا شيء منه بكاتب بالإمكان العام القضية المنفصلة المرددة بين نقيضي المطلقة العامة والممكنة العامة على سبيل منع الخلو وهي قولنا: إما بعض الإنسان ليس كاتبا دائما وإما بعض الإنسان كاتب بالضرورة، وقس على هذا الوقتية والمنتشرة. إسماعيل)
'Every writer etc.' – this is a particular conditional (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa), composed of a general conditional and an absolute general proposition. Its contradictory is the contradictory of one of these two propositions on the basis of preventing vacuity. So the contradictory of the conditional temporal (ḥīniyya) is the possible, and the contradictory of the absolute general is the absolute perpetual. Therefore, the contradictory of this particular conditional is the concept alternating between one of these two contradictories on the basis of preventing vacuity. 'And after you have knowledge etc.' means that the contradictories of the remaining composites become apparent with minimal reflection after comprehending the natures of composites and the contradictories of simples. For if we know that the universal affirmative customary particular ('urfiyya khāṣṣa) is composed of a universal affirmative general customary and a universal negative absolute general, and the contradictory of the first is a particular negative absolute temporal (ḥīniyya), and the contradictory of the second is a particular affirmative perpetual, then it becomes clear to us that the contradictory of the particular customary is the concept alternating between these two contradictories on the basis of preventing vacuity. For example, the contradictory of our saying perpetually 'Every writer is a finger-mover as long as he is a writer, not always' i.e. 'No writer is actually a finger-mover' is a disjunctive proposition that prevents vacuity, which is our saying: 'Either some writer is not a finger-mover when he is actually a writer, or some writer is a finger-mover always.' Similarly, the contradictory of the existential non-necessary (wujūdiyya lā-ḍarūriyya) such as our saying 'Every human is actually a writer, not necessarily' i.e. 'None of him is a writer by general possibility' is the disjunctive proposition alternating between the contradictories of the absolute general and the general possible on the basis of preventing vacuity, which is our saying: 'Either some human is not a writer always, or some human is a writer necessarily.' Analogize to this the temporal and diffused ones. (Ismā'īl)
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed explanation of the contradictory of the particular conditional (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa) and extends the method to other composite propositions. The author identifies that the particular conditional is composed of a general conditional (mashrūṭa 'āmma) and an absolute general (muṭlaqa 'āmma). Its contradictory is the contradictory of one of these on man' al-khuluww, i.e., a disjunction between the contradictory of the general conditional (which is the possible (mumkina) temporal (ḥīniyya)) and the contradictory of the absolute general (which is the absolute perpetual (dā'ima muṭlaqa)). The key is that the contradictory is the concept alternating between these two contradictory propositions. The commentator then elaborates on the phrase 'after you have knowledge' from the matn, explaining that one can derive the contradictories of all remaining composites once the natures of composites and contradictories of simples are understood. Two further examples are given: (1) The 'urfiyya khāṣṣa (customary particular), which is composed of a universal affirmative general customary and a universal negative absolute general. Its contradictory is a disjunction between the particular negative absolute temporal and the particular affirmative perpetual. (2) The wujūdiyya lā-ḍarūriyya (existential non-necessary), whose contradictory is a disjunction between the contradictory of the absolute general (which is a particular negative perpetual) and the contradictory of the general possible (which is a particular affirmative necessary). The passage concludes by instructing the reader to apply analogical reasoning to temporal and diffused propositions.

مشروطة خاصة
عرفية خاصة
وجودية لاضرورية

To find the contradictory of a composite proposition, disjoin the contradictories of its simple components using the man' al-khuluww principle.

Contradictory of Particular Composite Propositions
Author explains the correct method for particular composite contradictories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the correct contradictory of a particular composite modal proposition by applying the per-individual alternation rule.

Core Idea
Particular composites need universal contradictory with per-individual alternation.

Finding the contradictory of a proposition is a key skill in logic, but particular composite propositions require special care. This lesson unpacks the precise method taught in the classical tradition: instead of simply flipping each part, you must take all individuals of the subject and alternate between the contradictories for each one. Let's see why this is necessary and how to do it correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of a particular composite
A particular composite proposition has a quantifier 'some' and a composite predicate (e.g., 'Some S is P in mode M, not in mode N'). Here the example is 'Some animal is actually a human, not always' – this is false because no animal is actually human.
2
Test the simple alternation method
One might think the contradictory is obtained by alternating between the contradictories of the two parts (the two universal propositions). The parts here are the temporal conditional and the absolute. Their contradictories are 'No animal is a human always' and 'Every animal is a human always', both false. So simple alternation fails.
The passage emphasizes that this method would give a false result if the original is false and both partial contradictories are also false.
3
Apply the correct method: universal quantification per individual
The contradictory of a particular is a universal. So we start with 'Every animal' and then for each individual animal we alternate between the contradictories of the two parts. This yields 'Every animal is either a human always or not a human always'.
This proposition is true, satisfying the requirement that the contradictory of a false proposition is true.
4
Express the result as a categorical with alternating predicate
The correct contradictory is a universal categorical proposition with an alternating predicate (murdada al-mahmul). In our example: 'Every animal is either always a human or not always a human.'
This form is called 'hamliyya murdada al-mahmul'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولكن في الجزئية بالنسبة إلى كل فرد فرد. على حقائق المركبات ونقائض البسائط تتمكن من استخراج تفاصيل نقائض المركبات: قوله: ولكن في الجزئية بالنسبة إلى كل فرد فرد يعني لا يكفي في أخذ نقيض القضية المركبة الجزئية الترديد بين نقيضي جزئيها وهما الكليتان إذ قد تكذب المركبة الجزئية، كقولنا: بعض الحيوان إنسان بالفعل لا دائما ويكذب كلا نقيضي جزئيها أيضاً وهما: قولنا: لا شيء من الحيوان بإنسان دائما وقولنا كل حيوان إنسان دائما، وحينئذ فطريق أخذ نقيض المركبة الجزئية أن يوضع أفراد الموضوع كلها ضرورة أن نقيض الجزئية هي الكلية ثم تردد بين نقيضي الجزئين بالنسبة إلى كل واحد من تلك الأفراد فيقال في المثال المذكور كل حيوان إما إنسان دائما أو ليس بإنسان دائما وحينئذٍ فيصدق النقيض وهو قضية حملية مرددة المحمول. فقوله بالنسبة إلى كل فرد فرد أي أفراد الموضوع.
But in the particular [proposition], it is with respect to each individual. [The commentator says:] 'After understanding the realities of composites and the contradictories of simples, you can derive the details of the contradictories of composites. His saying: 'But in the particular, with respect to each individual' means that it is not sufficient in taking the contradictory of a particular composite proposition to merely alternate between the contradictories of its two parts, which are the two universal propositions, because the particular composite may be false. For example, our saying: 'Some animal is actually a human, not always' is false, and both of its two parts' contradictories are also false, namely our saying: 'No animal is a human always' and our saying: 'Every animal is a human always.' In that case, the way to take the contradictory of the particular composite is to set all the individuals of the subject, because the contradictory of a particular is a universal, then alternate between the contradictories of the two parts with respect to each one of those individuals. So in the mentioned example, one says: 'Every animal is either a human always or not a human always.' Then the contradictory is true, and it is a categorical proposition with an alternating predicate. So his saying 'with respect to each individual' means the individuals of the subject.'
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage addresses a special nuance regarding the contradictory of particular composite propositions (al-murakkaba al-juz'iyya). The commentator explains that simply alternating between the two contradictories of the two parts (which are universal) is not sufficient when the composite is particular. He provides an example: 'Some animal is actually a human, not always' is a false particular composite. Its two parts are presumably a temporal conditional and an absolute. The contradictories of those parts are (1) 'No animal is a human always' and (2) 'Every animal is a human always' – both false. Therefore, a different approach is needed: since the contradictory of a particular proposition is a universal, we must consider all individuals of the subject. For each individual, we alternate between the contradictories of the two parts. The result is a universal categorical proposition with an alternating predicate, e.g., 'Every animal is either a human always or not a human always.' This ensures the contradictory is true. The commentator concludes that the phrase 'with respect to each individual' refers to the individuals of the subject.

Key Terms
جزئية (juz'iyya): particular proposition
كل فرد فرد (kull fard fard): each individual of the subject

When a Disjunction Acts as a Contradictory: Metaphorical Usage in Islamic Logic
Author raises and resolves objection about disjunction as contradictory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a strict contradictory and a logically equivalent proposition, and explain why an alternating proposition may be metaphorically called a contradictory in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
A disjunction can serve as a contradictory by logical equivalence, not by strict opposition.

In this passage, the author deals with two objections about the nature of contradiction for composite propositions. The first objection arises when a disjunctive concept is mistakenly taken as the direct contradictory of a particular affirmative, leading to absurd consequences. The second, more refined objection points out that both the alternating proposition and the original affirmative are affirmative, violating the condition of contradiction. The author resolves this by explaining that the term 'contradictory' is used metaphorically—the alternating proposition is not the strict contradictory but is logically equivalent (musāwiya) to it. Understanding this distinction is crucial for accurate logical analysis in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objections in the passage
Read the Arabic text and locate where the author introduces each objection. The first is about 'the particular composite' and the second about the categorical alternating proposition and the existential non-perpetual.
The first objection is refuted by showing false implications; the second is answered by clarifying metaphorical usage.
2
Analyze the reductio ad absurdum for the first objection
The author shows that if the alternating concept were the contradictory of the particular composite, it would lead to two false outcomes: negating the specific from all individuals of the general, and predicating the specific on all individuals of the general. Verify why both are false.
This step demonstrates that the supposed contradictory cannot hold.
3
Understand the second objection: both propositions are affirmative
The objector notes that contradiction requires one affirmative and one negative, but here both the alternating categorical and the particular existential are affirmative. Grasp the logical problem.
This objection is more subtle and targets the definition of naqīḍ.
4
Grasp the author's resolution: metaphorical usage (tajawwuz)
The author responds that 'naqīḍ' is used metaphorically; the alternating proposition is not the strict contradictory but is musāwiya (logically equivalent) to the true contradictory. Recognize that equivalence, not strict opposition, justifies the terminology.
This is the key logical move: distinguishing between logical equivalence and strict contradiction.
5
Apply the distinction to other Islamic logical examples
Practice identifying when a proposition is called a contradictory but is actually equivalent. For instance, in kalam, the disjunction 'The world is either eternal or created' is used as the contradictory of 'The world is eternal' even though both are affirmative? Actually check if it is equivalent to the negative. Work through an example from fiqh or kalam.
This step solidifies the lesson by applying it to real Islamic rational sciences.

ولكن في الجزئية: دفع للتوهم الناشي من قوله : " وللمركبة إلخ" من أنها وقعت مطلقة غير مقيدة بالكلية والمطلقات العلوم كليات، فيتوهم أن المفهوم المردد نقيض للمركبة الجزئية أيضاً. لا شيء من الحيوان إلخ وكذبهما ظاهر؛ لأنه يلزم في الأول سلب الأخص عن جميع أفراد الأعم وفي الثاني حمل الأخص على كل أفراد الأعم وكلاهما باطلان. كل حيوان إما إنسان إلخ إن قيل: إن هذه القضية الحملية المرددة المحمول كيف تكون نقيضا للوجودية اللادائمة المذكورة أي :قولنا بعض الحيوان إنسان بالفعل لا دائما؛ فإن كلا من هاتين القضيتين موجبتان ومن شرائط التناقض الاختلاف في الإيجاب والسلب كما مر فجوابه أن إطلاق النقيض ههنا على التجوز، وفي الحقيقة أنها مساوية لنقيضها. إسماعيل)
But in the particular: it is a refutation of the misconception arising from his saying 'and for the composite etc.' that it [the alternation] was made absolute not restricted by universality, and absolute things are known to be universal, so one might imagine that the alternating concept is also the contradictory of the particular composite. 'No animal etc.' and their falsity is obvious, because in the first it necessitates negating the more specific from all individuals of the more general, and in the second it necessitates predicating the more specific of all individuals of the more general, and both are false. 'Every animal is either a human etc.' If it is said: How can this categorical proposition with an alternating predicate be the contradictory of the existential non-perpetual mentioned, i.e. our saying 'Some animal is actually a human, not always'? For both of these propositions are affirmative, and one of the conditions of contradiction is difference in affirmation and negation, as previously mentioned. Its answer is that the term 'contradictory' here is used metaphorically, and in reality it is equivalent (musāwiya) to its contradictory. (Ismā'īl)
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection to the preceding analysis of the contradictory of particular composites. The objection arises from a misunderstanding: because the earlier statement about composites was unrestricted (muṭlaqa) and not limited to universal propositions, one might mistakenly think that the alternating concept (al-mafhūm al-muraddad) is also the contradictory of a particular composite. The commentator refutes this by showing the falsity of the supposed contradictories for the particular composite. Then a second objection is raised: How can a categorical proposition with an alternating predicate (such as 'Every animal is either a human always or not a human always') be the contradictory of an affirmative proposition (e.g., 'Some animal is actually a human, not always') when both are affirmative, and contradiction requires one affirmative and one negative? The answer is that the term 'contradictory' (naqīḍ) is used here metaphorically (tajawwuz). In reality, the alternating proposition is not the exact contradictory but is equivalent (musāwiya) to the true contradictory, which would have been a negative proposition. Thus, the relationship is one of logical equivalence rather than strict contradiction, but it is called contradictory in a loose sense.

Key Terms
مساوية (musāwiya): Equivalent (logically equivalent) to another proposition, not exactly the contradictory. [Note: In logic, musāwiya denotes equivalence; here it is contrasted with naqīḍ.]
تجوز (tajawwuz): Metaphorical or extended use of a term; here 'naqīḍ' is used metaphorically for 'musāwiya'. [Note: Standard term in Arabic rhetoric and logic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Definition and Conditions of Simple Conversion
Author defines simple conversion and clarifies its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of simple conversion, distinguish conceptual from literal swapping, and determine which propositions admit conversion.

Core Idea
Simple conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving truth, but only for standard propositions with conceptual swapping.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the concise definition of simple conversion from the matn. You will learn not just the definition but the crucial conditions that ensure the converse preserves truth. We will walk through the meaning of 'swapping extremes', the requirement of conceptual (not literal) exchange, and why disjunctives and non-standard propositions are excluded.

Guided Path
1
Locate the core definition
Find the concise definition at the beginning: 'Simple conversion is swapping the two extremes of the proposition while preserving truth.' Note the two components: swapping and truth-preservation.
2
Understand what 'extremes' means
The commentator clarifies that 'extremes' includes subject/predicate for categoricals and antecedent/consequent for conditionals. This is broader than just subject-predicate.
This ensures the definition covers both categorical and conditional propositions.
3
Distinguish conceptual from literal swapping
The passage mentions 'tabdīl ma‘nawī' (conceptual swapping). In conversion, the essence (dhat) of the predicate becomes subject, and the description (wasf) of the subject becomes predicate. Literal swapping would fail for categoricals.
For example, 'Every A is B' converts to 'Some B is A', not 'Every B is A'.
4
Identify excluded cases
The author excludes disjunctives because the mutual opposition (mu‘ānada) remains unchanged, so no benefit. Non-standard propositions (e.g., 'Some man is Zayd') are excluded because their converses are false or invalid.
Only standard propositions where a universal is predicated of its particulars are considered.

العكس المستوي تبديل طرفي القضية مع بقاء الصدق
قوله تبديل طرفي القضية سواء كان الطرفان هما الموضوع والمحمول أو المقدم والتالي. واعلم أن العكس كما يطلق على المعنى المصدري المذكور كذلك يطلق على القضية الحاصلة من التبديل، وذلك الإطلاق مجازي من قبيل إطلاق اللفظ على الملفوظ والخلق على المخلوق. قوله: مع بقاء الصدق:
طرفي القضية : أي جعل أحد طرفيها مكان الطرف الآخر والآخر مكانه هذه أولى من الموضوع والمحمول كما ذكر بعضهم؛ لشموله عكس المتصلات واعلم أنه لا عكس للمنفصلات لعدم الفائدة إذ المعاندة بين الطرفين تبقى على حالها سواء قدم الطرف الآخر أو لا، كما ذكرنا ولا علمت أن المراد بالتبديل التبديل المعنوي الذي يغير المعنى فاعلم أن الطرف الأول والثاني من القضية هو ذات الموضوع ووصف المحمول، ففي العكس يكون ذات المحمول موضوعا ووصف الموضوع محمولا لا أن ذات الموضوع يكون محمولا ووصف المحمول يكون موضوعا فافهم ولا تكن من الغافلين (عبد) تبديل طرفي القضية والمراد بالتبديل المعنوي الذي يغير المعنى؛ ولهذا قيل ولا عكس للمنفصلة إذ المعاندة بين الطرفين تبقى على حالها، ويمكن أن يراد بالعكس المنفي العكس المعتد به إلى لا عكس معتد به للمنفصلات، فلا يرد إن عني التبديل الحقيقي لم تنعكس الحمليات؛ إذ موضوعها الذات ومحمولها المفهوم، وإن عني الذكري لا انعكس المنفصلات واعلم أن المراد بالقضية المتعارفة وهي التي فيها حمل الكلي على جزئياته فخرجت القضايا الغير المتعارفة، فلا يرد أن بعض الإنسان زيد صادق عند من جوز حمل الجزئي فلا يستقيم عكسه عليه، لأن فيه حملا أوليا؛ إذ المحمول ههنا نفس معنون الموضوع وأيضاً بعض النوع إنسان صادق وعكسه كاذب وكذا يصدق لا شيء من الإنسان بنوع مع كذب عكسه وهو لا شيء من النوع بإنسان لصدق نقيضه وهو بعض النوع إنسان وصاحب السلم له ذهب إلى كذب بعض النوع إنسان مستدلا بقولنا لا شيء من النوع بإنسان صادق وهو ينعكس إلى ما يناقضه وهو لا شيء من الإنسان بنوع وقال: والسرفيه أن المعتبر في الحمل المتعارف صدق مفهوم المحمول لا نفس مفهومه .

Definition of Simple Conversion
Section
Simple conversion is swapping the two extremes of the proposition while preserving truth.
His saying: 'swapping the two extremes of the proposition' whether the two extremes are the subject and predicate or the antecedent and consequent. And know that conversion is applied both to the infinitival meaning mentioned and also to the proposition resulting from the swapping, and that application is metaphorical, like applying the word to the spoken thing and the creation to the created thing. His saying: 'while preserving truth'.
[Comment: 'the two extremes of the proposition' means making one of its extremes take the place of the other extreme and the other its place; this is better than [saying] subject and predicate as some have mentioned, because it includes the conversion of conditionals. And know that there is no conversion for disjunctives due to lack of benefit, since the mutual opposition between the two extremes remains as it was whether the other extreme is advanced or not, as we mentioned. And I do not know—but the intended meaning of swapping is the conceptual swapping that changes the meaning. So know that the first and second extremes of the proposition are the essence (dhat) of the subject and the description of the predicate. So in conversion, the essence of the predicate becomes subject and the description of the subject becomes predicate, not that the essence of the subject becomes predicate and the description of the predicate becomes subject. Understand and do not be among the heedless. ('Abd) Swapping the two extremes of the proposition: the intended meaning is the conceptual swapping that changes the meaning; for this reason it is said there is no conversion for the disjunctive, since the mutual opposition between the two extremes remains as it was. And it is possible that the negated conversion is the conversion that is taken into account, i.e., there is no conversion taken into account for disjunctives. So it is not objected that if real swapping is meant, categoricals would not convert, since their subject is the essence and their predicate is the concept; but if the intended is the mention-swapping, then disjunctives would convert. And know that what is meant by 'proposition' [in the definition] is the standard proposition, which is that in which a universal is predicated over its particulars. Thus non-standard propositions are excluded. So it is not objected that 'Some man is Zayd' is true for the one who allows predication of the particular, and its conversion would not be valid, because it involves a primary predication, since the predicate here is the same as the designated meaning of the subject. Also 'Some species is man' is true but its conversion is false, and similarly 'No man is a species' is true while its conversion 'No species is man' is false because its contradictory 'Some species is man' is true. And the author of al-Sullam held that 'Some species is man' is false, arguing that 'No species is man' is true and it converts to what contradicts it, i.e., 'No man is species'. And he said: rather, what is considered in standard predication is the truth of the concept of the predicate, not its very concept.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage defines simple conversion (al-‘aks al-mustawi) and belongs to a commentary (sharh) on a matn. The definition is given: swapping the two extremes of a proposition while preserving truth. The commentator then expands on the definition. First, he clarifies that 'extremes' includes both subject/predicate in categoricals and antecedent/consequent in conditionals. He notes that the term 'conversion' applies both to the act of swapping and to the resulting proposition, the latter being metaphorical. He emphasizes the condition 'while preserving truth' meaning the converse must be true if the original is true. Then he explains that swapping is not literal word-for-word replacement but a conceptual swap: the essence (dhat) of the original predicate becomes the new subject, and the description (wasf) of the original subject becomes the new predicate. This ensures that e.g., 'Every A is B' converts to 'Some B is A', not 'Every B is A'. He then discusses why disjunctive (munfasila) propositions have no conversion: the mutual opposition (mu‘ānada) between the two parts remains unchanged, so no benefit. He distinguishes between 'real' swapping (swapping the actual words, which fails for categoricals) and 'mention' swapping, which would wrongly allow conversion of disjunctives. Therefore, the intended meaning is conceptual swapping. Finally, he restricts the definition to 'standard propositions' (al-qaḍiyya al-muta‘ārafa) where a universal is predicated of its particulars. Non-standard propositions like 'Some man is Zayd' (where a particular is predicated) or 'Some species is man' are excluded, because their converses are false or invalid. He gives examples and refers to the author of al-Sullam for discussion.

تبديل معنوي
قضية متعارفة
معاندة
حمل أولي

Simple conversion is a rule-governed operation that requires conceptual swapping and applies only to standard categoricals and conditionals; disjunctives and non-standard propositions do not admit conversion.

Conversion of Affirmative Propositions
Author explains conversion rule for affirmatives and common errors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that affirmative propositions convert only to particular affirmatives and identify common errors in conversion due to misidentifying the logical predicate.

Core Idea
Affirmative converts only to particular; truth preservation is required, but falsehood is not.

In this passage, the author lays out a crucial rule for simple conversion: every affirmative proposition, whether universal or particular, converts only to a particular affirmative. He also clarifies the logical conditions—preservation of truth is necessary, but preservation of falsehood is not—and warns against a common pitfall where the predicate is misidentified, leading to invalid converses. Let's walk through the rule and the errors step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conversion rule for affirmatives
Find the author's statement: 'the affirmative only converts as particular' (الموجبة إنما تنعكس جزئية). Note that this applies to both universal and particular affirmatives. The converse must be affirmative and particular.
2
Understand truth preservation versus falsehood preservation
The author explains that only preservation of truth is required: if the original is true, the converse must be true. However, a false original can have a true converse. For example, 'Every animal is human' (false) converts to 'Some human is animal' (true). This is because the converse may be more general.
3
Apply the conversion rule to an example
Take a universal affirmative: 'Every human is an animal' (كل إنسان حيوان). According to the rule, its converse should be the particular affirmative 'Some animal is human' (بعض الحيوان إنسان). Check that the quality and quantity match the rule.
4
Identify common errors in conversion
The author warns that when the predicate is a relational phrase (e.g., 'on the throne'), novices mistakenly treat the noun alone as the predicate. For 'Every king is on the throne', they incorrectly convert to 'Some throne is on the king'. The correct predicate is the whole phrase 'on the throne', so the proper converse would be 'Some thing on the throne is a king' (or similar).
