أحكام العكس المستوي ويلزمه العكس حينئذ وهو أن بعض ما صدق عليه "ب" بالإمكان صدق عليه "ج" بالإمكان، وعلى رأي الشيخ معنى كل ج ب بالإمكان هو أن كل ما صدق عليه "ج" بالفعل صدق عليه "ب" بالإمكان ويكون عكسه على أسلوب الشيخ هو أن بعض ما صدق عليه "ب" بالفعل صدق عليه "ج" بالإمكان، ولا شك أنه لا يلزم من صدق الأصل حينئذ صدق العكس، مثلا: إذا فرض أن مركوب زيد بالفعل منحصر في الفرس، صدق كل حمار بالفعل مركوب زيد بالإمكان ولم يصدق عكسه وهو أن بعض مركوب زيد بالفعل حمار بالإمكان. فالمصنف لما اختار مذهب الشيخ؛ إذ هو المتبادر في العرف واللغة حكم بأنه لا عكس للممكنتين. ويلزمه العكس وإلا يصدق نقيضه ونضمه مع الأصل بأن نجعل الأصل لإيجاب صغرى وهذا النقيض لكلية كبرى ونقول كل ج ب بالإمكان ولا شيء من ب ج بالضرورة ينتج لا شيء من ج ج بالضرورة، وهو سلب الشيء عن نفسه، وهو محال، وهذا المحال إنما نشأ من صدق نقيضه؛ لكون الأصل مفروض الصدق والهيأة منتجة ومنشأ المحال محال، فهذا النقيض محال، فالعكس حق. إذ هو المتبادر : فإن "ج" لا يطلق في العرف ولا في اللغة على ما لا يكون متصفا بالجيمية أزلا وأبدا، فالأبيض مثلا لا يطلق على ما لا يكون البياض قائما به دائما، فلا يقال للزنجي: إنه أبيض لا عرفا ولا لغة، نعم! إطلاقه على ما يكون أبيض بالفعل سواء كان في الزمان الماضي أو المستقبل أو الحال صحيح قطعا . (برهان) حكم بأنه لا عكس للممكنتين: اعلم أن القدماء ذهبوا إلى أنهما تنعكسان ممكنة عامة واستدلوا عليه بثلاثة وجوه الأول الافتراض تقريره: أنا إذا فرضنا أن الذات التي يصدق عليها "ج" و"ب" بالإمكان "د"، فنقول: "د" "ب" بالإمكان و "د" "ج" بالإمكان. الثاني الخلف، تقريره أنه لو لم يصدق بعض ب ج بالإمكان صدق لا شيء من ب ج بالضرورة، فيحصل كبرى مع الأصل فينتج المحمول وهو ناش من نقيض العكس فهو باطل، فالعكس حق، الثالث العكس تقريره أن قولنا : لا شيء من ب ج بالضرورة ينعكس إلى قولنا: لا شيء من ج ب بالضرورة قد كان بعض ج ب ،بالإمكان هذا خلف والمتأخرون قالوا بعدم انعكاسها، وأجابوا الاستدلالات فعن الأولين بمنع إنتاج الصغرى الممكنة في الأول والثاني وعن الثالث بمنع انعكاس السالبة الضرورية سالبة ضرورية والحق ما يستفاد من كلام الشارح من المعتبر في عقد الوضع لو كان صدق الوصف العنواني على الذات بالإمكان كما هو مذهب الفارابي فهما تنعكسان إلى الممكنة العامة بالضرورة إن كان صدقه عليها بالفعل كما هو ظاهر من كلام الشيخ فلا عكس لهما كما علمت في الشرح مشروحا.
Rulings of simple conversion: Its conversion is necessary then, which is that some of what is described by 'B' possibly is described by 'C' possibly. According to the opinion of the Shaykh, the meaning of 'Every C is B possibly' is that everything which is actually described by 'C' is described by 'B' possibly, and its conversion according to the method of the Shaykh is that some of what is actually described by 'B' is described by 'C' possibly. There is no doubt that it does not follow from the truth of the original then the truth of the converse. For example: if we assume that what Zayd rides is actually confined to the horse, then 'Every donkey is something ridden by Zayd possibly' is true, but its converse, which is 'Some of what is ridden by Zayd actually is a donkey possibly' is not true. So the author, when he chose the doctrine of the Shaykh—since it is the prevalent in custom and language—ruled that there is no conversion for the two possible propositions. Its conversion is necessary; otherwise its contradictory would be true, and we combine it with the original by making the original a minor premise (affirmative) and this contradictory a universal major premise, and we say: 'Every C is B possibly' and 'No B is C necessarily' yields 'No C is C necessarily', which is the negation of a thing of itself, and that is impossible. This impossibility arises only from the truth of its contradictory; because the original is assumed true and the mood is productive, and the source of impossibility is impossible, so this contradictory is impossible, thus the conversion is true. Since it is the prevalent: for 'C' is not applied in custom nor in language to what is not described by C-ness eternally and forever. For example, 'white' is not applied to what does not have whiteness subsisting in it always. So one does not say of a black person: 'He is white', neither customarily nor linguistically. Yes! Its application to what is actually white, whether in past, future, or present time, is certainly correct. (Proof) ruling that there is no conversion for the two possible propositions: Know that the ancients held that they convert to a general possible and supported it with three arguments. First: hypothesis—its formulation: that if we assume the essence described by 'C' and 'B' possibly to be 'D', we say: 'D is B possibly' and 'D is C possibly'. Second: reductio—its formulation: that if 'Some B is C possibly' were not true, then 'No B is C necessarily' would be true. So a major premise is combined with the original and produces the conclusion which follows from the contradictory of the converse, so it is false, thus the converse is true. Third: conversion—its formulation: that our statement 'No B is C necessarily' converts to 'No C is B necessarily', and there was 'Some C is B possibly' – this is a contradiction. The later logicians said that they do not convert, and they replied to the arguments: to the first two by denying the productivity of the possible minor premise in the first and second, and to the third by denying that the necessary negative converts as a necessary negative. The truth is what is understood from the words of the commentator: if the truth of the attributive description of the essence is by possibility, as is the doctrine of al-Farabi, then they convert to a general possible necessarily; if its truth is by actuality, as appears from the words of the Shaykh, then there is no conversion for them, as you have learned from the detailed commentary.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the conversion of possible propositions (al-mumkinatan) in categorical logic. The author first explains the standard view (that of Ibn Sina, referred to as 'al-Shaykh') that possible propositions do not convert. He gives an example: if all of Zayd's actual mounts are horses, then 'Every donkey is possibly ridden by Zayd' is true, but its converse 'Something actually ridden by Zayd is possibly a donkey' is false, because nothing actually ridden by Zayd is a donkey. The author then presents a proof by reductio that the conversion is actually necessary: assuming the converse is false leads to a contradiction (a thing being negated of itself). The proof sets up a syllogism with the original as minor and the contradictory of the converse as major, yielding 'No C is C necessarily', which is impossible. Therefore the converse must be true. Next, the author explains why the Shaykh's interpretation is prevalent: a term like 'white' is only applied to things that have whiteness in actuality, not to potential things. Then a distinction is made between the ancients (qudama') and later logicians (muta'akhkhirun). The ancients argued that possible propositions do convert via three proofs: 1) hypothesis (postulating a subject D that is both C and B), 2) reductio (similar to above), 3) conversion of the necessary negative. The later logicians rejected each: the first two because the minor premise 'D is B possibly' does not yield a conclusion; the third because a necessary negative does not convert as a necessary negative. The author concludes that the truth depends on whether the predicate applies to the subject in actuality or possibility, siding with al-Farabi's view that if the predicate is attributed possibly, conversion holds; if actually, it does not. All examples use non-secular contexts like Zayd's riding and colors.

الممكنة
الشيخ

Conversion of Perpetual and General Universal Negatives
Conversion rules for perpetual and general universal negatives with reductio proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert any perpetual or general universal negative proposition into its converse and verify the validity of the conversion using a reductio argument.

Core Idea
Perpetual and general universal negatives convert to corresponding perpetual or general customaries, always preserving truth.

This lesson unpacks the conversion rules for two types of modal universal negatives: the perpetuals (absolutely necessary and absolutely perpetual) and the generals (conditional general and customary general). You will learn not only what they convert to, but also how the classical logicians proved these conversions using reductio ad absurdum. The examples in the source use everyday items, but we will illustrate using Islamic legal propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal type of the universal negative
Determine whether the proposition is perpetual (absolutely necessary or absolutely perpetual) or general (conditional general or customary general). Check the wording: perpetuals often include 'necessarily' or 'perpetually'; generals include 'as long as' conditions.
Perpetuals assert the predicate is absent for all times; generals assert absence only when the subject has a certain attribute.
2
State the conversion rule
For perpetuals, the converse is also a perpetual universal negative. For generals, the converse is a general customary universal negative (i.e., the predicate is denied of the subject as long as the subject has the attribute described in the original).
The modal strength is preserved in the converse.
3
Apply the conversion to a new proposition
Given a perpetual negative like 'No dead animal is permissible to eat, necessarily' (perpetual), its converse is 'No permissible-to-eat thing is a dead animal, perpetually'.
Ensure the terms are swapped and the quality and quantity remain the same.
4
Test the conversion using reductio
Assume the converse is false; then its contradictory ('Some permissible-to-eat thing is a dead animal, actually') is true. Combine this with the original premise to derive an absurdity (e.g., something being both permissible and not permissible), proving the converse must be true.
The reductio works by using the original as a major premise and the negation of the converse as a minor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنعكس الدائمتان مطلقة دائمة، والعامتان عرفية عامة والخاصتان عرفية، لا دائمة في البعض، قوله تنعكس الدائمتان مطلقة: أي الضرورية المطلقة والدائمة المطلقة تنعكسان دائمة مطلقة، مثلا إذا صدق قولنا: لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة أو بالدوام، صدق لا شيء من الحجر بإنسان دائما، وإلا لصدق نقيضه - وهو بعض الحجر إنسان بالفعل وهو مع الأصل ينتج "بعض الحجر ليس بحجر دائما". هذا خلف. قوله : والعامتان عرفية عامة أي المشروطة العامة والعرفية العامة تنعكسان عرفية عامة مثلا إذا صدق بالضرورة أو بالدوام لا شي من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لصدق بالدوام لا شيء من ساكن الأصابع بكاتب ما دام ساكن الأصابع، وإلا فيصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا: بعض ساكن الأصابع كاتب حين هو ساكن الأصابع بالفعل- وهو مع الأصل ينتج "بعض ساكن الأصابع ليس بساكن الأصابع حين هو ساكن الأصابع". هذا خلف قوله : والخاصتان إلخ أي المشروطة الخاصة والعرفية الخاصة تنعكسان إلى عرفية عامة سالبة كلية مقيدة باللادوام في البعض، وهو إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة موجبة جزئية، فنقول: إذا صدق لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما، صدق لا شيء من الساكن بكاتب ما دام ساكنا لا دائماً في البعض، أي بعض الساكن كاتب بالفعل. وهو مع الأصل إلخ: بأن يجعل هذا النقيض لإيجابه صغرى، والأصل لكليته كبرى، فيلزم سلب الشيء عن نفسه، ومنشؤه ليس الأصل؛ لأنه مفروض الصدق، ولا الهيأة؛ لأنها بديهية الإنتاج، فليس إلا هذا النقيض، فيكون باطلا، فالعكس حق والخاصتان: الضابطة في السوالب أن السالبة الجزئية لا تنعكس إلا في الخاصتين؛ فإنهما تنعكسان عرفية خاصة، وأما السالبة الكلية فإن لم يصدق عليها الدوام الوصفي أعني العرف العام فلا تنعكس أصلا، وهي السوالب السبع الوقتيتان والوجوديتان والممكنتان والمطلقة العامة، وإن صدق عليها الدوام الوصفي، وهي ست قضايا: فإن صدق عليها الدوام الذاتي أيضاً وهما الدائمتان انعكست كلية إلى الدوام الوصفي العرفي العام و إن لم يكن مقيدا باللادوام ،وهما العامتان وإن كانت مقيدة به وهما الخاصتان انعكست كلية إلى الدوام الوصفي مع قيد اللادوام في البعض. (نور الله)
The two perpetuals convert to an absolute perpetual, and the two generals to a general customary, and the two specific to a customary, not perpetual in some. His statement 'the two perpetuals convert to absolute perpetual' means the absolute necessary and the absolute perpetual convert to a perpetual absolute. For example, if our statement 'No human is a stone necessarily or perpetually' is true, then 'No stone is a human perpetually' is true; otherwise its contradictory would be true—which is 'Some stone is a human actually'—and when combined with the original it yields 'Some stone is not a stone perpetually'. This is a contradiction. His statement: 'and the two generals to a general customary' means the conditional general and the customary general convert to a general customary. For example, if it is true necessarily or perpetually that 'No writer is finger-sitting as long as he is a writer', then it is true perpetually that 'No finger-sitter is a writer as long as he is a finger-sitter'; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our statement 'Some finger-sitter is a writer while he is actually a finger-sitter'—and combined with the original it yields 'Some finger-sitter is not a finger-sitter while he is a finger-sitter'. This is a contradiction. His statement: 'and the two specific, etc.' means the conditional specific and the customary specific convert to a general customary universal negative restricted to non-perpetuity in some, which indicates a general absolute affirmative particular. So we say: if 'No writer is finger-sitting as long as he is a writer, not perpetually' is true, then 'No finger-sitter is a writer as long as he is a finger-sitter, not perpetually in some' is true, i.e., 'Some finger-sitter is a writer actually'. And it is combined with the original, etc.: by making this contradictory a minor premise (affirmative) and the original a universal major premise, then the negation of a thing of itself follows. Its source is not the original because it is assumed true, nor the mood because it is evidently productive, so it must be this contradictory, so it is false, thus the converse is true. And the two specifics: the rule for negations is that the particular negative converts only in the two specifics; for they convert to a customary specific. As for the universal negative, if descriptive perpetuity (i.e., general custom) does not apply to it, then it does not convert at all—these are the seven negatives: the two time-relative, the two existential, the two possible, and the absolute general. If descriptive perpetuity applies to it—and these are six propositions: if essential perpetuity also applies to them, which are the two perpetuals, they convert universally to general customary descriptive perpetuity, not restricted to non-perpetuity; these are the two generals; and if they are restricted by it, which are the two specifics, they convert universally to descriptive perpetuity with the restriction of non-perpetuity in some. (Nur Allah)
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage provides the rules for the conversion of various modal universal negative propositions. It first states the rule: the two perpetuals (al-dā'imātān: the absolute necessary and the absolute perpetual) convert to an absolute perpetual (dā'ima muṭlaqa). An example: 'No human is a stone necessarily/perpetually' converts to 'No stone is a human perpetually'. A reductio proof is given: if the converse were false, its contradictory 'Some stone is a human actually' would be true, and combining with the original yields the impossible conclusion that some stone is not a stone perpetually. Next, the two generals (al-‘āmmatān: the conditional general and the customary general) convert to a general customary (ʿurfiyya ʿāmma). Example: 'No writer is finger-sitting as long as he is a writer' converts to 'No finger-sitter is a writer as long as he is a finger-sitter', with a similar reductio. The two specifics (al-khāṣṣatān: the conditional specific and the customary specific) convert to a general customary universal negative restricted to non-perpetuity in some, which is equivalent to a general absolute affirmative particular. Example: 'No writer is finger-sitting as long as he is a writer, not perpetually' converts to 'No finger-sitter is a writer as long as he is a finger-sitter, not perpetually in some', meaning some finger-sitter is actually a writer. The proof again uses reductio. The author then summarizes: particular negatives only convert in the two specifics. Universal negatives that lack descriptive perpetuity (al-dawām al-waṣfī) do not convert at all (e.g., the seven: time-relative, existential, possible, and absolute general). If they have descriptive perpetuity, six propositions remain: if they also have essential perpetuity, they are the two perpetuals and convert universally without restriction; if not, they are the two generals and convert universally with restriction; if they are restricted, they are the two specifics and convert universally with the restriction of non-perpetuity in some. All examples come from ordinary life (e.g., 'human', 'stone', 'writer', 'finger-sitter') and are used purely for logical illustration.

العامتان
السالبة الكلية
العرفية العامة

Conversion of Specific Universal Negatives and Non-Convertible Types
Conversion rule for specific negatives and classification based on descriptive perpetuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a specific universal negative and identify which universal negatives do not convert due to lack of descriptive perpetuity.

Core Idea
Specific negatives convert only to a restricted form, and without descriptive perpetuity, universal negatives are inconvertible.

This lesson covers the remaining category: the two specific negatives (conditional specific and customary specific). It also introduces the crucial concept of descriptive perpetuity (al-dawām al-waṣfī), which determines whether a universal negative can be converted at all. You will learn to classify propositions based on this property and apply the correct conversion rule.

Guided Path
1
Recognize a specific universal negative
A specific negative is a universal negative that adds the qualifier 'not perpetually' (lā dā'iman). Example: 'No writer is finger-sitting as long as he is a writer, not perpetually.'
It is a general proposition with a non-perpetuity condition.
2
State the conversion rule for specifics
Specific negatives convert to a general customary universal negative restricted to 'not perpetually in some' (lā dā'iman fī 'l-ba‘ḍ), which is equivalent to a particular affirmative absolute (e.g., 'Some finger-sitter is a writer actually').
The converse is no longer a universal but a particular.
3
Identify propositions that lack descriptive perpetuity
Descriptive perpetuity means the predicate is always denied of the subject whenever the subject has the attribute described. Propositions without it include the seven negatives: two time-relative, two existential, two possible, and the absolute general. These do not convert at all.
Check if the predicate applies to the subject in all cases of the attribute; if not, no conversion.
4
Apply the conversion to an Islamic legal example
Take a specific negative: 'No intoxicant is lawful to consume as long as it is intoxicating, not perpetually' (i.e., it might become lawful in some circumstance). Its converse is: 'No lawful thing is an intoxicant as long as it is lawful, not perpetually in some', meaning 'Some lawful thing is an intoxicant actually'.
The particular converse indicates that at least one lawful thing is actually an intoxicant.

تنعكس الدائمتان مطلقة دائمة، والعامتان عرفية عامة والخاصتان عرفية، لا دائمة في البعض، قوله تنعكس الدائمتان مطلقة: أي الضرورية المطلقة والدائمة المطلقة تنعكسان دائمة مطلقة، مثلا إذا صدق قولنا: لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة أو بالدوام، صدق لا شيء من الحجر بإنسان دائما، وإلا لصدق نقيضه - وهو بعض الحجر إنسان بالفعل وهو مع الأصل ينتج "بعض الحجر ليس بحجر دائما". هذا خلف. قوله : والعامتان عرفية عامة أي المشروطة العامة والعرفية العامة تنعكسان عرفية عامة مثلا إذا صدق بالضرورة أو بالدوام لا شي من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لصدق بالدوام لا شيء من ساكن الأصابع بكاتب ما دام ساكن الأصابع، وإلا فيصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا: بعض ساكن الأصابع كاتب حين هو ساكن الأصابع بالفعل- وهو مع الأصل ينتج "بعض ساكن الأصابع ليس بساكن الأصابع حين هو ساكن الأصابع". هذا خلف قوله : والخاصتان إلخ أي المشروطة الخاصة والعرفية الخاصة تنعكسان إلى عرفية عامة سالبة كلية مقيدة باللادوام في البعض، وهو إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة موجبة جزئية، فنقول: إذا صدق لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما، صدق لا شيء من الساكن بكاتب ما دام ساكنا لا دائماً في البعض، أي بعض الساكن كاتب بالفعل. وهو مع الأصل إلخ: بأن يجعل هذا النقيض لإيجابه صغرى، والأصل لكليته كبرى، فيلزم سلب الشيء عن نفسه، ومنشؤه ليس الأصل؛ لأنه مفروض الصدق، ولا الهيأة؛ لأنها بديهية الإنتاج، فليس إلا هذا النقيض، فيكون باطلا، فالعكس حق والخاصتان: الضابطة في السوالب أن السالبة الجزئية لا تنعكس إلا في الخاصتين؛ فإنهما تنعكسان عرفية خاصة، وأما السالبة الكلية فإن لم يصدق عليها الدوام الوصفي أعني العرف العام فلا تنعكس أصلا، وهي السوالب السبع الوقتيتان والوجوديتان والممكنتان والمطلقة العامة، وإن صدق عليها الدوام الوصفي، وهي ست قضايا: فإن صدق عليها الدوام الذاتي أيضاً وهما الدائمتان انعكست كلية إلى الدوام الوصفي العرفي العام و إن لم يكن مقيدا باللادوام ،وهما العامتان وإن كانت مقيدة به وهما الخاصتان انعكست كلية إلى الدوام الوصفي مع قيد اللادوام في البعض. (نور الله)
The two perpetuals convert to an absolute perpetual, and the two generals to a general customary, and the two specific to a customary, not perpetual in some. His statement 'the two perpetuals convert to absolute perpetual' means the absolute necessary and the absolute perpetual convert to a perpetual absolute. For example, if our statement 'No human is a stone necessarily or perpetually' is true, then 'No stone is a human perpetually' is true; otherwise its contradictory would be true—which is 'Some stone is a human actually'—and when combined with the original it yields 'Some stone is not a stone perpetually'. This is a contradiction. His statement: 'and the two generals to a general customary' means the conditional general and the customary general convert to a general customary. For example, if it is true necessarily or perpetually that 'No writer is finger-sitting as long as he is a writer', then it is true perpetually that 'No finger-sitter is a writer as long as he is a finger-sitter'; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our statement 'Some finger-sitter is a writer while he is actually a finger-sitter'—and combined with the original it yields 'Some finger-sitter is not a finger-sitter while he is a finger-sitter'. This is a contradiction. His statement: 'and the two specific, etc.' means the conditional specific and the customary specific convert to a general customary universal negative restricted to non-perpetuity in some, which indicates a general absolute affirmative particular. So we say: if 'No writer is finger-sitting as long as he is a writer, not perpetually' is true, then 'No finger-sitter is a writer as long as he is a finger-sitter, not perpetually in some' is true, i.e., 'Some finger-sitter is a writer actually'. And it is combined with the original, etc.: by making this contradictory a minor premise (affirmative) and the original a universal major premise, then the negation of a thing of itself follows. Its source is not the original because it is assumed true, nor the mood because it is evidently productive, so it must be this contradictory, so it is false, thus the converse is true. And the two specifics: the rule for negations is that the particular negative converts only in the two specifics; for they convert to a customary specific. As for the universal negative, if descriptive perpetuity (i.e., general custom) does not apply to it, then it does not convert at all—these are the seven negatives: the two time-relative, the two existential, the two possible, and the absolute general. If descriptive perpetuity applies to it—and these are six propositions: if essential perpetuity also applies to them, which are the two perpetuals, they convert universally to general customary descriptive perpetuity, not restricted to non-perpetuity; these are the two generals; and if they are restricted by it, which are the two specifics, they convert universally to descriptive perpetuity with the restriction of non-perpetuity in some. (Nur Allah)
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage provides the rules for the conversion of various modal universal negative propositions. It first states the rule: the two perpetuals (al-dā'imātān: the absolute necessary and the absolute perpetual) convert to an absolute perpetual (dā'ima muṭlaqa). An example: 'No human is a stone necessarily/perpetually' converts to 'No stone is a human perpetually'. A reductio proof is given: if the converse were false, its contradictory 'Some stone is a human actually' would be true, and combining with the original yields the impossible conclusion that some stone is not a stone perpetually. Next, the two generals (al-‘āmmatān: the conditional general and the customary general) convert to a general customary (ʿurfiyya ʿāmma). Example: 'No writer is finger-sitting as long as he is a writer' converts to 'No finger-sitter is a writer as long as he is a finger-sitter', with a similar reductio. The two specifics (al-khāṣṣatān: the conditional specific and the customary specific) convert to a general customary universal negative restricted to non-perpetuity in some, which is equivalent to a general absolute affirmative particular. Example: 'No writer is finger-sitting as long as he is a writer, not perpetually' converts to 'No finger-sitter is a writer as long as he is a finger-sitter, not perpetually in some', meaning some finger-sitter is actually a writer. The proof again uses reductio. The author then summarizes: particular negatives only convert in the two specifics. Universal negatives that lack descriptive perpetuity (al-dawām al-waṣfī) do not convert at all (e.g., the seven: time-relative, existential, possible, and absolute general). If they have descriptive perpetuity, six propositions remain: if they also have essential perpetuity, they are the two perpetuals and convert universally without restriction; if not, they are the two generals and convert universally with restriction; if they are restricted, they are the two specifics and convert universally with the restriction of non-perpetuity in some. All examples come from ordinary life (e.g., 'human', 'stone', 'writer', 'finger-sitter') and are used purely for logical illustration.

Key Terms
الخاصتان
اللادوام
الدوام الوصفي
العرف العام

Conversion Rules for Modal Negative Propositions
Author states and explains three conversion rules for modal negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conversion rules for modal negative propositions and verify them using reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
Conversion of modal negatives is determined by the type of permanence, not just quantity and quality.

In immediate inference, simple conversion swaps subject and predicate preserving truth. For modal negatives, the resulting modality depends on the original's permanence type. This passage classifies modal negatives into three pairs—the two permanent (الدائمتان), the two general (العامتان), and the two particular (الخاصتان)—and states their conversion rules, each proven by reductio. Mastering these rules sharpens your ability to manipulate modal propositions in Islamic logical argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three pairs of modal negatives
Notice the author distinguishes 'الدائمتان' (necessary absolute and permanent absolute), 'العامتان' (universal conditional and general customary), and 'الخاصتان' (particular conditional and particular customary). Each pair shares a kind of permanence.
2
Learn the conversion rule for each pair
The permanent (da'imatan) convert to an absolute permanent (مطلقة دائمة). The general ('ammatan) convert to a general customary (عرفية عامة). The particular (khasatan) convert to a general customary but with non-permanence in some, resulting in a particular affirmative.
3
Understand the proof by contradiction (khalf)
For each case, the commentator assumes the converse false, derives its contradictory (a particular affirmative), then combines it with the original universal negative via a syllogism to produce a self-negation (e.g., 'some stone is not stone'). This absurdity proves the converse must be true.
The syllogism uses the original as major premise and the contradictory as minor.
4
Apply the general principle for universal negatives
The passage later summarizes that universal negatives lacking descriptive permanence (seven types: temporal, existential, possible, general absolute) do not convert at all. Those with descriptive permanence (six types) convert according to whether they also have essential permanence (-> absolute permanent) or are qualified by non-permanence (-> general customary with partial non-permanence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحكام العكس المستوي
تنعكس الدائمتان مطلقة دائمة، والعامتان عرفية عامة والخاصتان عرفية، لا دائمة في البعض،
قوله تنعكس الدائمتان مطلقة: أي الضرورية المطلقة والدائمة المطلقة تنعكسان دائمة مطلقة، مثلا إذا صدق قولنا: لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة أو بالدوام، صدق لا شيء من الحجر بإنسان دائما، وإلا لصدق نقيضه - وهو بعض الحجر إنسان بالفعل وهو مع الأصل ينتج "بعض الحجر ليس بحجر دائما". هذا خلف. قوله : والعامتان عرفية عامة أي المشروطة العامة والعرفية العامة تنعكسان عرفية عامة مثلا إذا صدق بالضرورة أو بالدوام لا شي من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لصدق بالدوام لا شيء من ساكن الأصابع بكاتب ما دام ساكن الأصابع، وإلا فيصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا: بعض ساكن الأصابع كاتب حين هو ساكن الأصابع بالفعل- وهو مع الأصل ينتج "بعض ساكن الأصابع ليس بساكن الأصابع حين هو ساكن الأصابع". هذا خلف قوله : والخاصتان إلخ أي المشروطة الخاصة والعرفية الخاصة تنعكسان إلى عرفية عامة سالبة كلية مقيدة باللادوام في البعض، وهو إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة موجبة جزئية، فنقول: إذا صدق لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لا دائما، صدق لا شيء من الساكن بكاتب ما دام ساكنا لا دائماً في البعض، أي بعض الساكن كاتب بالفعل.
وهو مع الأصل إلخ: بأن يجعل هذا النقيض لإيجابه صغرى، والأصل لكليته كبرى، فيلزم سلب الشيء عن نفسه، ومنشؤه ليس الأصل؛ لأنه مفروض الصدق، ولا الهيأة؛ لأنها بديهية الإنتاج، فليس إلا هذا النقيض، فيكون باطلا، فالعكس حق والخاصتان: الضابطة في السوالب أن السالبة الجزئية لا تنعكس إلا في الخاصتين؛ فإنهما تنعكسان عرفية خاصة، وأما السالبة الكلية فإن لم يصدق عليها الدوام الوصفي أعني العرف العام فلا تنعكس أصلا، وهي السوالب السبع الوقتيتان والوجوديتان والممكنتان والمطلقة العامة، وإن صدق عليها الدوام الوصفي، وهي ست قضايا: فإن صدق عليها الدوام الذاتي أيضاً وهما الدائمتان انعكست كلية إلى الدوام الوصفي العرفي العام و إن لم يكن مقيدا باللادوام ،وهما العامتان وإن كانت مقيدة به وهما الخاصتان انعكست كلية إلى الدوام الوصفي مع قيد اللادوام في البعض. (نور الله)
Judgments of Simple Conversion
The two permanent (negatives) convert to an absolute permanent; the two general (negatives) convert to a general customary; the two particular (negatives) convert to a customary (general), not permanent in some.
His saying: 'The two permanent convert to an absolute permanent' means the necessary absolute and the permanent absolute convert to a permanent absolute. For example, if our statement is true: 'No human is a stone' by necessity or by perpetuity, then 'No stone is a human' is true perpetually. Otherwise, its contradictory would be true – which is 'Some stone is a human' actually – and this together with the original yields 'Some stone is not a stone perpetually'. This is a reductio.
His saying: 'And the two general convert to a general customary' means the universal conditional and the general customary convert to a general customary. For example, if it is true by necessity or perpetuity that 'No writer is still-fingered as long as he is a writer', then it is true perpetually that 'No still-fingered person is a writer as long as he is still-fingered'. Otherwise, its contradictory would be true, which is our statement: 'Some still-fingered person is a writer at the time he is still-fingered' – actually – and this together with the original yields 'Some still-fingered person is not still-fingered at the time he is still-fingered'. This is a reductio.
His saying: 'And the two particular etc.' means the particular conditional and the particular customary convert to a general customary universal negative qualified by non-permanence in some, which is an indication of a general absolute particular affirmative. So we say: If it is true that 'No writer is still-fingered as long as he is a writer, not perpetually', then it is true that 'No still-fingered person is a writer as long as he is still-fingered, not perpetually in some', i.e., some still-fingered person is actually a writer.
'And it is with the original etc.': by making this contradictory, due to its affirmation, a minor premise, and the original, due to its universality, a major premise, there follows the negation of the thing of itself. Its source is not the original because it is assumed true, nor the form because it is self-evidently productive, so this contradictory is the only suspect, so it is false. Thus the converse is true.
The two particular: The rule for negatives is that the particular negative converts only in the two particular; for they convert to a particular customary. As for the universal negative, if the descriptive permanence (i.e., the general customary) does not apply to it, then it does not convert at all. These are the seven negatives: the two temporal, the two existential, the two possible, and the general absolute. If the descriptive permanence does apply to them, there are six propositions: if the essential permanence also applies, and they are the two permanent, they convert universally to the descriptive general customary permanence, not qualified by non-permanence – and they are the two general. If they are qualified by it, and they are the two particular, they convert universally to the descriptive permanence with the qualification of non-permanence in some. (Nur Allah)
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage defines the rules of simple conversion for modal negative propositions. It begins by stating three conversion types: the two permanent (da'imatan) convert to an absolute permanent; the two general ('ammatan) convert to a general customary; and the two particular (khasatan) convert to a customary (general) but not permanent in some (i.e., a particular affirmative). The commentator then explains each case. For the permanent, he gives the example 'No human is a stone' (a necessary universal negative) and shows that its converse 'No stone is a human' is also permanent. He proves by contradiction: if the converse were false, its contradictory 'Some stone is a human' would be true, which with the original implies a contradiction (some stone is not stone). Similarly for the general: example 'No writer is still-fingered as long as he is a writer' converts to 'No still-fingered person is a writer as long as he is still-fingered'. The proof is analogous. For the particular: 'No writer is still-fingered as long as he is a writer, not perpetually' converts to a particular affirmative 'Some still-fingered person is actually a writer' (i.e., a general absolute particular). The proof again uses contradiction. The commentator then generalizes: the particular negative only converts in the two particular types. Universal negatives that lack descriptive permanence do not convert at all (seven types). Those that have descriptive permanence are six: if also essential permanence (the two permanent) they convert universally without non-permanence qualification; if they are qualified by non-permanence (the two general and the two particular), they convert universally to the descriptive permanence with the non-permanence condition in some. The passage ends with a summary by Nur Allah.

العامتان
الخاصتان

The Reductio Proof for Conversion of Particular Customary Negatives
Proof by contradiction of the conversion rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio argument that proves the conversion of a particular customary negative using the general customary proposition as a necessary consequence.

Core Idea
The converse of a particular customary negative is proved by contradiction.

This passage provides a rigorous proof for the conversion of particular customary negatives. The proof uses a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the converse is false leads to an impossible consequence. By walking through the steps, you will understand how the chain of logical consequences ensures the validity of the conversion rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim to be proved
The claim is that the particular customary negative (e.g., 'Some S is not P perpetually') converts to a particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some P is S'). The proof aims to show this conversion is valid.
2
Understand the proof structure
The proof assumes the contradictory of the converse is true. If the converse were false, its contradictory (a universal negative) would be true.
This sets up the reductio.
3
Combine with the original proposition
The contradictory (universal negative) is combined with the original (particular customary negative) using a syllogism, yielding an absurd conclusion: a thing is not itself.
The argument is: 'No still-fingered person is a writer perpetually' and 'Every writer is still-fingered actually' imply 'No writer is a writer perpetually'.
4
Conclude the truth of the converse
Since the assumption leads to an impossibility (contradiction), the assumption must be false. Therefore, the converse is true, proving the conversion rule.
This is a standard reductio ad absurdum.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the explanation in all [cases] is that the contradictory of the converse together with the original...
As for the first part, its explanation has passed: that it is a necessary consequence of the two general (negatives), and they are necessary consequences of the two particular (negatives), and a consequence of a consequence is a consequence.
As for the second part: if the converse were not true, its contradictory would be true, i.e., 'No still-fingered person is a writer perpetually'. And this, together with the original which is 'Every writer is still-fingered actually', yields 'No writer is a writer perpetually'. This is a reductio. The non-permanence in the universal (general) is necessitated because in our example, 'Every still-fingered person is a writer actually' is false, since our statement 'Some still-fingered person is not a writer perpetually' is true, like the earth. The author said: 'The secret in that is that the non-permanence of the negative is affirmative, and it does not convert except as particular.' And in this there is contemplation, for the conversion of the whole to the whole is not dependent on the conversion of the parts to the parts, as is witnessed by considering the conversion of affirmative modal propositions as previously.
As for the first part: the result is that if it is true by necessity or perpetuity that 'No writer is still-fingered as long as he is a writer, not perpetually', then it is true that 'No still-fingered person is a writer as long as he is still-fingered', otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is: 'Some still-fingered person is a writer at the time he is still-fingered'. So if we make it a minor premise for the first part of the original assumed true, it yields 'Some still-fingered person is not still-fingered at the time he is still-fingered'. And it is also possible to explain that the general customary is a necessary consequence of the two general (negatives) since it has been established that they convert to it, and the consequence of the more general is a necessary consequence of the more specific by self-evidence, so the general customary is a necessary consequence of the two particular (negatives) necessarily. (Ismail)
As for the second part: it is the non-permanence in the universal (general). That is, because the analogical reasoning would be that the non-permanence in the converse is an indication of a universal absolute general affirmative, because the non-permanence is an indication of a general absolute that contradicts [the original] in quality and agrees with it in quantity, so the truth of the non-permanence in some in the converse – i.e., it being an indication of a particular affirmative – is theoretical and in need of explanation. So he said: 'It is not necessitated etc.' And upon this it can be said: His statement 'and it is only necessitated the non-permanence in the universal (general)' is a response to an implied question, which is: 'The non-permanence in some contradicts what you mentioned that the non-permanence is an indication of a general absolute. Like the earth: the first in the example: like the birds; for one objects regarding the earth that what is meant by 'still' here is still-fingered, and the earth does not have fingers.' And the answer is: 'The still is the one lacking movement, and the earth, due to not having fingers, it is true that it is not moving-fingered, so understand.' (Burhan)
That the non-permanence of the negative: meaning that the secret in that the non-permanence in the converse is particular not universal is that the non-permanence of the negative – i.e., the original mentioned – is affirmative, for the second part in the compound is opposite to the first in quality, and it is apparent that the conversion of the affirmative, whether universal or particular, is a particular affirmative. (Burhan)
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage provides the detailed proof for the conversion of particular customary negatives. It is divided into two parts. The commentator begins by stating the overall proof structure: the negation of the converse together with the original yields a contradiction. The first part explains that the general customary proposition is a necessary consequence of the two general negatives, and they are consequences of the two particular negatives. Thus, the general customary is a necessary consequence of the particular negatives. The second part addresses the specific case of the particular customary negatives, where the converse is not simply a general customary universal but rather a particular affirmative (i.e., a general absolute with non-permanence in some). The commentator shows that if the converse were false, a contradiction arises. Then he discusses the author's statement that the secret is that the non-permanence of the negative is affirmative, and its conversion is particular. However, the commentator (Ismail) expresses doubt, noting that the conversion of a compound to another compound does not depend on the conversion of its parts individually. Then he offers an alternative explanation: the general customary is a necessary consequence of the two general negatives. The second part then delves into the nature of the non-permanence (ladawam). The author says that the non-permanence of the original negative is affirmative, so its converse is particular affirmative. The commentator (Burhan) clarifies that the non-permanence qualifies the negation, but when negating the predicate, the non-permanence attaches to the predicate, making the converse a particular affirmative. The passage ends with a discussion of an example involving 'earth' and 'birds', indicating that the term 'still-fingered' applies to anything lacking finger movement.

The Secret of Non-Permanence: Why the Converse is a Particular Affirmative
Explanatory note on the conversion result.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the conversion of a particular customary negative yields a particular affirmative rather than a universal, using the concept of non-permanence.

Core Idea
The non-permanence of the negative makes the converse affirmative, and it can only convert as particular.

The passage reveals the secret behind the conversion result: the non-permanence (ladawam) of a negative is actually affirmative, so the converse must be affirmative and, since the original is particular, the converse remains particular. However, a commentator raises a subtle objection about the composition of the proposition. This lesson unpacks that puzzle.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the puzzle
The converse of a particular customary negative is a particular affirmative, not a universal. The puzzle is: why is the non-permanence in the converse particular instead of universal?
The phrase 'non-permanence' appears in the converse as a modal qualifier.
2
Understand the author's answer
The author states the secret: the non-permanence of a negative is actually an affirmation. That is, the compound proposition 'Some S is not P non-permanently' is equivalent to an affirmative. Therefore, its conversion follows the rule for affirmatives, which convert only as particulars.
An affirmative, whether universal or particular, converts to a particular affirmative.
3
Consider the commentator's doubt
The commentator (Ismail) objects that the conversion of a compound proposition does not necessarily follow from the conversion of its parts. The whole may behave differently.
He argues that the conversion of the whole to the whole is not dependent on the conversion of parts to parts.
4
Follow the refined explanation
Burhan clarifies by analyzing the compound: the original negative has two components – the negation and the non-permanence. The non-permanence component has the opposite quality, so the entire proposition becomes affirmative. Consequently, its conversion is a particular affirmative.
This resolves the doubt by showing that the quality of the whole is indeed determined by the interplay of its parts.

Simple Conversion of Universal Negative: The Reductio Proof
Proof of universal negative conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof that a universal negative proposition converts to a universal negative using reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
The universal negative converts to itself; the proof lies in the contradiction from assuming otherwise.

The commentary establishes that 'No A is B' implies 'No B is A'. This lesson walks through the two-part proof that uses the contradictory of the converse together with the original to produce an absurdity. You will learn the logical structure of this immediate inference and see how the first-figure syllogism generates the contradiction.

Guided Path
1
State the original proposition and its converse
The original is a universal negative, e.g., 'No writer has fingers while writing' (from the text). Its converse would be 'No finger-having resident is a writer while a resident.' Note the non-permanence (the 'while' condition) in both.
2
Assume the contradictory of the converse
Suppose the converse is false, so its contradictory, a particular affirmative, is true: 'Some finger-having resident is a writer while a resident.' This is the starting point of the reductio.
3
Combine it with the original to derive an absurdity
Use the original proposition (e.g., 'Every writer is actually a resident' from the text) as a premise. The contradictory of the converse and the original together yield 'Some finger-having resident is not a finger-having resident while a resident' via a first-figure syllogism, an obvious contradiction.
4
Conclude the converse is true
Since the contradictory leads to absurdity, it must be false; therefore the converse (the universal negative) is true. This establishes the conversion rule.

Source Text

(إسماعيل) وأما الجزء الثاني: وهو اللادوام في الكل، يعني لما كان القياس أن يكون اللادوام في العكس إشارة إلى موجبة كلية مطلقة عامة؛ لما أن اللادوام يكون إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة مخالفة لما قيد به في الكيف وموافقة له في مر من الكم، فصدق اللادوام في البعض في العكس أي كونه إشارة إلى موجبة جزئية نظري محتاج إلى البيان فقال: "إنما لم يلزم إلخ" وعلى هذا يمكن أن يقال: إن قوله : "وإنما يلزم اللادوام في الكل" جواب عن سؤال مقدر، وهو قولكم: "اللادوام في "البعض يخالف ما ذكرتم من أن اللادوام إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة. كالأرض: الأولى في المثال : كالطيور؛ إذ يناقش في الأرض بأن المراد من الساكن ههنا ساكن الأصابع، والأرض ليس كذلك؛ لعدم الأصابع لها وأجيب بأن الساكن هو عديم الحركة، والأرض لعدم الأصابع لها يصدق عليها أنها ليست بمتحرك الأصابع فافهم. (برهان)

ينتج المحال، ولا عكس للبواقي بالنقض.
فإن الخاصتين الموجبتين تنعكسان إلى الحينية اللادائمة، مع أن الجزء الثاني منهما -وهو المطلقة العامة السالبة - لا عكس .لها. فتدبر. قوله: ينتج المحال : فهذا المحال إما أن يكون ناشئاً عن الأصل أو عن نقيض العكس أو عن هيئة تأليفهما، لكن الأول مفروض الصدق، والثالث هو الشكل الأول المعلوم صحته وإنتاجه، فتعين الثاني، فيكون النقيض باطلا، فيكون العكس حقا. قوله: ولا عكس للبواقي: أي السوالب الباقية وهي تسعة: الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة والمطلقة العامة والممكنة العامة من البسائط، والوقتيتان والوجوديتان والممكنة الخاصة من المركبات قوله: بالنقض: أي بدليل
فتدبر إشارة إلى الجواب عن جانب المصنف بأن انعكاس المجموع إلى المجموع موقوف على انعكاس الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء. وأما انعكاس الخاصتين الموجبتين إلى الحينية المطلقة اللادائمة فمستثنى عن ذلك؛ إما لأن المطلقة العامة السالبة لا عكس لها كما سيجيء، أو لأن الخاصتين إذا كانتا موجبتين جزئيتين فيكون لا دوامهما حينئذ إشارة إلى سالبة جزئية مطلقة عامة، وقد برهن على عدم انعكاس السالبة الجزئية مطلقا من غير نظر إلى أنها مطلقة عامة أو غيرها. (عبد) فتدبر : لعله إشارة إلى أنه إن كان المراد أن انعكاس المجموع إلى المجموع ليس منوطا بانعكاس الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء في جميع المركبات فمسلم لكن لا يضرنا، وإن كان المراد أنه ليس منوطا به مطلقا فممنوع؛ فإن انعكاس المجموع إلى المجموع منوط بانعكاس الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء لو كانت تلك الأجزاء قابلة للانعكاس، وأما إذا لم تكن قابلة له فإما أن لا يكون المجموع منعكسا أصلا، أو يكون منعكسا بطريق آخر، ولا شك أن الجزء الثاني ههنا قابل للانعكاس؛ لأنه موجبة كلية؛ إذ هو مفهوم لا دوام السالبة الكلية، فلا تنعكس إلا إلى جزئية. فافهم. إسماعيل) أي السوالب الباقية أي الكليات وأما الجزئيات فلا انعكاس فيها أصلا إلا للخاصتين فلا يناقش أن قوله: "للبواقي" لا يكاد يصح؛ إذ الجزئيتان الخاصتان من السوالب تنعكسان. (برهان والاستدلال على عدم انعكاس السالبة الجزئية في غير الخاصتين بما اشتهر عندهم من أن ما عداهما من قضايا أخص بعضها الضرورية وبعضها الوقتية والسالبة الجزئية لا تنعكس منهما ؛ لصدق قولنا بعض الحيوان" ليس بإنسان بالضرورة" كذب قولنا: "بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان بالإمكان العام ؛ ضرورة أن كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة، ولصدق قولنا: "ليس بعض القمر منخسفا بالضرورة وقت التربيع لا دائما" مع كذب قولنا: "ليس" بعض المنخسف بقمر بالإمكان العام؛ ضرورة أن كل منخسف قمر بالضرورة. ومن البين أن عدم انعكاس الأخص يستلزم عدم انعكاس الأعم مطلقا. (أبو الفتح)
Rulings of simple conversion. And the demonstration in all is that the contradictory of the converse together with the original [proposition]... As for the first part, its explanation has passed that it is a necessary concomitant of the two general [propositions], and they are necessary concomitants of the two specific [propositions], and the necessary concomitant of a necessary concomitant is necessary. As for the second part: if the converse were not true, then its contradictory would be true, namely 'No resident is a writer at all times' - and this together with the non-permanence [la dawam] of the original, which is 'Every writer is actually a resident', yields 'No writer is a writer at all times', which is absurd. And the non-permanence [la dawam] only occurs in the universal because in this example 'Every resident is actually a writer' is falsified, due to the truth of our statement 'Some resident is not a writer at all times', like the earth. The author said: The secret in that is that the non-permanence of the negative is affirmative, and it only converts to a particular, and there is deliberation in this, because the conversion of the whole to the whole is not dependent on the conversion of the parts to the parts, as is witnessed by observing the conversion of affirmative modal [propositions] as previously... As for the first part: the result is that if it is true necessarily or perpetually that 'No writer has fingers as long as he is a writer at every time', then it is true that 'No finger-having resident is a writer as long as he is a resident', otherwise its contradictory would be true: 'Some finger-having resident is a writer while he is a finger-having resident', and if we make it the minor premise for the first part of the original assumed true, it yields 'Some finger-having resident is not a finger-having resident while he is a finger-having resident'. And it can also be demonstrated that the general 'urfiyya is a necessary concomitant of the two general [propositions]; because it is established that they convert to it, and the necessary concomitant of the more general is necessarily the necessary concomitant of the more specific. So the general 'urfiyya is necessarily a necessary concomitant of the two specific [propositions]. (Isma'il) As for the second part: it is the non-permanence in the universal, meaning that since the analogy was that the non-permanence in the converse indicates an absolute universal affirmative, because non-permanence indicates an absolute [proposition] differing in quality from what it qualifies and agreeing in quantity, so the truth of non-permanence in some of the converse, i.e. its being an indication of a particular affirmative, is theoretical and needs explanation. So he said: 'Indeed, it does not necessitate etc.' And upon this it can be said that his statement 'And only the non-permanence in the universal occurs' is an answer to an implicit question, namely your statement: 'The non-permanence in some contradicts what you mentioned that non-permanence indicates an absolute universal. Like the earth: the better example is birds; because the earth is disputed on the grounds that the intended meaning of 'resident' here is 'finger-having resident', and the earth is not like that because it does not have fingers, and it was answered that the resident is the one lacking movement, and the earth, lacking fingers, is truly described as not moving its fingers, so understand. (Burhan) That the non-permanence of the negative: meaning that the secret why non-permanence in the converse is particular not universal is that the non-permanence of the negative, i.e. the original mentioned, is affirmative; because the second part in the compound differs from the first in quality, and it is obvious that the converse of an affirmative, whether universal or particular, is a particular affirmative. (Burhan) It produces impossibility, and there is no conversion for the remaining by contraposition. For the two specific affirmative [propositions] convert to the non-permanent 'urfiyya, even though the second part of them, which is the absolute universal negative, has no conversion. So reflect. His statement 'it produces impossibility': so this impossibility is either arising from the original, or from the contradictory of the converse, or from the form of their composition, but the first is assumed true, and the third is the first figure whose validity and production are known, so the second is determined, so the contradictory is false, so the converse is true. His statement 'and no conversion for the remaining': i.e. the remaining negatives, which are nine: the absolute temporal, the absolute pervasive, the absolute universal, the absolute possible from simple [propositions], and the two temporal, the two existential, and the specific possible from composite [propositions]. His statement 'by contraposition': i.e. by proof. 'Fata'dabbar' is an indication of the answer on behalf of the author that the conversion of the whole to the whole is dependent on the conversion of the parts to the parts. As for the conversion of the two specific affirmatives to the absolute non-permanent 'urfiyya, it is exempt from that; either because the absolute universal negative has no conversion as will come, or because the two specific [affirmatives] when they are particular affirmatives, then their non-permanence at that time indicates a particular absolute universal negative, and it has been proven that the particular negative has no conversion absolutely, regardless of whether it is absolute universal or otherwise. ('Abd) 'Fata'dabbar': perhaps it is an indication that if what is meant is that the conversion of the whole to the whole is not dependent on the conversion of the parts to the parts in all compounds, then it is accepted but does not harm us, and if what is meant is that it is not dependent on it absolutely, then it is denied; for the conversion of the whole to the whole is dependent on the conversion of the parts to the parts if those parts are convertible, but if they are not convertible, then either the whole does not convert at all, or it converts by another way, and there is no doubt that the second part here is convertible because it is a universal affirmative; since it is the concept of non-permanence of the universal negative, so it only converts to a particular. So understand. (Isma'il) i.e. the remaining negatives are the universals; as for particulars, there is no conversion in them at all except for the two specific [propositions], so it is not objected that his statement 'for the remaining' is hardly correct; because the two specific negative particulars do convert. (Burhan) And the proof for the non-conversion of the particular negative other than the two specific [propositions] is based on what is well-known among them that what other than them are more specific propositions: some are necessary and some are temporal, and the particular negative does not convert from them; because the truth of our statement 'Some animal is not a human necessarily' makes 'Some human is not an animal by general possibility' false, since necessarily every human is an animal; and because of the truth of our statement 'Some eclipse is not a lunar eclipse necessarily at the time of quadrature not always' while 'Some lunar eclipse is not an eclipse by general possibility' is false, since necessarily every lunar eclipse is an eclipse. And it is evident that the non-conversion of the more specific implies the non-conversion of the more general absolutely. (Abu al-Fath)
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rulings of simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) of categorical propositions, specifically focusing on universal negatives and the concept of non-permanence (la dawam). The commentator expands on the proof that a universal negative proposition (e.g., 'No A is B') converts to a universal negative ('No B is A'), using a reductio argument. The proof involves two parts: first, establishing that the contradictory of the converse together with the original yields an absurdity; second, handling the non-permanence qualification. The commentator explains that the non-permanence in the converse of a universal negative must be understood as indicating a particular affirmative proposition, not a universal, because the non-permanence (la dawam) of a negative is affirmative in quality and thus converts to a particular. This is illustrated with examples like 'resident' and 'writer'. The commentary then addresses an objection about the dependence of the conversion of the whole on the conversion of its parts, with reference to the two specific affirmatives that convert to the non-permanent unconditional (al-'urfiyya al-'amma). Finally, it lists the nine negative propositions (e.g., absolute temporal, absolute pervasive) that do not have a simple converse, and provides proofs using examples from natural phenomena (e.g., animal/human, eclipse) and the principle that non-conversion of the specific implies non-conversion of the general.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the reductio proof combine to produce an absurdity?
A) The original and the contradictory of the converse
B) The converse and its contradictory
C) The original and the converse
D) The contradictory of the original and the converse

Key Takeaway
A universal negative proposition converts to a universal negative by reductio: assuming the converse false leads to a contradiction with the original.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the kalam example: 'No created thing is eternal'. Construct the reductio proof step by step to show that this converts to 'No eternal thing is created'.
?
Identify the two parts of the proof in the commentary and explain how the non-permanence (la dawam) affects the second part, especially in the example of 'writer' and 'resident'.

Immediate Inference
advanced
10 min
Why Non-Permanence (La Dawam) in the Converse is Particular Affirmative
Explanation of non-permanence in conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the non-permanence (la dawam) in the converse of a universal negative indicates a particular affirmative rather than a universal affirmative, and identify the underlying logical reason.

Core Idea
The non-permanence of a negative is affirmative, so it converts to a particular.

When dealing with universal negatives qualified by non-permanence, the la dawam in the converse might seem to indicate a universal affirmative. However, the commentator reveals the secret: the non-permanence qualifier is itself an affirmative proposition, and affirmatives only convert to particulars. This lesson unpacks that reasoning using the text's own examples and clarifies this subtle point of modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the non-permanence (la dawam) in the original proposition
In the passage, the original is a universal negative with a temporal condition: 'No writer has fingers as long as he is a writer at every time' – the non-permanence is the 'at every time' which is actually a perpetual condition? Actually, the la dawam is the negation of perpetuity: the proposition is true 'as long as he is a writer' but not always. The la dawam qualifier is the conditional scope.
2
Convert the proposition and examine the resulting non-permanence
The converse is 'No finger-having resident is a writer as long as he is a resident'. The la dawam in the converse is the same conditional scope. The commentator argues that this la dawam does not indicate a universal affirmative but a particular affirmative.
3
Analyze the quality of the la dawam in the original
The commentator states: 'the secret that the non-permanence in the converse is particular not universal is that the non-permanence of the negative is affirmative.' This means the la dawam qualifier (the second part of the compound proposition) is considered affirmative in quality, even though it qualifies a negative.
4
Apply the conversion rule for affirmative propositions
Affirmative propositions, whether universal or particular, convert only to particular affirmatives. Since the la dawam part is affirmative, its converse must be a particular affirmative. Therefore, the la dawam in the overall converse represents a particular affirmative (e.g., 'some resident is a writer at some time').
5
Conclude with an Islamic example
Consider: 'No fasting person is eating at any time during the fast' (universal negative with la dawam). The la dawam 'any time during the fast' is a conditional scope. The converse becomes 'No eating person is fasting at any time during the fast', and the la dawam here indicates a particular affirmative: 'some eating person is not fasting at some time'? Actually, this is tricky but illustrates the principle.

أحكام العكس المستوي
والبيان في الكل أن نقيض العكس مع الأصل
أما الجزء الأول فقد مر بيانه من أنه لازم للعامتين وهما لازمتان للخاصتين، ولازم اللازم لازم. وأما الجزء الثاني: فإنه لو لم يصدق العكس لصدق نقيضه وهو لا شيء من الساكن بكاتب دائما -
وهذا مع لا دوام -الأصل وهو أن كل كاتب ساكن بالفعل - ينتج لا شيء من الكاتب بكاتب دائما، هذا خلف، وإنما يلزم اللادوام في الكل؛ لأنه يكذب في مثالنا هذا كل ساكن كاتب بالفعل؛ لصدق قولنا بعض الساكن ليس بكاتب دائما كالأرض. قال المصنف : السر في ذلك أن لا دوام السالبة موجبة، وهي لا تنعكس إلا جزئية، وفيه تأمل؛ إذ ليس انعكاس المجموع إلى المجموع منوطا بانعكاس الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء، كما يشهد بذلك ملاحظة انعكاس الموجهات الموجبة على ما مر؛
أما
الجزء الأول: الحاصل أنه إذا صدق بالضرورة أو بالدوام لا شيء من الكاتب بساكن الأصابع ما دام كاتبا لادائما، صدق لا شيء من ساكن الأصابع بكاتب ما دام ساكنا، وإلا فيصدق نقيضه وهو: بعض ساكن الأصابع كاتب حين هو ساكن الأصابع، فإذا جعلناه صغرى للجزء الأول من الأصل المفروض الصدق ينتج "بعض ساكن الأصابع ليس بساكن الأصابع حين هو ساكن الأصابع"، ويمكن البيان أيضاً بأن العرفية العامة لازمة للعامتين؛ لما ثبت أنهما تنعكسان إليها، ولازم الأعم لازم الأخص بالبداهة، فالعرفية العامة لازمة للخاصتين بالضرورة. (إسماعيل) وأما الجزء الثاني: وهو اللادوام في الكل، يعني لما كان القياس أن يكون اللادوام في العكس إشارة إلى موجبة كلية مطلقة عامة؛ لما أن اللادوام يكون إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة مخالفة لما قيد به في الكيف وموافقة له في مر من الكم، فصدق اللادوام في البعض في العكس أي كونه إشارة إلى موجبة جزئية نظري محتاج إلى البيان فقال: "إنما لم يلزم إلخ" وعلى هذا يمكن أن يقال: إن قوله : "وإنما يلزم اللادوام في الكل" جواب عن سؤال مقدر، وهو قولكم: "اللادوام في "البعض يخالف ما ذكرتم من أن اللادوام إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة. كالأرض: الأولى في المثال : كالطيور؛ إذ يناقش في الأرض بأن المراد من الساكن ههنا ساكن الأصابع، والأرض ليس كذلك؛ لعدم الأصابع لها وأجيب بأن الساكن هو عديم الحركة، والأرض لعدم الأصابع لها يصدق عليها أنها ليست بمتحرك الأصابع فافهم. (برهان) أن لا دوام السالبة: يعني أن السر في أن اللادوام في العكس جزئية لا كلية أن اللادوام السالبة أي الأصل المذكور موجبة؛ إذ الجزء الثاني في المركبة مخالفة للأول في الكيف، ومن الظاهر أن عكس الموجبة سواء كانت كلية أو جزئية موجبة جزئية. (برهان)

ينتج المحال، ولا عكس للبواقي بالنقض.
فإن الخاصتين الموجبتين تنعكسان إلى الحينية اللادائمة، مع أن الجزء الثاني منهما -وهو المطلقة العامة السالبة - لا عكس .لها. فتدبر. قوله: ينتج المحال : فهذا المحال إما أن يكون ناشئاً عن الأصل أو عن نقيض العكس أو عن هيئة تأليفهما، لكن الأول مفروض الصدق، والثالث هو الشكل الأول المعلوم صحته وإنتاجه، فتعين الثاني، فيكون النقيض باطلا، فيكون العكس حقا. قوله: ولا عكس للبواقي: أي السوالب الباقية وهي تسعة: الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة والمطلقة العامة والممكنة العامة من البسائط، والوقتيتان والوجوديتان والممكنة الخاصة من المركبات قوله: بالنقض: أي بدليل
فتدبر إشارة إلى الجواب عن جانب المصنف بأن انعكاس المجموع إلى المجموع موقوف على انعكاس الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء. وأما انعكاس الخاصتين الموجبتين إلى الحينية المطلقة اللادائمة فمستثنى عن ذلك؛ إما لأن المطلقة العامة السالبة لا عكس لها كما سيجيء، أو لأن الخاصتين إذا كانتا موجبتين جزئيتين فيكون لا دوامهما حينئذ إشارة إلى سالبة جزئية مطلقة عامة، وقد برهن على عدم انعكاس السالبة الجزئية مطلقا من غير نظر إلى أنها مطلقة عامة أو غيرها. (عبد) فتدبر : لعله إشارة إلى أنه إن كان المراد أن انعكاس المجموع إلى المجموع ليس منوطا بانعكاس الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء في جميع المركبات فمسلم لكن لا يضرنا، وإن كان المراد أنه ليس منوطا به مطلقا فممنوع؛ فإن انعكاس المجموع إلى المجموع منوط بانعكاس الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء لو كانت تلك الأجزاء قابلة للانعكاس، وأما إذا لم تكن قابلة له فإما أن لا يكون المجموع منعكسا أصلا، أو يكون منعكسا بطريق آخر، ولا شك أن الجزء الثاني ههنا قابل للانعكاس؛ لأنه موجبة كلية؛ إذ هو مفهوم لا دوام السالبة الكلية، فلا تنعكس إلا إلى جزئية. فافهم. إسماعيل) أي السوالب الباقية أي الكليات وأما الجزئيات فلا انعكاس فيها أصلا إلا للخاصتين فلا يناقش أن قوله: "للبواقي" لا يكاد يصح؛ إذ الجزئيتان الخاصتان من السوالب تنعكسان. (برهان والاستدلال على عدم انعكاس السالبة الجزئية في غير الخاصتين بما اشتهر عندهم من أن ما عداهما من قضايا أخص بعضها الضرورية وبعضها الوقتية والسالبة الجزئية لا تنعكس منهما ؛ لصدق قولنا بعض الحيوان" ليس بإنسان بالضرورة" كذب قولنا: "بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان بالإمكان العام ؛ ضرورة أن كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة، ولصدق قولنا: "ليس بعض القمر منخسفا بالضرورة وقت التربيع لا دائما" مع كذب قولنا: "ليس" بعض المنخسف بقمر بالإمكان العام؛ ضرورة أن كل منخسف قمر بالضرورة. ومن البين أن عدم انعكاس الأخص يستلزم عدم انعكاس الأعم مطلقا. (أبو الفتح)
Rulings of simple conversion. And the demonstration in all is that the contradictory of the converse together with the original [proposition]... As for the first part, its explanation has passed that it is a necessary concomitant of the two general [propositions], and they are necessary concomitants of the two specific [propositions], and the necessary concomitant of a necessary concomitant is necessary. As for the second part: if the converse were not true, then its contradictory would be true, namely 'No resident is a writer at all times' - and this together with the non-permanence [la dawam] of the original, which is 'Every writer is actually a resident', yields 'No writer is a writer at all times', which is absurd. And the non-permanence [la dawam] only occurs in the universal because in this example 'Every resident is actually a writer' is falsified, due to the truth of our statement 'Some resident is not a writer at all times', like the earth. The author said: The secret in that is that the non-permanence of the negative is affirmative, and it only converts to a particular, and there is deliberation in this, because the conversion of the whole to the whole is not dependent on the conversion of the parts to the parts, as is witnessed by observing the conversion of affirmative modal [propositions] as previously... As for the first part: the result is that if it is true necessarily or perpetually that 'No writer has fingers as long as he is a writer at every time', then it is true that 'No finger-having resident is a writer as long as he is a resident', otherwise its contradictory would be true: 'Some finger-having resident is a writer while he is a finger-having resident', and if we make it the minor premise for the first part of the original assumed true, it yields 'Some finger-having resident is not a finger-having resident while he is a finger-having resident'. And it can also be demonstrated that the general 'urfiyya is a necessary concomitant of the two general [propositions]; because it is established that they convert to it, and the necessary concomitant of the more general is necessarily the necessary concomitant of the more specific. So the general 'urfiyya is necessarily a necessary concomitant of the two specific [propositions]. (Isma'il) As for the second part: it is the non-permanence in the universal, meaning that since the analogy was that the non-permanence in the converse indicates an absolute universal affirmative, because non-permanence indicates an absolute [proposition] differing in quality from what it qualifies and agreeing in quantity, so the truth of non-permanence in some of the converse, i.e. its being an indication of a particular affirmative, is theoretical and needs explanation. So he said: 'Indeed, it does not necessitate etc.' And upon this it can be said that his statement 'And only the non-permanence in the universal occurs' is an answer to an implicit question, namely your statement: 'The non-permanence in some contradicts what you mentioned that non-permanence indicates an absolute universal. Like the earth: the better example is birds; because the earth is disputed on the grounds that the intended meaning of 'resident' here is 'finger-having resident', and the earth is not like that because it does not have fingers, and it was answered that the resident is the one lacking movement, and the earth, lacking fingers, is truly described as not moving its fingers, so understand. (Burhan) That the non-permanence of the negative: meaning that the secret why non-permanence in the converse is particular not universal is that the non-permanence of the negative, i.e. the original mentioned, is affirmative; because the second part in the compound differs from the first in quality, and it is obvious that the converse of an affirmative, whether universal or particular, is a particular affirmative. (Burhan) It produces impossibility, and there is no conversion for the remaining by contraposition. For the two specific affirmative [propositions] convert to the non-permanent 'urfiyya, even though the second part of them, which is the absolute universal negative, has no conversion. So reflect. His statement 'it produces impossibility': so this impossibility is either arising from the original, or from the contradictory of the converse, or from the form of their composition, but the first is assumed true, and the third is the first figure whose validity and production are known, so the second is determined, so the contradictory is false, so the converse is true. His statement 'and no conversion for the remaining': i.e. the remaining negatives, which are nine: the absolute temporal, the absolute pervasive, the absolute universal, the absolute possible from simple [propositions], and the two temporal, the two existential, and the specific possible from composite [propositions]. His statement 'by contraposition': i.e. by proof. 'Fata'dabbar' is an indication of the answer on behalf of the author that the conversion of the whole to the whole is dependent on the conversion of the parts to the parts. As for the conversion of the two specific affirmatives to the absolute non-permanent 'urfiyya, it is exempt from that; either because the absolute universal negative has no conversion as will come, or because the two specific [affirmatives] when they are particular affirmatives, then their non-permanence at that time indicates a particular absolute universal negative, and it has been proven that the particular negative has no conversion absolutely, regardless of whether it is absolute universal or otherwise. ('Abd) 'Fata'dabbar': perhaps it is an indication that if what is meant is that the conversion of the whole to the whole is not dependent on the conversion of the parts to the parts in all compounds, then it is accepted but does not harm us, and if what is meant is that it is not dependent on it absolutely, then it is denied; for the conversion of the whole to the whole is dependent on the conversion of the parts to the parts if those parts are convertible, but if they are not convertible, then either the whole does not convert at all, or it converts by another way, and there is no doubt that the second part here is convertible because it is a universal affirmative; since it is the concept of non-permanence of the universal negative, so it only converts to a particular. So understand. (Isma'il) i.e. the remaining negatives are the universals; as for particulars, there is no conversion in them at all except for the two specific [propositions], so it is not objected that his statement 'for the remaining' is hardly correct; because the two specific negative particulars do convert. (Burhan) And the proof for the non-conversion of the particular negative other than the two specific [propositions] is based on what is well-known among them that what other than them are more specific propositions: some are necessary and some are temporal, and the particular negative does not convert from them; because the truth of our statement 'Some animal is not a human necessarily' makes 'Some human is not an animal by general possibility' false, since necessarily every human is an animal; and because of the truth of our statement 'Some eclipse is not a lunar eclipse necessarily at the time of quadrature not always' while 'Some lunar eclipse is not an eclipse by general possibility' is false, since necessarily every lunar eclipse is an eclipse. And it is evident that the non-conversion of the more specific implies the non-conversion of the more general absolutely. (Abu al-Fath)
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rulings of simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) of categorical propositions, specifically focusing on universal negatives and the concept of non-permanence (la dawam). The commentator expands on the proof that a universal negative proposition (e.g., 'No A is B') converts to a universal negative ('No B is A'), using a reductio argument. The proof involves two parts: first, establishing that the contradictory of the converse together with the original yields an absurdity; second, handling the non-permanence qualification. The commentator explains that the non-permanence in the converse of a universal negative must be understood as indicating a particular affirmative proposition, not a universal, because the non-permanence (la dawam) of a negative is affirmative in quality and thus converts to a particular. This is illustrated with examples like 'resident' and 'writer'. The commentary then addresses an objection about the dependence of the conversion of the whole on the conversion of its parts, with reference to the two specific affirmatives that convert to the non-permanent unconditional (al-'urfiyya al-'amma). Finally, it lists the nine negative propositions (e.g., absolute temporal, absolute pervasive) that do not have a simple converse, and provides proofs using examples from natural phenomena (e.g., animal/human, eclipse) and the principle that non-conversion of the specific implies non-conversion of the general.

Key Takeaway
The non-permanence (la dawam) of a negative proposition is affirmative in quality, so it converts to a particular affirmative, not a universal.
