Conversion of the Two Specific Propositions and Non-Convertible Propositions
Discussion of specific propositions and non-convertible negatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which propositions have a valid simple conversion and which do not, specifically focusing on the two specific propositions (particular affirmative and negative) and the nine absolute propositions that lack a converse.

Core Idea
Only universal negatives and particular affirmatives convert straightforwardly; many negative propositions, especially absolute ones, do not.

The commentary discusses the conversion of the two specific propositions (al-khassatayn: particular affirmative and particular negative). While the particular affirmative converts to a non-permanent conditional, the particular negative generally does not convert. The passage also lists the nine types of negative propositions (such as absolute temporal, pervasive, possible) that have no simple converse. This lesson clarifies these exceptions using examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two specific propositions (al-khassatayn)
These are the particular affirmative ('Some A is B') and particular negative ('Some A is not B'). They are called 'specific' because they are less general than universal propositions.
2
Analyze the conversion of the particular affirmative
The passage states that the particular affirmative converts to a non-permanent conditional (al-'urfiyya al-'amma). For example, 'Some animal is human' converts to 'Some human is animal at some time' (always true, but with a temporal qualifier).
3
Examine the status of the particular negative
The particular negative generally does not convert. The text provides examples: 'Some animal is not a human necessarily' (true) does not convert to 'Some human is not an animal' (false). However, there is a nuance: the two specific negative particulars (i.e., particular negatives) do convert according to one commentator (Burhan), but this is disputed. For our purposes, we focus on the standard view that particular negatives do not convert.
4
List the nine non-convertible negatives
The text enumerates: absolute temporal, absolute pervasive, absolute universal, absolute possible from simple propositions, and two temporal, two existential, and specific possible from composite propositions. These do not have a simple converse.
5
Understand the proof using examples from nature
The passage gives examples: 'Some animal is not a human necessarily' (a particular negative necessary) does not convert; similarly, 'Some eclipse is not a lunar eclipse at the time of quadrature' (a particular negative temporal) does not convert. The non-conversion of the specific implies the non-conversion of the general.

(إسماعيل) وأما الجزء الثاني: وهو اللادوام في الكل، يعني لما كان القياس أن يكون اللادوام في العكس إشارة إلى موجبة كلية مطلقة عامة؛ لما أن اللادوام يكون إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة مخالفة لما قيد به في الكيف وموافقة له في مر من الكم، فصدق اللادوام في البعض في العكس أي كونه إشارة إلى موجبة جزئية نظري محتاج إلى البيان فقال: "إنما لم يلزم إلخ" وعلى هذا يمكن أن يقال: إن قوله : "وإنما يلزم اللادوام في الكل" جواب عن سؤال مقدر، وهو قولكم: "اللادوام في "البعض يخالف ما ذكرتم من أن اللادوام إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة. كالأرض: الأولى في المثال : كالطيور؛ إذ يناقش في الأرض بأن المراد من الساكن ههنا ساكن الأصابع، والأرض ليس كذلك؛ لعدم الأصابع لها وأجيب بأن الساكن هو عديم الحركة، والأرض لعدم الأصابع لها يصدق عليها أنها ليست بمتحرك الأصابع فافهم. (برهان) أن لا دوام السالبة: يعني أن السر في أن اللادوام في العكس جزئية لا كلية أن اللادوام السالبة أي الأصل المذكور موجبة؛ إذ الجزء الثاني في المركبة مخالفة للأول في الكيف، ومن الظاهر أن عكس الموجبة سواء كانت كلية أو جزئية موجبة جزئية. (برهان)

ينتج المحال، ولا عكس للبواقي بالنقض.
فإن الخاصتين الموجبتين تنعكسان إلى الحينية اللادائمة، مع أن الجزء الثاني منهما -وهو المطلقة العامة السالبة - لا عكس .لها. فتدبر. قوله: ينتج المحال : فهذا المحال إما أن يكون ناشئاً عن الأصل أو عن نقيض العكس أو عن هيئة تأليفهما، لكن الأول مفروض الصدق، والثالث هو الشكل الأول المعلوم صحته وإنتاجه، فتعين الثاني، فيكون النقيض باطلا، فيكون العكس حقا. قوله: ولا عكس للبواقي: أي السوالب الباقية وهي تسعة: الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة والمطلقة العامة والممكنة العامة من البسائط، والوقتيتان والوجوديتان والممكنة الخاصة من المركبات قوله: بالنقض: أي بدليل
فتدبر إشارة إلى الجواب عن جانب المصنف بأن انعكاس المجموع إلى المجموع موقوف على انعكاس الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء. وأما انعكاس الخاصتين الموجبتين إلى الحينية المطلقة اللادائمة فمستثنى عن ذلك؛ إما لأن المطلقة العامة السالبة لا عكس لها كما سيجيء، أو لأن الخاصتين إذا كانتا موجبتين جزئيتين فيكون لا دوامهما حينئذ إشارة إلى سالبة جزئية مطلقة عامة، وقد برهن على عدم انعكاس السالبة الجزئية مطلقا من غير نظر إلى أنها مطلقة عامة أو غيرها. (عبد) فتدبر : لعله إشارة إلى أنه إن كان المراد أن انعكاس المجموع إلى المجموع ليس منوطا بانعكاس الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء في جميع المركبات فمسلم لكن لا يضرنا، وإن كان المراد أنه ليس منوطا به مطلقا فممنوع؛ فإن انعكاس المجموع إلى المجموع منوط بانعكاس الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء لو كانت تلك الأجزاء قابلة للانعكاس، وأما إذا لم تكن قابلة له فإما أن لا يكون المجموع منعكسا أصلا، أو يكون منعكسا بطريق آخر، ولا شك أن الجزء الثاني ههنا قابل للانعكاس؛ لأنه موجبة كلية؛ إذ هو مفهوم لا دوام السالبة الكلية، فلا تنعكس إلا إلى جزئية. فافهم. إسماعيل) أي السوالب الباقية أي الكليات وأما الجزئيات فلا انعكاس فيها أصلا إلا للخاصتين فلا يناقش أن قوله: "للبواقي" لا يكاد يصح؛ إذ الجزئيتان الخاصتان من السوالب تنعكسان. (أبو الفتح)
Rulings of simple conversion. And the demonstration in all is that the contradictory of the converse together with the original [proposition]... As for the first part, its explanation has passed that it is a necessary concomitant of the two general [propositions], and they are necessary concomitants of the two specific [propositions], and the necessary concomitant of a necessary concomitant is necessary. As for the second part: if the converse were not true, then its contradictory would be true, namely 'No resident is a writer at all times' - and this together with the non-permanence [la dawam] of the original, which is 'Every writer is actually a resident', yields 'No writer is a writer at all times', which is absurd. And the non-permanence [la dawam] only occurs in the universal because in this example 'Every resident is actually a writer' is falsified, due to the truth of our statement 'Some resident is not a writer at all times', like the earth. The author said: The secret in that is that the non-permanence of the negative is affirmative, and it only converts to a particular, and there is deliberation in this, because the conversion of the whole to the whole is not dependent on the conversion of the parts to the parts, as is witnessed by observing the conversion of affirmative modal [propositions] as previously... As for the first part: the result is that if it is true necessarily or perpetually that 'No writer has fingers as long as he is a writer at every time', then it is true that 'No finger-having resident is a writer as long as he is a resident', otherwise its contradictory would be true: 'Some finger-having resident is a writer while he is a finger-having resident', and if we make it the minor premise for the first part of the original assumed true, it yields 'Some finger-having resident is not a finger-having resident while he is a finger-having resident'. And it can also be demonstrated that the general 'urfiyya is a necessary concomitant of the two general [propositions]; because it is established that they convert to it, and the necessary concomitant of the more general is necessarily the necessary concomitant of the more specific. So the general 'urfiyya is necessarily a necessary concomitant of the two specific [propositions]. (Isma'il) As for the second part: it is the non-permanence in the universal, meaning that since the analogy was that the non-permanence in the converse indicates an absolute universal affirmative, because non-permanence indicates an absolute [proposition] differing in quality from what it qualifies and agreeing in quantity, so the truth of non-permanence in some of the converse, i.e. its being an indication of a particular affirmative, is theoretical and needs explanation. So he said: 'Indeed, it does not necessitate etc.' And upon this it can be said that his statement 'And only the non-permanence in the universal occurs' is an answer to an implicit question, namely your statement: 'The non-permanence in some contradicts what you mentioned that non-permanence indicates an absolute universal. Like the earth: the better example is birds; because the earth is disputed on the grounds that the intended meaning of 'resident' here is 'finger-having resident', and the earth is not like that because it does not have fingers, and it was answered that the resident is the one lacking movement, and the earth, lacking fingers, is truly described as not moving its fingers, so understand. (Burhan) That the non-permanence of the negative: meaning that the secret why non-permanence in the converse is particular not universal is that the non-permanence of the negative, i.e. the original mentioned, is affirmative; because the second part in the compound differs from the first in quality, and it is obvious that the converse of an affirmative, whether universal or particular, is a particular affirmative. (Burhan) It produces impossibility, and there is no conversion for the remaining by contraposition. For the two specific affirmative [propositions] convert to the non-permanent 'urfiyya, even though the second part of them, which is the absolute universal negative, has no conversion. So reflect. His statement 'it produces impossibility': so this impossibility is either arising from the original, or from the contradictory of the converse, or from the form of their composition, but the first is assumed true, and the third is the first figure whose validity and production are known, so the second is determined, so the contradictory is false, so the converse is true. His statement 'and no conversion for the remaining': i.e. the remaining negatives, which are nine: the absolute temporal, the absolute pervasive, the absolute universal, the absolute possible from simple [propositions], and the two temporal, the two existential, and the specific possible from composite [propositions]. His statement 'by contraposition': i.e. by proof. 'Fata'dabbar' is an indication of the answer on behalf of the author that the conversion of the whole to the whole is dependent on the conversion of the parts to the parts. As for the conversion of the two specific affirmatives to the absolute non-permanent 'urfiyya, it is exempt from that; either because the absolute universal negative has no conversion as will come, or because the two specific [affirmatives] when they are particular affirmatives, then their non-permanence at that time indicates a particular absolute universal negative, and it has been proven that the particular negative has no conversion absolutely, regardless of whether it is absolute universal or otherwise. ('Abd) 'Fata'dabbar': perhaps it is an indication that if what is meant is that the conversion of the whole to the whole is not dependent on the conversion of the parts to the parts in all compounds, then it is accepted but does not harm us, and if what is meant is that it is not dependent on it absolutely, then it is denied; for the conversion of the whole to the whole is dependent on the conversion of the parts to the parts if those parts are convertible, but if they are not convertible, then either the whole does not convert at all, or it converts by another way, and there is no doubt that the second part here is convertible because it is a universal affirmative; since it is the concept of non-permanence of the universal negative, so it only converts to a particular. So understand. (Isma'il) i.e. the remaining negatives are the universals; as for particulars, there is no conversion in them at all except for the two specific [propositions], so it is not objected that his statement 'for the remaining' is hardly correct; because the two specific negative particulars do convert. (Burhan) And the proof for the non-conversion of the particular negative other than the two specific [propositions] is based on what is well-known among them that what other than them are more specific propositions: some are necessary and some are temporal, and the particular negative does not convert from them; because the truth of our statement 'Some animal is not a human necessarily' makes 'Some human is not an animal by general possibility' false, since necessarily every human is an animal; and because of the truth of our statement 'Some eclipse is not a lunar eclipse necessarily at the time of quadrature not always' while 'Some lunar eclipse is not an eclipse by general possibility' is false, since necessarily every lunar eclipse is an eclipse. And it is evident that the non-conversion of the more specific implies the non-conversion of the more general absolutely. (Abu al-Fath)

This passage is a commentary on the rulings of simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawi) of categorical propositions, specifically focusing on universal negatives and the concept of non-permanence (la dawam). Proof that Simple Conversion Yields Impossibility
Author proves that simple conversion yields impossibility and identifies the source of contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof that simple conversion yields impossibility by identifying the source of contradiction.

Core Idea
Impossibility in conversion proofs is not from the premise but from the contrary of the conclusion.

In this lesson, we examine how logicians prove the validity of simple conversion. The passage presents a classic proof by contradiction: assuming the contrary of the conversion leads to an impossibility, which cannot come from the original premise (assumed true) nor from the syllogistic form (which is valid), so it must arise from the contrary of the conversion itself. This establishes the conversion as true.

Guided Path
1
State the original proposition
Start with a true proposition whose simple conversion you want to prove. For example, a universal affirmative 'Every A is B'.
The original is assumed true.
2
Assume the contrary of the conversion
Assume the opposite of the proposed conversion. For 'Every A is B', the simple conversion is 'Some B is A'; its contrary is 'No B is A'.
This contrary is what we aim to disprove.
3
Form a first-figure syllogism
Combine the original premise with the assumed contrary to create a syllogism in the first figure. For example, 'Every A is B' (major) and 'No B is A' (minor) yield 'No A is A' – a contradiction.
The first figure is known to be valid and productive.
4
Identify the source of impossibility
The resulting contradiction cannot come from the original (it is true) nor from the syllogistic form (it is valid), so it must come from the assumed contrary. Therefore, the contrary is false.
This forces the acceptance of the conversion.
5
Conclude the conversion's truth
Since the contrary is false, the conversion itself must be true.
This proof works for all propositions that have a valid simple conversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحكام العكس المستوي ينتج المحال، ولا عكس للبواقي بالنقض. فإن الخاصتين الموجبتين تنعكسان إلى الحينية اللادائمة، مع أن الجزء الثاني منهما -وهو المطلقة العامة السالبة - لا عكس لها. فتدبر. قوله: ينتج المحال : فهذا المحال إما أن يكون ناشئاً عن الأصل أو عن نقيض العكس أو عن هيئة تأليفهما، لكن الأول مفروض الصدق، والثالث هو الشكل الأول المعلوم صحته وإنتاجه، فتعين الثاني، فيكون النقيض باطلا، فيكون العكس حقا.
Rulings of simple conversion: It yields impossibility, and there is no conversion for the remaining [propositions] by refutation. For the two particular affirmative propositions convert to indefinite [propositions] without perpetuity, even though the second part of them—which is the absolute universal negative—has no conversion. So reflect. His saying: 'it yields impossibility': this impossibility either arises from the original, or from the contrary of the conversion, or from the form of their combination; but the first is assumed true, and the third is the first figure whose validity and productivity is known, so the second is determined, so the contrary is false, so the conversion is true.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling of simple conversion. The author states that simple conversion yields impossibility (المحال) and that the remaining nine negative propositions do not convert. The commentary then analyzes the source of the impossibility when proving conversion: it cannot come from the original (since it is assumed true), nor from the syllogistic form (since the first figure is valid), so it must come from the contrary of the conversion, proving the conversion correct. The 'two particular affirmative propositions' refer to the specific affirmative propositions that do convert, but their conversion is to indefinite (مطلقة) without perpetuity, while the second part (the absolute universal negative) does not convert. This shows the care needed in conversion.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي
المحال
الخاصتين الموجبتين

Key Takeaway
The contradiction in proving simple conversion is not a flaw but a sign that the conversion is true, because the impossibility originates from assuming its contrary.

The Nine Negative Propositions That Do Not Convert
Author lists nine non-converting negatives and notes the exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate and categorize the nine negative propositions that do not undergo simple conversion.

Core Idea
Nine negatives fail conversion; only two particular affirmatives are excepted.

This lesson identifies which negative propositions cannot be simply converted. The author enumerates nine: four from simple propositions and six from compound propositions. Knowing these helps avoid invalid conversions in logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
The nine are split into simple (basa'it) and compound (murakkabat). Note the simple group has four, compound has six.
2
List the simple negatives
The four simples: temporal absolute, pervasive absolute, universal absolute, and general possible. Understand each term's meaning.
These are from the modal propositions.
3
List the compound negatives
The six compounds: two temporals, two existential, and the particular possible. Recognize these are compound propositions.
Compound here means they have a composite structure.
4
Recognize the exception
All these are negative and do not convert. However, the two particular affirmative propositions do convert exceptionally to indefinite absolute without perpetuity.
This exception is discussed further in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: ولا عكس للبواقي: أي السوالب الباقية وهي تسعة: الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة والمطلقة العامة والممكنة العامة من البسائط، والوقتيتان والوجوديتان والممكنة الخاصة من المركبات. قوله: بالنقض: أي بدليل. فتدبر إشارة إلى الجواب عن جانب المصنف بأن انعكاس المجموع إلى المجموع موقوف على انعكاس الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء. وأما انعكاس الخاصتين الموجبتين إلى الحينية المطلقة اللادائمة فمستثنى عن ذلك؛ إما لأن المطلقة العامة السالبة لا عكس لها كما سيجيء، أو لأن الخاصتين إذا كانتا موجبتين جزئيتين فيكون لا دوامهما حينئذ إشارة إلى سالبة جزئية مطلقة عامة، وقد برهن على عدم انعكاس السالبة الجزئية مطلقا من غير نظر إلى أنها مطلقة عامة أو غيرها. (عبد) فتدبر : لعله إشارة إلى أنه إن كان المراد أن انعكاس المجموع إلى المجموع ليس منوطا بانعكاس الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء في جميع المركبات فمسلم لكن لا يضرنا، وإن كان المراد أنه ليس منوطا به مطلقا فممنوع؛ فإن انعكاس المجموع إلى المجموع منوط بانعكاس الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء لو كانت تلك الأجزاء قابلة للانعكاس، وأما إذا لم تكن قابلة له فإما أن لا يكون المجموع منعكسا أصلا، أو يكون منعكسا بطريق آخر، ولا شك أن الجزء الثاني ههنا قابل للانعكاس؛ لأنه موجبة كلية؛ إذ هو مفهوم لا دوام السالبة الكلية، فلا تنعكس إلا إلى جزئية. فافهم. إسماعيل) أي السوالب الباقية أي الكليات وأما الجزئيات فلا انعكاس فيها أصلا إلا للخاصتين فلا يناقش أن قوله: "للبواقي" لا يكاد يصح؛ إذ الجزئيتان الخاصتان من السوالب تنعكسان. (برهان والاستدلال على عدم انعكاس السالبة الجزئية في غير الخاصتين بما اشتهر عندهم من أن ما عداهما من قضايا أخص بعضها الضرورية وبعضها الوقتية والسالبة الجزئية لا تنعكس منهما؛ لصدق قولنا بعض الحيوان" ليس بإنسان بالضرورة" كذب قولنا: "بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان بالإمكان العام ؛ ضرورة أن كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة، ولصدق قولنا: "ليس بعض القمر منخسفا بالضرورة وقت التربيع لا دائما" مع كذب قولنا: "ليس" بعض المنخسف بقمر بالإمكان العام؛ ضرورة أن كل منخسف قمر بالضرورة. ومن البين أن عدم انعكاس الأخص يستلزم عدم انعكاس الأعم مطلقا. (أبو الفتح)
His saying: 'And no conversion for the remaining': i.e., the remaining negative propositions, and they are nine: the temporal absolute, the pervasive absolute, the universal absolute, the general possible from simple [propositions], and the two temporals, the two existential, and the particular possible from compound [propositions]. His saying: 'by refutation': i.e., by proof. 'So reflect' is an indication of the answer regarding the author’s side: that the conversion of the whole to the whole depends on the conversion of the parts to the parts. And the conversion of the two particular affirmative [propositions] to the indefinite absolute without perpetuity is an exception to that; either because the universal absolute negative has no conversion as will come, or because the two particular [propositions] if they are particular affirmative, then their non-perpetuity at that time is an indication of a particular absolute negative general, and it has been proven that there is no conversion of the particular negative absolutely without regard to whether it is universal absolute or otherwise. (Abd) So reflect: perhaps it is an indication that if what is meant is that the conversion of the whole to the whole is not dependent on the conversion of the parts to the parts in all compounds, then it is accepted but does not harm us; and if what is meant is that it is not dependent at all, then it is rejected; for the conversion of the whole to the whole is dependent on the conversion of the parts to the parts if those parts are capable of conversion, and if they are not capable, then either the whole does not convert at all, or it converts in another way, and there is no doubt that the second part here is capable of conversion because it is a universal affirmative; for it is the concept of the non-perpetuity of the universal negative, so it converts only to a particular. So understand. (Ismail) That is, the remaining negatives are the universals, and as for the particulars, there is no conversion in them at all except for the two particular [affirmatives], so it should not be objected that his saying 'for the remaining' is hardly correct; for the two particular negatives convert. (Burhan) And the proof of the non-conversion of the particular negative in other than the two particular [affirmatives] is what is well known among them: that what is other than them is more specific, some of them are necessary and some temporal, and the particular negative does not convert from them because of the truth of our saying 'some animal is not human necessarily' and the falsehood of our saying 'some human is not animal by general possibility' because every human is animal necessarily, and because of the truth of our saying 'some moon is not eclipsed necessarily at the time of quadrature not always' with the falsehood of our saying 'some eclipse is not moon by general possibility' because every eclipse is moon necessarily. And it is clear that the non-conversion of the more specific entails the non-conversion of the more general absolutely. (Abu al-Fath)
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage details the exceptions to simple conversion. The author lists nine negative propositions that do not convert: three from simple propositions (temporal absolute, pervasive absolute, universal absolute, general possible) and six from compound propositions (the two temporals, two existential, particular possible). Then the commentary explains that the conversion of the whole depends on the parts, but the two particular affirmatives are an exception because their conversion is to indefinite without perpetuity. The marginal glosses further clarify: (Abd) discusses the dependency, (Ismail) notes that the remaining are universal negatives (particular negatives do convert), and (Burhan) provides an example proving that particular negatives do not convert except the two specific ones, using examples from necessary and temporal truths. The examples involve animal/human and moon/eclipse, showing that if the original is true, the conversion is false, proving non-conversion.

Key Terms
السوالب الباقية
الوقتية المطلقة
المنتشرة المطلقة
المطلقة العامة
الممكنة العامة

Why Particular Negatives Do Not Convert: Proofs and Examples
Author provides proof for non-conversion of particular negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof that particular negative propositions do not convert, using logical examples.

Core Idea
Particular negatives fail conversion because a true original yields a false converse.

This lesson examines the proof given in the commentary for why particular negatives (except the two special ones) do not convert. Using examples like 'Some animal is not human necessarily' and 'Some moon is not eclipsed necessarily,' the logicians demonstrate that conversion fails.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proof strategy
To show a particular negative does not convert, find a counterexample: a true original and false converse.
This is a standard method in logic.
2
First example: animal and human
Consider the true proposition 'Some animal is not human necessarily' (true because there are non-human animals). Its converse would be 'Some human is not animal' which is false because every human is animal necessarily. So conversion fails.
The conversion would be a particular negative, but it is false.
3
Second example: moon and eclipse
Consider 'Some moon is not eclipsed necessarily at quadrature' (true). Its converse 'Some eclipse is not moon' is false because every eclipse is moon necessarily.
The temporal modality ensures truth of original.
4
Generalize the conclusion
Since these specific cases (more specific) do not convert, the more general case (particular negative without modality) also does not convert. This establishes the rule.
The reasoning uses the principle that non-conversion of the specific entails non-conversion of the general.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Definition and Proof of Conversion by Contraposition
Author defines conversion by contraposition and proves why the contrapositive must be particular and possible general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform conversion by contraposition on a categorical proposition and justify why the contrapositive must be particular and possible general.

Core Idea
The contrapositive must be particular possible because it is more general; if false, no more specific form can be true.

In the previous lesson, you learned about simple conversion. Now we tackle conversion by contraposition (aks al-naqid), which swaps the negated subject and predicate. But not every proposition yields a valid contrapositive. The author shows that for a temporal negative, the contrapositive fails unless we choose the particular possible form. This choice is not arbitrary—it is the only way to guarantee a valid test of necessity. Let's walk through the logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Conversion by contraposition is defined as swapping the two negated extremes (subject and predicate). This means: take the original proposition, negate both subject and predicate, and swap them. For example, from 'No S is P' we get 'No non-P is non-S'? Actually, the passage uses a different operation: from 'No S is P' (where S is 'moon', P is 'eclipsed at quadrature not always') the contrapositive is 'Some non-P is not non-S' but in the passage it's 'Some eclipse is not a moon by general possibility'. Note that the operation here is: from a universal negative (subject S, predicate P), the contrapositive is a particular negative with subject and predicate swapped and negated? Let's parse the passage carefully.

In the example: Original: 'لا شيء من القمر بمنخسف وقت التربيع لا دائما' (No moon is eclipsed at quadrature not always). The contrapositive they test is: 'بعض المنخسف ليس بقمر بالإمكان العام' (Some eclipse is not a moon by general possibility). So the operation: take original subject 'moon' (negated? Actually original predicate is complex). Better to think: original proposition: 'No moon is an eclipse-not-always'? That is confusing. However, the author focuses on the logical operation: swap the negated extremes. The key is that the contrapositive must be particular and possible general to be a valid test.
The exact logical form is less important than the principle: the contrapositive must be the most general form that can still detect failure.
2
Identify the original and its contrapositive
The original proposition is the temporal negative: 'No moon is eclipsed at quadrature not always' (meaning: whenever the moon is at quadrature, it is not eclipsed; but the 'not always' indicates temporal modality). The contrapositive they consider is: 'Some eclipse is not a moon by general possibility' (i.e., it is possible that some eclipse is not a moon). The original is true (the moon is not eclipsed at quadrature), but the contrapositive is false because every eclipse is a moon necessarily.
This shows that the original does not imply its contrapositive universally; i.e., conversion by contraposition is not valid for all propositions.
3
Understand the reductio argument
If the contrapositive were universal (e.g., 'No eclipse is a moon' or 'Every non-eclipse is a non-moon'), an objector could claim that even if the universal is false, the particular might still be true, so the non-conversion is not proved. But by choosing the particular and possible as the contrapositive—the most general form—if this general form is false, then all more specific forms (universal, actual, etc.) are also false. This is because falsity of the general entails falsity of the specific.
The author uses the principle: if the more general is false, the more specific cannot be true. This is the key logical insight.
4
Apply the tool to a new example
Take a universal negative proposition: 'No forbidden action is permissible'. Its contrapositive (if we follow the same pattern) would be 'Some permissible action is not forbidden by general possibility'? Actually, careful: the operation in the passage is not standard contraposition (which would be 'Every permissible action is not forbidden'? Let's not get bogged. The important thing is to test the necessity of the original by checking if the contrapositive (in the most general form) holds in all instances. For practice, you can try to construct a similar argument using a fiqh proposition.

التصديقات تعريف عكس النقيض فصل عكس النقيض: تبديل نقيضي الطرفين التخلف في مادة، بمعنى أنه يصدق الأصل في مادة بدون العكس، فيعلم بذلك أن العكس غير لازم لهذا الأصل. وبيان التخلف في تلك القضايا أن أخصها وهي الوقتية قد تصدق بدون العكس؛ فإنه يصدق 'لا شيء من القمر بمنخسف وقت التربيع لا دائما' مع كذب 'بعض المنخسف ليس بقمر بالإمكان العام'؛ لصدق نقيضه - وهو كل منخسف قمر بالضرورة- وإذا تحقق التخلف وعدم الانعكاس في الأخص تحقق في الأعم؛ إذ العكس لازم للقضية، فلو انعكس الأعم كان العكس لازما للأعم، والأعم لازم للأخص، ولازم اللازم لازم، فيكون العكس لازما للأخص أيضاً. وقد بينا عدم انعكاسه هذا خلف. وإنما اخترنا في العكس الجزئية؛ لأنها أعم من الكلية والممكنة العامة؛ لأنها أعم من سائر الموجهات وإذا لم يصدق الأعم لم يصدق الأخص بالطريق الأولى بخلاف العكس الكلي. قوله: تبديل نقيضي الطرفين: بخلاف قوله: وإنما اخترنا إلخ جواب عن سؤال وهو أن العكس للسالبة الكلية السالبة الكلية، فعكس الوقتية المذكورة لو أمكن كانت السالبة الكلية الفعلية، فبم فرض الشارح الجزئية دون الكلية؟ و لم فرض الممكنة دون الفعلية؟(برهان) لأنها وعدم صدق الأعم يستلزم عدم صدق الأخص؛ فإن سلب الحيوان عن الشيء يستلزم سلب الإنسان عنه، عدم صدق الأخص؛ فإنه لا يستلزم عدم صدق الأعم. ألا ترى أن الإنسان مسلوب عن الفرس مع صدق الحيوان، فلو اخترنا الكلية في العكس لكان للسائل مجال أن يقول: سلمنا عدم صدق السالبة الكلية في العكس، لكن لا يلزم منه عدم صدق السالبة الجزئية؛ فإن الكلية أخص من الجزئية وعدم صدق الأخص لا يستلزم عدم صدق الأعم؛ فإن كل حيوان إنسان" كاذب وبعض الحيوان إنسان صادق، فيجوز أن لا يصدق السالبة الكلية في عكس الوقتية، ويصدق السالبة الجزئية فيه فلا يتم التقريب؛ لأن المطلوب عدم انعكاس الوقتية مطلقا، وقس عليه قوله : 'والممكنة العامة' أي وإنما اخترنا في العكس الممكنة العامة لئلا يبقى مجال للسؤال.
Propositions: Definition of conversion by contraposition. Chapter: Conversion by contraposition is swapping the two negated extremes. [It is used to demonstrate] failure in a material, meaning that the original is true in a material without the conversion, so it is known that the conversion is not necessary for this original. The demonstration of failure in those propositions is that the most specific of them, namely the temporal, may be true without the conversion; for it is true: 'No moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature not always' while false: 'Some eclipse is not a moon by general possibility'; because its contrary is true: 'Every eclipse is a moon necessarily'. And when failure and non-conversion is verified in the more specific, it is verified in the more general; because the conversion is necessary for the proposition, so if the more general converted, the conversion would be necessary for the more general, and the more general is necessary for the more specific, and a necessary of a necessary is necessary, so the conversion would be necessary for the more specific as well. And we have proven its non-conversion by this reduction to impossibility. We only chose the particular in the conversion because it is more general than the universal, and the general possible because it is more general than other modalities. And if the more general is not true, the more specific is not true by primary way, unlike the universal conversion. His saying: 'swapping the two negated extremes' [excludes something]. And his saying: 'We only chose...' is an answer to the question: that the conversion of the universal negative is [supposed to be] a universal negative, so the conversion of the mentioned temporal, if possible, would be an actual universal negative. So why did the commentator assume the particular instead of the universal? And why did he assume the possible instead of the actual? (Burhan) Because the non-truth of the more general entails the non-truth of the more specific; for the negation of 'animal' about something entails the negation of 'human' about it. But the non-truth of the more specific does not entail the non-truth of the more general. Do you not see that 'human' is negated about a horse while 'animal' is true? So if we chose the universal in the conversion, the objector could say: we concede the non-truth of the universal negative in the conversion, but that does not entail the non-truth of the particular negative; for the universal is more specific than the particular, and non-truth of the more specific does not entail non-truth of the more general; for 'Every animal is human' is false, but 'Some animal is human' is true. So it is possible that the universal negative is not true in the conversion of the temporal, while the particular negative is true in it, so the argument would not be complete; because the goal is the non-conversion of the temporal absolutely. And similarly his saying: 'and the general possible'—i.e., we only chose the general possible in the conversion so that no room for the question remains.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage defines conversion by contraposition (عكس النقيض) as swapping the negated subject and predicate. The author explains that this conversion is used to show that a proposition is not universally necessary by finding a material (specific instance) where the original is true but the contrapositive is false. The example given: a temporal negative proposition 'No moon is eclipsed at quadrature not always' is true, but its contrapositive 'Some eclipse is not a moon by general possibility' is false because every eclipse is a moon necessarily. This proves that the temporal proposition does not convert universally. The author then explains why the contrapositive must be particular and possible general: because if we assumed universal, an objector could claim that the universal falsehood does not rule out the particular being true, so the proof would fail. Using the more general form (particular and possible) ensures that if the contrapositive is false, no more specific form can be true, thus proving the non-conversion conclusively. The marginal comment by (Burhan) further clarifies with an example using animal/human.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض
الجزئية
الممكنة العامة

A) Because it is more specific and gives stronger proof
B) Because it is more general and if false, the more specific is also false

D) Because the temporal proposition requires it

Key Takeaway
The aks al-naqid must be particular and possible general because it is the most general form; if it is false, no more specific form can be true, ensuring the proof of non-conversion is airtight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a universal negative proposition from Islamic law (e.g., 'No invalid contract is binding') and write its aks al-naqid in the particular possible form. Then provide a concrete instance (like the moon example) where the original is true but the contrapositive is false, demonstrating non-conversion.
?
Using the principle that falsity of the more general entails falsity of the more specific, explain why the author chose the particular possible over the universal actual. Provide an example from everyday life (like the animal/human example in the passage) to illustrate.

Immediate Inference
intermediate
6 min
Defining 'Aks al-Naqid: Two Methods
Definition of 'aks al-naqid with two variations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'aks al-naqid and distinguish between the early and later scholars' definitions.

Core Idea
'Aks al-naqid swaps contrapositives of terms, either preserving or changing quality.

In this lesson, we unpack the definition of contrapositive conversion ('aks al-naqid), a key immediate inference in Islamic logic. The commentator presents two versions: the early scholars' method preserves both truth and quality, while the later scholars alter the quality. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying the operation correctly in theological and juristic proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find what is being defined: 'aks al-naqid (contrapositive conversion).
2
Extract the two definitions
Locate the two formulations in the Arabic: one with 'preserving truth and quality', another with 'changing quality'.
The first definition is: تبديل نقيضي الطرفين مع بقاء الصدق والكيف. The second: جعل نقيض الثاني أولاً مع مخالفة الكيف.
3
Analyze the operation
The definition says: make the contrapositive of the first part the second part, and the contrapositive of the second part the first part. This is exchanging terms via their negations.
Contrapositive of a term is its negation (e.g., not-B).
4
Test with an example
Apply to 'Every J is B' to get 'Everything that is not B is not J'. Verify that truth is preserved (if the original is true, the converse is true).
This is the early scholars' method.
5
Compare the two methods
Note the key difference: the later scholars allow the quality to change (e.g., from affirmative to negative or vice versa) in the converse.
The passage does not give an example for the second method, but the operation differs.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل
عكس النقيض: تبديل نقيضي الطرفين مع بقاء الصدق والكيف، أو جعل نقيض الثاني أولاً مع مخالفة الكيف.
قوله: تبديل نقيضي الطرفين:
أي جعل نقيض الجزء الأول من الأصل جزءًا ثانياً من العكس، ونقيض الثاني جزءاً أولاً. قوله: مع بقاء الصدق: أي إن كان الأصل صادقا كان العكس صادقا قوله: والكيف: أي إن كان الأصل موجبا كان العكس موجبا وإن كان سالبا كان سالبا مثلا قولنا: "كل ج ب" ينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا: "كل ليس ب ليس ج"، وهذا طريق القدماء.
وإنما اخترنا في العكس الجزئية؛ لأنها أعم من الكلية والممكنة العامة؛ لأنها أعم من سائر الموجهات وإذا لم يصدق الأعم لم يصدق الأخص بالطريق الأولى بخلاف العكس الكلي قوله: تبديل نقيضي الطرفين:
بخلاف
وإنما اخترنا إلخ جواب عن سؤال وهو أن العكس للسالبة الكلية السالبة الكلية، فعكس الوقتية المذكورة لو أمكن كانت السالبة الكلية الفعلية، فبم فرض الشارح الجزئية دون الكلية؟ و لم فرض الممكنة دون الفعلية؟(برهان) لأنها وعدم صدق الأعم يستلزم عدم صدق الأخص؛ فإن سلب الحيوان عن الشيء يستلزم سلب الإنسان عنه، عدم صدق الأخص؛ فإنه لا يستلزم عدم صدق الأعم. ألا ترى أن الإنسان مسلوب عن الفرس مع صدق الحيوان، فلو اخترنا الكلية في العكس لكان للسائل مجال أن يقول: سلمنا عدم صدق السالبة الكلية في العكس، لكن لا يلزم منه عدم صدق السالبة الجزئية؛ فإن الكلية أخص من الجزئية وعدم صدق الأخص لا يستلزم عدم صدق الأعم؛ فإن كل حيوان إنسان" كاذب وبعض الحيوان إنسان صادق، فيجوز أن لا يصدق السالبة الكلية في عكس الوقتية، ويصدق السالبة الجزئية فيه فلا يتم التقريب؛ لأن المطلوب عدم انعكاس الوقتية مطلقا،
وقس عليه قوله : "والممكنة العامة أي وإنما اخترنا في العكس الممكنة العامة لثلا يبقى محال السؤال."
برهان الدين لذكره ويمكن أن يقال: إن عكس النقيض لازم للقضية، وصدق الملزوم يستلزم صدق اللازم فلذا قال : ومع بقاء الصدق؛ للعلة المذكورة، وتركه ثانيا لوجود تلك العلة ههنا أيضاً.
Chapter
Contrapositive (aks al-naqid): exchanging the two contrapositives of the two terms while preserving truth and quality, or making the contrapositive of the second term the first (part) while changing the quality.
His saying: exchanging the two contrapositives of the two terms:
It means making the contrapositive of the first part of the original a second part of the converse, and the contrapositive of the second part a first part. His saying: while preserving truth: i.e., if the original is true, the converse is true. His saying: and quality: i.e., if the original is affirmative, the converse is affirmative; if it is negative, it is negative. For example, our saying: "Every J is B" is converted by contrapositive to our saying: "Everything that is not B is not J". And this is the way of the early scholars.
And we only chose the partial (juz'iyya) in the converse because it is more general than the universal, and the general possible (mumkina 'amma) because it is more general than the other modal propositions. And if the more general is not true, the more specific is not true by primary implication, unlike the universal converse. His saying: exchanging the two contrapositives of the two terms:
Contrary to
And 'we only chose' etc. is an answer to a question, namely that the converse of the universal negative is the universal negative. So the converse of the mentioned temporal (waqtiyya), if possible, would be the actual universal negative. So why did the commentator assume the partial and not the universal? And why did he assume the possible and not the actual? (Proof) Because the lack of truth of the more general entails the lack of truth of the more specific; for negating 'animal' from something entails negating 'human' from it. The lack of truth of the more specific does not entail the lack of truth of the more general. Do you not see that 'human' is negated from the horse while 'animal' is true? So if we had chosen the universal in the converse, the questioner could have said: we grant the lack of truth of the universal negative in the converse, but it does not necessitate the lack of truth of the partial negative. For the universal is more specific than the partial, and the lack of truth of the more specific does not necessitate the lack of truth of the more general. For 'every animal is human' is false, and 'some animal is human' is true. So it is possible that the universal negative is not true in the converse of the temporal, while the partial negative is true in it, so the reasoning (taqrib) is not complete, because what is required is the non-conversion of the temporal absolutely.
And analogously for his saying: 'and the general possible' i.e., and we only chose the general possible in the converse so that there remains no place for the question.
Burhan al-Din for his mention, and it can be said: that contrapositive (aks al-naqid) is a necessary consequent of the proposition, and the truth of the antecedent necessitates the truth of the consequent. Therefore he said: 'while preserving truth' due to the mentioned cause, and he omitted it in the second definition because that cause also exists here.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage defines 'aks al-naqid (conversion by contrapositive) and explains why the partial negative and the general possible are chosen in the converse of temporal propositions. The author begins by giving two definitions: one according to the early scholars (qudama') and one according to the later scholars (muta'akhkhirun). The first definition states that contrapositive conversion involves exchanging the two contrapositives of the subject and predicate while preserving both truth and quality. The author then expands on this: the contrapositive of the first part (the subject) becomes the second part (the predicate) of the converse, and the contrapositive of the second part becomes the first part. Preserving truth means if the original is true, the converse is true. Preserving quality means the converse retains the affirmative or negative form of the original. An example is given: 'Every J is B' converts to 'Everything that is not B is not J'. 

The passage then addresses a potential objection: why did the commentator (the author of the sharh) assume a partial negative and a general possible in the converse of the temporal proposition (e.g., 'No moon is eclipsed at the time of quadrature')? The questioner argues that the converse of a universal negative should be a universal negative, not a partial. The author provides a proof (burhan) to justify this choice. The reasoning is that the partial (juz'iyya) is more general than the universal (kulliyya), and the general possible (mumkina 'amma) is more general than other modal propositions. If the more general (i.e., the partial) were false, then the more specific (the universal) would also be false. But the converse needs to be a necessary consequence of the original; if we choose the universal, it might be false while the partial could still be true, breaking the necessary conversion. The author uses an example from logic: negating 'animal' from something entails negating 'human', but the reverse is not true. Similarly, if the universal negative were chosen as the converse, it could be that the universal negative is false while the partial negative is true, so the conversion would not be absolute. Therefore, the partial is chosen to ensure that if the original is true, the converse is always true, avoiding counterexamples. The same reasoning applies to choosing the general possible over the actual. The passage concludes by noting that the definition of contrapositive includes preserving truth because it is a necessary consequent of the original.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض
الجزئية
الممكنة العامة

Checkpoint
According to the early scholars' definition, which of the following is a valid contrapositive of 'Every J is B'?
A) Every B is J
B) Every not-B is not-J
C) Some not-B is not J
D) Some B is J

Key Takeaway
The contrapositive of a proposition is formed by exchanging the negations of its subject and predicate, either preserving or changing the quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
